*“I desire to remind you that the colonies,
scattered as they are in different parts of the
world, and occupied by a small population, have
before them a task which I think will better pro-
mote the greatness of the empire by the develop-
ment of their resources than by a course which
will tend to cripple their finances for the benefit
of the navy.”—Luminous remark by “ No Mr
Goschen ” Reid, now “Yes, Mr. Chamberlain”
Reid, reported in S.M. Herald , July 5 1897

THE RED PAGE.
NAVAL TRIBUTE.
TH HE abstract of the Colonial Con-
| ference held in London last year has
three impressive lines :
Naval Contributions.— Canada declined to
contribute. Australia undertook to increase her
contribution to £200,000 per annum.
The abstract, of course, is not pre-
cisely accurate. It was not Canada,
but her Prime Minister, that declined.
It was not Australia, but her Prime
Minister, that undertook. And as
Australia’s Prime Minister, before he
went to London, was expressly pledged
to commit this country to nothing at all,
he did commit this country to nothing
at all. He merely committed himself to
advocate Chamberlain’s policy in Aus-
tralia—thus justifying the apprehensions
voiced before be set out.
Presently, it appears, Barton will rise
in the Commonwealth Parlianjent to ad-
vocate the increased naval subsidy. But,
if “ loyal ” Canada can refuse to pay
naval tribute, why should Australia pay ?
jt
One way to ascertain if naval tribute
will profit us in the future, is to study if
it has profited us in the past.
The so-called “ Australasian squadron ”
came here in 1891, as a result of the
dexterous management of Australian
delegates to the Colonial Conference of
1887. The squadron consists of seven
vessels, of which four only are kept per-
manently in commission. The others are
held “in reserve”— i.e., more or less
unready for active service. Not one of the
vessels ranks as first-class.. Not one of
them ranks as second-class. Five of them
are third-class cruisers; two afe torpedo
gun-boats. Exactly what kind of fight
the whole seven, now more or less obsolete
in type, would make against one first-class
Japanese man-of-war, the British Admi-
ralty has never explained. Precisely
how long six of them could sustain their
advertised speed of 19 knots, is a conun-
drum referred to the commanders.
For these vessels Australia and Maori-
land have paid in twelve years, at
£126,000 per year, £1,512,000. Of the
annual £126,000, £91,000 is for mainten-
ance and £35,000 for interest, charged
at 5 per cent, on the £700,000 which the
squadron cost to construct.
“ The vessels have been built by the
British Government; and the Austra-
lasian Governments have no voice what-
ever in their management, nor any control
over their movements. On the termina-
tion of the agreement the vessels will
remain the property of the Imperial
Government.”— The Seven Colonies of
Australasia , 1901-2, p. 741.
je
This is a clear case of taxation without
efficient representation—a clear breach of
the constitutional principle affirmed by
Barton, Reid, and every constitutional
lawyer as essential to the maintenance of
logical and just democracy. Australia’s
right of self-government under the Crown
has been admitted by Britain—separately
for the States, once again for the Com-
monwealth. It is a right most precious,
gained originally with difficulty, and
never to be .relinquished by a self-respect-
ing people. The smallest infraction of
its authority is of the very gravest
moment.
The naval tribute agreement is no
small infraction. In every other depart-
ment of our administration Parliament
controls the expenditure of the money
which it votes. In the case of the naval
subsidy it exercises no control. The
officer in command of the Australasian
squadron is responsible, not to the Aus-
tralasian taxpayers who pay him, but to
the Imperial authorities who do not pay
him.
As constitutional States, Australia and
Maoriland work on constitutional methods.
For every penny of the people’s money
expended, the Parliaments are entitled
to demand an exact account. But, for
the money we pay as subsidy to a British
fleet, we get no account whatever. We
cannot ask how it goes, because the
admiral has nothing to do with us ; he
takes his orders from England. Would
we permit the commander of an Aus-
tralian land force to refuse obedience to
everyone but the British War Office?
On what grounds, then, should the com-
mander of an Australian sea force be
permitted to refuse obedience to every-
one but the British Admiralty 1
Under the old agreement, there was a
British pledge that the fleet should remain
in Australian waters—a safe pledge,
since, without the possibility of Austra-
lian interference, those who made it
could break it when they chose. By the
new agreement even this shred of Aus-
tralian authority is taken away. The
sphere of operations is to include Chinese
and Indian waters. Granting that an
effective blow for Australia may be struck
at Corea or Ceylon: is not the point of
attack the point of danger ? And is it not
common sense and common prudence to
preserve the means of defence at the
point of danger? Hostile fleets have
slipped past guarding fleets in the British
Channel. At the new speed of cruisers,
how easy to slip past a guarding fleet on
the other side of the Indian Ocean. And
then—Sydney ? And then—Melbourne ?
In any case, the judgment should be
an Australian judgment, not a British
judgment. The sea-dog that Britain
feeds bites where he is told. The sea-dog
that Australia feeds should bite where he
is told. If subsidy is granted on the new
terms, it will be a subsidy to the British
navy. The problem of Australian defence
will still confront us.
For £1,512,000 paid away in twelve
years, we can show no permanent benefit
whatever. The vessels remain, but they
are British vessels, not Australian. Yet
it is Australian money that has kept them
in a state of efficiency.
In the ten years currency of the new
agreement, it is estimated that we
shall pay away £2,400,000 (Australia,
£2,000,000; Maoriland, £400,000); and
at the end of the ten years we shall be
able to show no permanent benefit what-
ever. Our shores (or Indian shores, or
Chinese shores) will have been defended
—we trust; but for the payment of
£2,400,000 we shall have not one man-of-
war, not one cruiser, to call our own.
This is what Barton, supported by Reid,
backed by Seddon, tells us is the most
intelligent and the least expensive plan
of naval defence that we can adopt !
Yet the credit of the Commonwealth is
moderately good ; the credit of Maoriland
is fair. The Commonwealth could borrow
money probably at 3 per cent., or a trifle
over. An annual payment of £200,000,
capitalised at 3 per cent., is £6,666,666.
The seven vessels of the existing Defence
Squadron cost £700,000. One second-
class cruiser, better than any of
the vessels of the Defence Squadron,
costs £300,000. If we are to hire a fleet
in perpetuity, would it not be better to
put down the money at once—and own
the fleet, and control the fleet, and man
the fleet as a nursery of Australian
seamen 1
There is no need to put down six
millions. Suppose Australia were satis-
fied with the measure of naval defence
we have had in the last twelve years, and
started with a fleet to cost £700,000 —
but fewer vessels, and better. The cost
of maintaining that fleet is fixed at
£91,000 per annum. Interest at 3 per
cent, would be £21,000. A sinking-fund
payment of less than £68,000 would re-
pay the prime cost in ten years—the
term of the new agreement. So that
we should pay in all £lBO,OOO a year—
£2o,ooo less than is proposed—and at the
end of ten years we would own a small
fleet free from debt. Total of compara-
tive payments for ten years:—£l,Boo,ooo,
defence on the scale of the last twelve
years, and we own the ships; £2,000,000,
defence on a better scale, but we own
nothing.
This is urged to show the financial
absurdity of the hiring agreement. In
practice, since we have to find men as
well as ships, it would be better to start
with one or two cruisers, and add or
replace by degrees. At £200,000 a year,
we shall pay in two years for one second-
class cruiser and her maintenance, and
have nothing to show. Where is the
sense of it ?
There is no sense in naval tribute.
As a matter of fact, the subsidy is not
designed to secure the defence of Aus-
tralia. That is a secondary object. The
prime object is to secure a contribution
to the expense of the British navy, to
relieve the British taxpayer, and to
cement the bonds of empire. But at
whose cost 1 At the cost of Australia,
undoubtedly. As George Reid told
Goschen at the Hotel Cecil, London, on
July 2,1897, there is involved “a latent de-
sign on the Australian purse.” * Australia
is to bleed, in purse and life, that the
British skin and income may be saved
intact. A use has been found for “ the
colonies.” But Britain is still to rule,
though Australia is asked to pay: Aus-
tralia is to maintain the navy, but Britain
to control it—naturally.
The history of British relations with
Australia divides itself into three main
epochs. In each of those epochs British
policy has been guided by British self-
interest. In the first, Australia was seen
as a rubbish-heap at the other end of the
world, where the scum of Britain could
be conveniently thrown and forgotten.
Britain fought hard against the abolition
of transportation, and when she could
fight no longer, Australia ceased to be of
interest except as a barbarous place
where younger sons could be sent with a
chance of making money. British states-
men questioned seriously whether it was
worth while maintaining the bond of
union.
Then came Seeley’s notion that, in
view of the growth of Russia and
America, Britain must expand into an
empire, or risk falling to the rank of a
second-rate power ; and Dailey’s Soudan
contingent set the British pondering.
“ The colonies ” might pay Britain, after
all. Selfishness—neglect—and now the
wheel has turned full circle. The
African war has shaken the dust from
the sleepiest British eyes. What ! “ the
colonies can shed blood and cash to
save the Briton. What ! Australians
are fools who will follow a red herring of
sentiment so far that, though nominally
self-governed, they will cheerfully give
men and money to be spent in British
wars as Britain chooses. That is the
pleasant dream which Britain is straining
to make real.
Much water has passed under the
mill since George Reid defied Goschen in
i'V < ; and there have been many visitors
at the Hotel Cecil. Two „£ Lm w
know—Barton, Australian Premier; For-
rest, Australian Minister of Defence
They rode in British State carriages':
they smoked British State cigars • they
drank British State champagne; gold-
embroidered tapestries hung prominently
outside their British State apartments
British duchesses gave them strawberries
at garden-parties ; and—they saw Mr
Chamberlain. The British taxpayer
smiled, noting these things, confident in
Mr. Chamberlain.
Now Barton and Forrest are proposing
to pay £200,000 annually to the British
navy for ships which Australia does not
own and does not control. The British
taxpayer’s confidence in Mr. Chamberlain
has been justified—it is Australia that is
to foot the bill at the Hotel Cecil. The
persistent backstairs intrigue of the last
twelve years is about to be rewarded
through Barton and Forrest. At one
time there were doubts of Barton. It
was thought that a man who had been
called “ Australia’s noblest son” might
incline to put Australian interests before
British Interests. Plain Bill Lyne
seemed a politician more... amenable. It
is due to Barton to say that he has
banished all doubts. One could scarcely
find a better Imperialist— or a worse
Australian.
After the navy, the army—most as-
suredly. The Australian Army Corps is
already planned at the War Office.
Major-General Hutton spoke too soon
since one thing at a time is a good
maxim ; but the “ force for employment
abroad ” is nevertheless provided for in
Forrest’s draft bill. To what end is
Colonel Clarke—late Special Commis-
sioner of the War Office; author of The
N(ivy and the Nation and Imperial
Defence —governor of Victoria 1 To what
end is Major-General Chermside governor
of Queensland ? To what end is Vice-
Admiral Rawson governor of N.S. Wales 1
To what end is Vice-Admiral Bedford
governor of Westralia 1 To the end that
the governors may be recruiting-officers ;
and that “ Government House ” may be a
centre of influence for securing support
for the British army and navy. Where
the vultures are, there is the carcase.
Australia has tpAdepend upon the
lonest service Commonwealth Par-
. lament. I)g£pite the sinister alliance of
Barton with Reid, the subsidy should not
be granted. In any case, a hired fleet
should be kept for service where it is
hired. And at worst the subsidy should
specifically terminate at the end of ten
years unless fresh legislation renews it.
There is no hostility to Britain in the
creation of an independent Australian
navy, vessel by vessel, as time and money
serve. It is true that our greatest risk
of war comes from the British connec-
tion ; but were there no British connec-
tion we should still have to prepare for
war. That preparation cannot commence
too soon. We cannot'too soon have ships
of our own, and men trained to fight
them. Since there is bound to be an
Australian navy, the nucleus should be
formed at once. The talk of impossible
expenditure is nonsense. It has been
shown that the money that it is proposed
to throw away in hire can be devoted to
ownership, with a defensive result equal
to the existing power of defence. Let
us encourage our seamen.
Edmund Barton says truly that we
stand at a parting of the ways of na-
tional tendency. It is a question of vas-
salage or independence. Only the vassal
pays tribute without exercising rule.
Let us then have independence in amity
with#Britain, not distrust in subjection.
Australia cannot always be sacrificed.
Every jot of liberty that is yielded now
will have to be bought back by-and-by,
and it may be at the price of blood
A. G. S.

the next man. If you feel that you are run down ; that you are weak ;if you have tried the OLD
AND EVER-FAILING DRUG TREATMENT, give it up. Your body is like an engine without
steam, your nerve force is exhausted, you have no energy, you have no steam.
“Electricity is life.” It is the potent factor in the human body. Electricity is the
only rational and certain cure for functional disorders. If your BACK ACHES, if you are
NERVOUS, if you have RHEUMATISM, LUMBAGO, INDIGESTION, STOMACH or
KIDNEY TROUBLES, Electricity, properly applied, will cure you. There is no doubt about
it. It cannot fail. It quickens the pulse, invigorates the blood, puts new life into every nerve.
We have spent over Twenty years in the study and application of Electricity, and the results
have produced the marvellous invention with which we are curing thousands yearly.
Dr. McLaughlin’s Electric Belt
It is simple, but grand. There is nothing so penetrating, nothing so invigorating, nothing that
will relieve weakness and pain as speedily, so surely. It assists Nature by a general reinforcement of
the vital energy by infusing a mild, invigorating current of Electricity into the nerves, and by
supplying the system with the very essence of Nerve Vigor and Nerve Strength.

Various Verses.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Nostalgia.
Cold England ! with a white bias*
Shredding the country lane ;
Bowed twigs and wailing alders,
Grey sky and sleet and rain.
And oh ! my thoughts are Southward
On a shining tropic shore
That warm blue ocean kisses.
And I want the sun once more—
The trade wind s scent is in my brain
And I want the sun once more.
Cold London ! as a dead thing
Changeless and hard and stern :
A thousand lonely faces
Flit past and never turn
On me, whose hopeless longing
For a glance and smile is vain ;
And I curse the old conventions
As I dream of the South again,
I seem to olasp a dear friend’s hand
As I dream of the South again.
Cold women ! pale,’disdainful,
With wintry eyes of blue ;
Those pure proud lips, chill moulded,
No passion ever knew.
They bring to my heart, alone here,
In its haunting, beating pain,
A dark-haired girl in the sunshine,
And I want the South again.
Her sweet mouth smiles through a mist of tears,
The boom of our surf is in my ears,
The days seem mouths, and the months are years,
And I long for the South again.
JOHN POLDING.
A Prayer.
Lord, give heed to Thy servant’s plaint,
That he bow to Thy law as just;
Is it well a man should turn to the Deep
In fear lest his soul should rust ?
Is Thy power as strong on the deadened ground
As the power of the unbound Sea ?
Is Thy land but a dusty tomb, O Lord ?
For Thy truth’s sake answer me.
I feel no throb in the pulseless plain—
It lies as Thou made it—dead ;
And it is not fit—Thy pardon, Lord—
That the dead and the live should wed.
I hear low sobbing of waves that plead
To the shore that is but a bier—
Is half of Thy work unfinished, Lord?
Speak out that Thy folk may hear.
And we, who ’re made in Thy image, Lord,
The mould of Thy own good hand,
We chafe at the law—the unclean law—
To crawl on the still-born land.
The Sea’s dread mystery thralls our souls,
Fair souls that are claimed for Thee;
To whom is the ultimate triumph, Lord—
Thyself, or the endless Sea ?
U.L.
J. BARB.
Impenitent.
Give me a lovely soul to kill,
Give me a rousing dream of ill,
And the devils in hell, who work my will,
Re-echo my shout of gladness.
God gave me a soul of my own, they say,
White as the blossoms that rustle and play
In the sweet soft light of a moonbeam’s ray,
Ere dark turneth all to sadness.
But I met One there, where the slow waves fall
On the shimmering sands, with a long, low call
To the silent stars watching over all
This world, with its sin and sorrow.
And I looked deep down in the sleepy maze
Of her wondrous eyes, where the loser pays,
And the winner laughs at the soul she slays
And takes no heed for the morrow.
I have fathomed the depths of thedarkestshame,
On my blackened soul may God place the blame,
And drive it at last to the seething flame
Where I know her soul is burning.
God ! have no pity, I want none here ;
God ! have no pity, I want none there;
Only to be with her dark soul, bare,
In the pit whence there’s no returning.
N.B.W.
J.
The Restless Spirit.
The restless Spirit bruised its wings
Against the bars,
And sought in vain the key of things
Beyond the stars;
Assailed high Heaven, plunged to Hell,
Far spaces trod,
And called upon the snows to tell
The tale of God.
The Great Hierarch on Nature’s throne
The summons heard,
And answered back the mortal groan
With mortal word.
“ A sleepless God keeps up the jest
And shapes the toys,
And envies e’en the little rest
That man enjoys.
Poor fool 1 ’Tis thou hast made the book
And writ the tale,
And when thou wilt thou mayest look
Beyond the veil! ”
The restless Spirit paused—because
He feared to see,
Then looked, and saw, and lo ! there was
No mystery !
In farthest worlds a shining sun,
And nought above,
And nothing ended nor begun,
But Life and Love.
N.S.W.
ELISE ESPINASSE.

General Debilitation.
A CASE IN PYRMONT.
A Permanent Recuperation.
The Case of Mr. W. H. ATKINS.
(by our reporter.)
What an easy matter it is for a benevo-
lent being to diffuse pleasure and gladness
around him. In order to do so it is only
necessary to consider what means are pos-
sessed to lighten the burdens of our fellow-
men, and to help in whatever direction the
conscience may dictate. It is evident that
Mr. William Henry Atkins, of No. 211
Crown-road, Pyrmont, shares in this
opinion, for he said in reply to a question
put by our reporter : ,
“ Of course, I will give you any informa-
tion you require, as the knowledge I gained
may be turned to good account by some
poor sufferer in need of good advice. It
has always been my motto to help those in
distress, and I think I can do so by letting
humanity know about the remarkable ex-
perience I had£ I think that worry was at
the back of all my troubles, for after an in-
cident which grieved me greatly, I fell into
bad health. I did not care about anything
to eat, simply because I had no appetite.
The most tasty morsels imaginable failed to
tempt me as a rule, although I had to force
myself to take some nourishment when I
felt that I could not hold out any longer.
Not only was my appetite bad, but I ab-
stained from eating because of the pains
which afterwards appeared.r My stomach
always got full of wind, which shifted about
from side to side, and left cramping pains
in all directions, and heavy pains affected
my chest. Sometimes I felt as if the
breath was being crushed out of me, the
oppression was so great, and I naturally felt
deeply concerned about it. as chest com-
plaints are so serious in their consequences.
As my food was not doing me any good I
lost strength rapidly, and soon I dropped
into that languid state of indifference to
everything which is so disastrous to every
business man’s career. I felt as if I did
not care how things went, and the morbid-
ness of my spirits quite precluded me from
thinking about anything pleasant. ' Haras-
sed as I w T as by depressing headaches, and
with my eyes dim and feeling as if they
were swollen, no wonder that I lost all in-
terest in life, for the average man does not
w ish to live unless he is contented and at
ease.” ;
“ But your lot was not so hard c 6 bear
when night came, for then, I suppose, you
obtained relief in sleep?” remarked Mr.
Atkins’ interrogator.
“Don’t you believe it; if anything, my
nights were more intolerable than the days,
as I could not succeed in getting anything
like a night’s rest. Certainly, I used to
doze off from time to time, but the sleep
was not refreshing, because of the intrusion
of repulsive dreams, and when I got up I
felt as weary as if I had been awake all
night. I could hardly stand the bitter taste
which was in my mouth every morning, and
the unhealthy condition of my stomach
could be detected by the fur on my tongue;
but what I regretted more than all else was
the quick and painful throbbing of my heart.
When I laid down I felt that organ shaking
inside in a way that caused me much alarm,
so that, with every particle of strength
being gradually eaten away by my com-
plaints, Jthere was nothing in my condition
to assist me in entertaining hopes of a re-
covery. The least exertion brought on a
feeling of faintness, and when 1 stood up I
used to get quite giddy, as if everything
was on the move. All this I put down to
my extreme weakness, which was also re-
sponsible for the annoying aches in my legs
and loins From some cause or other my
arms were also intensely painful, and if ever
a nervc>"? system was wrecked it was mine.
On the least provocation I became excited
and irritable, while the trembling of my
nerves served to increase the general misery
of my existence. My body was greatly de-
nuded of flesh, and I can tell you in all
seriousness that my life was a burden which
I would willingly have cast off at any
moment.”
“ I believe you suffered in this way
several years,” interjected the scribe.
“ During the period of your illness did you
ever enlist the services of a medical man ?
“ Considering that I was lingering, wait-
ing, and hoping for years, I should rather
think I did,” replied Mr. Atkins. “ Why,
I consulted dozens of doctors, yet my suffer-
ings grew worse, and I may also state that
I have taken scores of medicines which were
procured at stores and chemists’ shops. It
would have been far better if I had kept
the money in my pocket, for all but one of the
medicines were of no service to me. Ihe
only remedy which suited my complaints
was Clements Tonic, and this was recom-
mended to me by a man living in Harris-
street, who had used it, and knew what it
could do. After taking two bottles of
Clements Tonic I felt "much better, and was
able to get about with much more freedom.
I went, to a chemist to get some more, but
he put me off by saying he could give me
something better, and pointed out the
difference in the size of the bottles, so,
foolishly enough, I bought his medicine in-
stead of Clements Tonic, and in a few days
I was as bad as ever. Then I insisted on
having Clements Tonic, and again I im-
proved under its influence, so that there was
no doubt about its being the best medicine
in the market. I would rather have one
dose of Clements Tonic than a barrelful of
any other medicine, for it proved its supe-
riority in every possible way. The first
thing it did was to make me eat and sleep,
and I was glad of the respite from my
afflictions which I had when resting. Be-
fore long a feeling of animation spread right
through my system, and I then shook off
the yoke of depression which had clouded
my past few years, as I felt assured that
Clements Tonic would pull me through. In
course of time the pains arising from indi-
gestion were quelled; the nauseous taste
left my mouth, and my head was free from
aches. Some of my doctors said my liver
was diseased, while others said my stomach
was extremely weak; but if they had seen
me after a few weeks’ use of Clements
Tonic they would not have known me. My
stomach could then digest and retain any
kind of food, and my liver symptoms were
non-existent. The shaking of my nerves
was promptly checked by Clements Tonic,
and the regulating and strengthening effects
of that medicine on my system generally
were really extraordinary. Every ache and
pain yielded to the pacifying influences of
Clements Tonic, and I am heartily thank-
ful to say that by its exclusive use my
health was re-established. I have lived in
this.house for the past 17 years, and all the
neighbours know that Clements Tonic cured

SATURDAY, JUNE 6, 1903.
Our Naval Policy—and Our Timid
Parliament.
The opening days of the second session of
the Australian Parliament have brought more
than the average amount of disappointment
to those who look to it as a national assembly
—the chosen voice of an Australian as
opposed to a parochial or a foreign sentiment.
True, almost every Parliament is disappoint-
ing. The tradition of public life has grown
to be one of small and grubbing compromise,
of a petty barter between parties, in which
principle is blended with expediency so as to
arrive as nearly as possible at an arrange-
ment which will give offence to nobody. The
politician is thus almost invariably Mr.
Politic, when he is not Mr. Facing-both-
ways, and his real delight is to be known as
a practical politician, which translated
into plain English is equivalent to
a grocer who mixes as much sand
with his sugar as the eyesight of his
customers will allow to pass without detec-
tion. The Australian Parliament is very
much superior to any of the State Parlia-
ments ; it has some ideals ; its debates
are now and again illuminated by the
glimmering of the dawn of a truly national
feeling ; its members boast a higher average
of intelligence, and therefore are somewhat
less disfigured with the stains of petty
cunning. Still it is almost as far from the
ideal Parliament as can be imagined. It
follows the political traditions of compro-
mise and expediency almost always, and at
times is so paltry in its neglects and its acts
as to suggest the miserable little municipal
council in which Aid. Jones, who wants his
allotments kerbed and guttered, is willing to
bargain with Aid. Brown, who wants his
drained, and Aid. Smith, who has a strip of
asphalt or a road-metal contract in his
mind’s eye.
• 9 •
There is nothing more absolutely and
certainly true than that in politics high suc-
cess comes only from courage—courage,
always courage. Where the palterer and
the trimmer gain tolerance, the courageous
man wins a lasting empire over his
countrymen’s minds. That is the sure
lesson of the history of every country.
Now and again its beacon shines through
(he murk of even Australian politics (which
have been of a low grade, mainly because
there has been no great inspiring struggle in
this land, no war of independence, no
desperate resistance of an invasion) to show
a Lalor, a Higinbotham, or a Ballance
winning honor, not by chaffering in the lobby
but by strenuous purpose. In the con-
temporary records, indeed, an instance can
be found in Kingston, who is the one
member of the Federal Government
most truly respected because he is the
one member who has shown some courage,
and has not hesitated to fight dishonesty
openly. It is not complimentary to the
political intelligence that Kingston’s ex-
ample has not been more widely fol-
lowed. There are, presumably, in public
life other men strong enough to stand with
him for absolute principle, and to submit to
the wild abuse of the wrongdoer for the
purpose of winning the sturdy esteem of the
best of the community. One can only be-
lieve that they are so dull as not to see the
opportunity, for certainly Kingston lacks
imitators.
m 9 * *
The speech put into the mouth of the
Governor at the opening of the Australian
Parliament by the Barton Government is a
conspicuous proof of the timorousness which
infects our public life as w r ith a palsy. It is,
throughout, couched in the deferential and
politic language of the man who wishes
to offend nobody and to please every-
body. What the Government would like to
do, but dare not, is postponed with a hypo-
critical sigh of regret that “ time will not
allow.” What the Government feels that it
must do, even though it encounters some
opposition, is put forward timidly and apolo-
getically, and in one case under the cover of
a falsehood. That is in the announcement
of the increased tribute proposed to be paid
to the British navy, on the condition that
such shadowy advantages as Australia gets
from its present subsidy are absolutely
withdrawn. The announcement reads :
You will be asked to ratifi' an agreement between the
Admiralty and the Government of the Commonwealth
which secures for the naval defence of Australia the pro-
tection of a powerful and continuously efficient squadron
of warships at a moderate cost.
• •
And it is simply a falsehood. The pro-
posal of the Barton Government is that
Australia shall increase its tribute to the
British navy from £lOO,OOO to £200,000 a
year ; that it shall also disband what little
naval forces it has of its own ; and that it
shall absolutely withdraw the condition of the
present naval agreement with Britain, which
binds that country in some sort of fashion
to keep for Australian protection the fleet for
which Australian money pays. It is true
that the pledge given by Britain in return
for the present subsidy, that the Australian
Squadron shall be kept to the Australian
coast for Australian defence, is one that could,
and probably would, be broken if necessity
arose. But it could only be broken by a breach
of faith. To avoid that unpleasant act
on Britain’s part, Premier Barton pro-
poses now to do away altogether with the
condition that the squadron we pay for
should be kept exclusively for our defence.
We are to pay tribute, and there the matter is
to end. The fleet which we pay for will pro-
bably be kept in our waters in peace time,
so that our idle rich may entertain its officers
and our brewers derive some benefit from its
sailors ; but in war time, it is expressly pro-
vided, the vessels are to be sent wherever
the exigencies of British defence suggest. A
candid statement of the Barton proposals—
proposals which Laurier firmly declined so
far as Canada was concerned—would be :
You will be asked to ratify an agreement between the
British Admiralty and the Government of the Common-
wealth which absolutely does away with any small local
naval defences that Australia now possesses ; which
absolves the British Government of the pledge, that it is
now under, of reserving the squadron paid for by Aus-
tralia for Australian defence ; which secures for Australia
nothing at all in the way of naval defence ; and which,
nevertheless, hugely increases the amount of the subsidy
to he paid by Australia to the British navy.
* • • •
That would be a fair statement of the case.
Premier Barton goes beyond being politic,
and is simply untruthful. He has not even
the courage of his betrayal. Were he to admit
frankly that on his recent visit to England,
dazzled by the rays of a G.C.M.G. decora-
tion ; bewildered by the flattery of London
(the more welcome because he had ceased to
receive the flattery of Australia when he
ceased to earn it) ; misled by banqueteering
speeches and specious arguments—he put
his name to an undertaking which is not so
much pro-imperial as anti-Australian ; and
that, having put his name to it, he expected
Australia to stand to him ; were he to say
these things his attitude would permit of
some sort of apology. But, timid and pal-
tering in the first instance, when he allowed
himself to be beguiled into saying “Yes ” to
an agreement which not only drains Australia
of a large tribute and removes from her
whatever protection the British fleet offered,
but also includes the abolition of any Aus-
tralian naval force which might have been
relied upon in an emergency, Premier
Barton is timid and paltering now, and
seeks to conceal his betrayal of the national
interests by the weak falsehood of the
Governor’s speech.
* * * •
And there was not a whisper of protest in
the Australian Parliament. Government
supporters sat dumb, swallowing the false-
hood as easily as the betrayal. The Oppo-
sition made no move. The Labor party
quietly acquiesced, and devoted its energy
to maundering over the dead-and-buried in-
cident of the Six Hatters who were not ex-
cluded. It might have been a Parliament
of the days of Elizabeth, so deferential was
it. The attitude of Government and Oppo-
sition men is in a measure under-
standable. Most of them cherish a
vague hope that they will be pro-
tected from desecration in their old age by
the hanging of a K.C.M.G. cross on their
breasts, or else have some other motive which
binds them to Britain, or the British
importer, rather than to Australia. But the
Labor party’s treachery to Australian ideals
is not so easy to explain. That party pro-
fesses to be the guardian of the public purse,
and should have protested against £200,000
a year of the country’s money going to no
better use than the securing of decorations
and titles for prominent Australian poli-
ticians. That party professes to be White
Australian and democratic in its ideas, and
should have fought from the first, with
tooth and nail, a scheme which, in the
case of war, might leave the
mercy of the fleet of Britain’s ally, Japan.
Probably, if the truth were known, the old
arguments of “policy ” and “ practical poli-
tics ” were used to nobble the Labor party, so
that it should refrain from dangerous protest;
and for the bait of some measure or other of
labor reform —which would have been won
far more certainly by courage than by bar-
gaining—it agreed to take up the K.C.M.G.
attitude on this question.
* m * *
The Labor party in the Australian Parlia-
ment indeed deserves the highest encomiums
from the Tories for its tame, fed-out-of-the-
hand attitude at the opening of this session.
There were three occasions for earnest pro-
test and hostile action —the presence of
Robert Reid, of the firm of Reid and Co.,
customs swindlers, in the Senate ; the false-
hood in the Address-in-Reply by which a
betrayal of Australia’s naval interests and a
looting of her treasury was made to masque-
rade as a benefit to Australia ; and the law-
less attack, during the recess, of Premier
Irvine on the Federal Constitution. On
each of these points a party with true ideas
of honest democracy and patriotic nationalism
would have divided the House. But the
Labor party, with its notions of being prac-
tical ... politic, took up an intensely lady-
like attitude of indifference. To have
referred to Robert Reid, of the Senate,
would have been “bad form;” to have
accused Premier Barton of putting a mis-
leading statement into the mouth of the
Governor-General would have vexed that
amiable gentleman ; to have impeached
Irvine as an anarchical Jack Cade would
have been going too far out of the beaten
path, and would have exposed the party to
the danger of being left in a solitary
position—the only defenders of Constitu-
tional law and of private liberty. The
Labor party avoided all these pitfalls ; it evi-
dently has its full share of the politic weak-
ness of liking to be “ with the crowd.”
• • • «.
On the present appearance of affairs, the
Australian Naval Tribute Bill will pass
through Parliament without any genuine
hostility. The Government is pledged to it;
the Opposition dare not, even for the sake
of office, go against the British importing
interests which keep it in funds ; the Labor
party may make a show of ladylike disap-
proval, but evidently contemplates no really
active hostility. Still it is a duty to point out
clearly, again and again, what are the conse-
quences of the new agreement which is
falsely described as “ securing for the naval
defence of Australia the protection of a pow-
erful and continuously efficient squadron of
warships at a moderate cost.” The cost will
be very great, more than sufficient to secure
for Australia a squadron of fast cruisers for
coast defence, which is the total of its real
requirements. The only advantage secured
will bethe presence of the warships in times of
peace ; for it is expressly stipulated that in
case of war the British Admiralty may with-
draw the ships to whatever spot it pleases.
For that advantage—purely a “ society ”
one—Australia will sacrifice money sufficient
to secure an efficient fleet of its own
and will also (which is more serious)
have to abandon its own locally controlled
naval reserves, coast-defence ships and
torpedo boats. It will strip herself of
its money and of its rights of local defence.
An equivalent policy in military matters
would be to disband all our local troops, to
pay the War Office an annual subsidy, and to
accept the quartering on our territory of a
number of regiments of British or Indian
troops—withdrawable by Britain in case of
war. Australia is asked to sacrifice its money,
its claim on British protection, and its right
of organising local forces in naval affairs.
It could not be asked to sacrifice more if
it were a subject state at the feet of a
victorious foe.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
Freetrader Braddon, the Protectionist.
The following extract from the Australian Parlia-
mentary reports speaks for itself :
Mr. Edward Braddon passed on to the accusation that
he had stated in Tasmania that he was in favor of a duty
on Maoriland oats and potatoes. There was something
in that accusation. He apologised for what appeared to
be a lapse from Freetrade. Tasmania had made many
sacrifices to come into the Federation. Maoriland had
made none, and refused to join. It was not right, then,
to give Maoriland the same advantages as Tasmania.
Therefore, as a matter of high principle, he believed in
retaining the duties referred to.
As a matter of high principle —let it be especially
noted that it is as a matter of high principle—
Braddon, the bogus Freetrader, the sham Cob-
denite, the collapsed supporter of Shoddy, most
cordially believes in heavily protecting the chief
industries of his own State. Braddon, the Free-
trader, holds that because Tasmania has made
certain sacrifices in joining the Commonwealth
its special industries should be protected;
but though N.S.W., Queensland, S.A. and
Victoria have made equal sacrifices in joining
the same Commonwealth on the same terms,
Braddon, the Parochialist, can’t see that their
special industries call for any Protection what-
Bver. Braddon, the man with the parish-pumn
mmd and the local intellect, holds that because
the English-speaking State of Maoriland (whose
products compete especially with those of his own
b lyspeck) is outside the Commonwealth its K oods
should be shut out by a Protective tariff; but when
say Victoria asks that goods of England, America
and Germany, which it finds to be its most dan
gerous competitors, and which are as much outside
the Commonwealth as Maoriland is or even
further outside, should be similarly penalised
Braddon, the Politically One-eyed, is shocked
beyond measure at their ignorance of
all true political economy. Braddon, the
I heorist, says that Protection is injurious
when Victoria or S. A. asks for it, and is grieved
that these illiterate States can’t understand so
simple a proposition ; yet Braddon, the Lopsided
demands Protection for Tasmania’s iron and Tas’-
mania’s produce, and savs Protection is a re
markably good thing for Tasmania. Braddon
the Backslider, lately stumped his island denounc
mg Protection (he was himself for five years
a Freetrade Premier maintaining a Protec-
tive tariff) and bounties and bonuses as unclean
and ridiculous things, and yet, at Emu Bay
Braddon, the Fiscal Renegade, said he enthusias
tically approved of both Protection and bonuses—
for the Tasmanian iron industry, though the system
still remained loathsome and accursed for the N S
Wales boot industry. Why, in Braodon’s own
wild 1 reetrade language, should all the Common-
wealth be taxed in order to prop up Tasmania’s
tottering industries according to the tenets of an
exploded fiscal superstition, &c. ? Will Braddon
the Incoherent, explain ? ’
+ .
The Sitting-Down Policy.
Australia has not been doing at all well for a
number of years, as the natural culmination of a
helpless policy of borrowing, cadging, importing
instead of manufacturing for ourselves, borrowing
some more to pay for imports, bragging about the
“ magnificent natural resources of the country,”
and sitting-down to wait for something to turn up.
The press of Australia has been, and still is, the
worst offender in this respect, for it is the
principal expounder of the policy of helplessness
that afflicts this country. It has weird expectancy
in its eye, and melancholy apology in its voice.
The great mass of the city papers have spent
years and years telling the people to wait—till
the drought broke, till the new loan came in, till
the population increased, till the “great natural
resources of the country ” had time to assert them-
selves. Now they say pretty unanimously that
the whole trouble lies in the Immigration Restric-
tion Law, and they urge the public to sit down
and wait till that is repealed ; in fact, the cpat-
tail of the daily press is worn and green with long
sitting down to wait for something. As regards
this latest aspect of the gospel of sitting down,
the following example is cut from Launceston
Examiner, though the same sort of thing can be
found any day in almost any daily paper any-
where :
This small population is endeavoring to open up the
country by building roads and railways, erecting wharves
and bridges, and in doing so has accumulated a large
debt, which is pressing heavily on the taxpayers. Mr.
Green has stated that the two things which the island
most needs are population and capital, and the correct-
ness of the statement will be universally acknowledged.
As Bishop Mercer remarked, in opening the discussion
on the paper, miles upon miles of roads have to be main-
tained by a sparse population, and our railways entail a
loss, roundly speaking, of £lOO,OOO a year, because there
are not sufficient people travelling on" them to make them
pay. If the population were doubled to-morrow the cost
of administration of road and railway maintenance would
be but slightly increased, but we should have twice as
many taxpayers to help us to bear the burden. But any-
thing in the shape of immigration is opposed by the
party who have been dominating Australian politics of
late. Under one plea or another, the doors have been
closed against workers from other States. One of the
leaders of that party has also publicly stated that we can
do without the man with capital. Yet we find that the
State whose progress has been more rapid of late than
any other in the world is receiving immigrants at the rate
of 20,000 a month, and it was estimated that one large
party of yeomen who left England for Canada lately took
with them £500,000 in the shape of capital.
And having thus laid the blame on the other
fellow, the helpless Eat Man of Tasmania feels he
can comfortably sit down and let things slide.
But before be sits down, perhaps he will look at
the other side of the case :
(1) In nine out of the 17 years up to the end of 1901,
Tasmania sent away more people than it received— and
there was no Immigration Restriction Law till 190%. In
the ten years (1592-1901) prior to the beginning of the
Immigration Restriction Law', Tasmania lost 5251 people
by excess of departures. Also from 1860 to 1880 it lost
4655 people by excess of departures. It has been losing
almost constantly for 40 years. Therefore, it is a cowardly
lie to describe the law which shuts out niggers, diseased
persons and lunatics as the reason why there is no
great influx of population.
(2) One visible reason why' artisans don’t flock into
Tasmania, as they do into Canada, is that Tasmania
(unlike Canada with its consistently Protectionist 50 per
cent, tariff) can only say to the average artisan on his
arrival, “ We have no work for you in your own line of
business.” Canada imports to the value of about £6 per
inhabitant annually, and makes the rest of what it wants
itself. Tasmania imports to the value of nearly £l2 per
inhabitant annually, and makes the rest itself. There-
fore, Canada has, on a rough estimate, £6 worth per
annum more work and wages to offer to its people than
Tasmania has.
(3) One visible reason why agriculturists don’t go to
Tasmania is that the V.D.L. Co. is allowed to keep
a huge area of the island roadless and shut up.
Also, the available State lands are roadless and inacces-
sible, and the little private land that is available and
accessible is too dear. A large deputation of Victorian
farmers went over to Tasmania recently looking for land,
and when this influx of desirable settlers with money
turned up, Tasmania really didn’t know what to do with
it.
(4) Tasmania has spent its land revenue as it went
along, instead of using it for public works. It has
borrowed for all its public works, and for almost every-
thing else. Now it spends most of its time scraping up
the interest on its debt. It can’t borrow the money to
open up the great wilderness of the south-west. It
hasn’t courage enough to raise the money by taxation.
It hasn’t courage enough to shift the V.D.L. Co.
So, with a huge proportion of the State quite unin-
habited and inaccessible, it sends away intending settlers,
and says, with tears, that it is the fault of the Labor
party.
(5) In any case, there is not likely to be a rush of
settlers to any State which is notoriously loaded up with
debt, and where every new arrival has to shoulder £5O of
financial dead horse for himself, and £5O for his wife, and
£5O for each of his children. In no part of the world
is there a rush of settlers to a country which is deep
in debt, which imports much and manufactures little,
which has no job to offer to the skilled artisan, and
which can’t offer land to the agriculturist because it
hasn’t the money to make roads to open up the land, or
because it lets a foreign V.D.L. Co. close up a great
extent of country and order the intending settler off.
But, anyhow, why should there be this lamenta-
tion about the non-arrival of new population
when the country fails so utterly to find work
for the population it has already ? This yearning
for more unemployed in order to promote
employment is a ridiculous business. Australia
is overcrowded in the true sense of the word.
Every country which has more peoole than it can
find work for is overcrowded.


Freetrader for Freetrade Newspapers.
That very excitable, not to say hysterical, advo-
cate of Freetrade, the Sydney Daily Telegraph,
has been, during the past week or two, coming out
daily purple with indignation at the cruel conduct
of the See Government in applying to its industry
the great principles of Freetrade and unaided
private enterprise. In its frenzied protests
against the withdrawal of the State subsidy from
the industry of newspaper publishing it has for-
gotten all about Mill and Coboen and Spencer ;
it has also forgotten a good many of the facts
of the controversy. This latter lapse needs to
be corrected in the interests of the public. The
history of the whole matter is, in brief, this :
Some years ago—many years ago in fact—the
Freetrade daily newspapers of Sydney persuaded
a so-called Freetrade Government to enact
free carriage by post of newspapers—N.S.W.
papers only. This amounted at once to an
almost prohibitive postal tariff against outside
newspapers, and an enormous subsidy to local
enterprise in the newspaper publishing line. The
legality of the step was unquestionable; the decency
of it from a “Freetrade” Government for the
benefit of Freetrade newspapers, which savagely
condemned proposals for similarly helping other
and far more deserving industries, is another
matter. Then the law was stretched a little to
provide still further privileges for the Sydney
Freetrade newspapers; an early morning train
service was instituted to carry their goods to
remote centres, where they could compete against
country newspapers. To secure this for the Free-
trade newspapers the Freetrade Government paid
a special subsidy to the N.S.W. Railway Commis-
sioners. With Federation came a new era of com-
parative purity and honesty of administration. The
Australian Government, not being under the thumb
of any coterie of newspapers, imposed postage on
papers—following therein the precedent set by
practically every other Government in the world —
and refused to continue the special subsidy
(about the legality of which there is grave
question) to the N.S.W. railways for carry-
ing newspapers free. This caused a bitter
shriek from the Freetrade papers, but they
were still in this position, that they could distri-
bute the bulk of their newspapers through the
train service by themselves paying the subsidy
originally found by the Government. That was a
half- way house to an equitable arrangement which
obviously could only be temporary. Newspapers
from other States coming to N.S.W. were still
required to pay for rail carriage at a quarter the
usual parcel rate; N.S.W. papers alone were
favored by a rate approximating to one-twelfth
the usual parcel rate. It is a moot point whether
that did not amount to a differential rate con-
trary to the Australian Constitution. It was
also open to question whether the Federal
Post-office could continue to allow the N.S.W.
railways to compete with it on unfair terms
in the carriage of newspapers. The See Govern-
ment settled one part of the difficulty, at any rate,
by applying to N.S.W. papers exactly the same
railway tariff as that paid by Victorian news-
papers : it thus enacted Freetrade within N.S.W.
for Commonwealth papers. As to the absolute
justice of this there can be no question. As to
the brassy impudence of the protests of N.S.W.
I reetrade newspapers, which openly claim pro-
tection against Victorian newspapers in the
Riverina, there can be also no question. The
business of producing newspapers is, on the
whole, a highly profitable one; the dividends
earned by it are large; the natural protection
it has against outside competition puts it in a
uniquely favorable position to stand the open air
of Freetrade. So the fact that they have enjoyed
a high degree of Protection and bonuses in the
past, constitutes no earthly reason why the Free-
trade newspapers should now have Protection
against Victorian competition, and a State sub-
sidy in addition. But the fact that they demand
these aids constitutes an excellent reason why
their pleas for Freetrade for other industries, and
their frenzied denunciations of bonuses for other
enterprises should be very much discounted. It’s
a poor cook who objects to his own victuals.
The Freetrade Federal Programme.
George Reid lias made, in the House of Repre-
sentatives, a solemn exposition of the policy of the
I ederal Freetrade party, and there is little in it
to cheer up this, at present, rather depressed
community. It was partly a looking backward
and a rehash of the old weary battered stock lies
of the Importing Party; and the rest of it was a
looking forward to the good time coming when
local manufactures will be destroyed so that there
may be more imports on which to collect duty.
It was, on the whole, a muddled and dejected
utterance evolved from the tail end of a worn and
weary intelligence. George Reid complained, to
begin with, that the Government should have
made the establishment of a Federal High Court
its first official act, but as he said last session that
there was no need of haste about the establishment
of the Federal High Court, and as he bragged before
several audiences during the recess that he had
successfully blocked the Bill for the establishment
of that court, this was merely the same carping
criticism of the same old useless potterer to which
Australia is now so well accustomed. He again
assured the country that he was against Federal
direct taxation, and that, if the Freetrade party
got into power, there would be no Freetrade, for
going to eat its principles and depend
wholly on customs and excise taxes, just as if Free-
trade had never been heard of. George Reid
was elected on a policy of Federal direct taxation,
but as he is always against the principles on
which he was elected, there is little in that. He
said that he cordially approved of the clause
“ under which the six hatters were kept out ”
they weren’t kept out, but the brazen lie about
the keeping of them out has been told so often
that most people now believe it. Also he said
that he was in favor of the repeal of the clause,
and that to keep out (who was kept out ?)
honest Englishmen, Irishmen, or Scotchmen was
a disgrace. Exactly how much he knew about
the honesty of the men referred to doesn’t
appear, but nowadays the story of the F“ -'leathers
who weren’t kept out, and who were oriiyßllfeiyed
because it was eight days before their bumptious
employer would sink his awful dignity sufficiently
to supply the information required by law, is an
essential item in every Freetrade speech. And in
addition to this George Reid outlined the policy
which he intends to follow when Australia is
cursed with his presence at the head of public
affairs : Although he is allegedly a Freetrader he
is not going to practise Freetrade. Although he
says that customs duties are a ruinous method of
raising revenue, and that a 12£ per cent, duty
adds 25 per cent, to the cost of goods, he is going
to depend on customs duties. After a life-
time spent in shrieking about the blessings of
free ports he isn’t going to make the ports
free. But he is going to reduce the duties in the
hope that imports will increase so enormously that
the people will pay more customs taxation than
they do now. He is confident that if he does this
the people of Australia, in addition to having to
pay more customs taxes than they do now, will
also have to pay many millions more for imported
goods which will, to a great extent, take the place
of local manufactures and throw the Australian
citizen out of a job, and that Reid’s friend, Im-
porter M‘Millan, will then have a good time.
The joy of paying millions more for imported
goods and a good deal more in customs taxes, and
having less local employment (for we can’t import
our boots and manufacture them also), and voting
for Freetrade yet never getting it, constitute the
millenium that Australia is to enjoy when George
Reid gets into office.

Parson Hennessey.
The Rev. N. M. Hennessey, Tremayne Ddnstan’s
successor in ‘‘Mr. James’s ’’church in Pitt-street,
Sydney, having been in Australia quite three
weeks, begins to stroke the fur of the front pews
the right way ; and all the daily papers start to
cheer excitedly—on his reputation, they didn’t
think he was that sort at all. Says the Rev.
Hennessey :
There is a third group, which boasts that it holds the
balance of power, that it can affect legislation either for
the city or the State. Now, I want to put a question to
that party. How is it that, within the small area
bounded by Elizabeth, Campbell, Hay and Market streets,
and Darling Harbor, they allow' 50 public-houses to con-
tinue ? In Woollahra, there are not more than 17. If this
third party have the power they claim, why do thev not
do what the wealthier people of Woollahra—people belong-
ing to a class they sometimes denounce in intemperate
language—have already done ? And when they have
answered that question, I shall have another for them.
The Rev. Hennessey is evidently, after all, only
one of the sort who empty their slops and throw
their sardine-tins into the next person’s yard, and
then pass remarks in public about the filthy
habits of their neighbor, as shown by the quantity
of slops and sardine-tins on his premises.
Another sort of preacher than the Rev, Hen-
nessey’s sort might have looked at the matter in
this fashion :
There are people who sit in the front seats in our
tabernacles when they’re nice, respectable tabernacles,
and drop sixpences in the plate regularly. Now I want
to ask those people a question. I notice that round
their own neighborhood there aren’t any pubs.—they
won’t have them there. In all Woollahra there
are only 17. Yet in the slummy part of the
city there are 50 within a small area; and
as it’s dead-certain that the people in the slums
don’t own those pubs., I want to know who does. You
needn’t tell me—l know. You own them. You, who
are too respectable to have pubs, in your own neigh-
borhood, plant them alongside the miserable hovels of
the poor; and if the miserable and wretched poor, with
no home comforts, throng- these places, and waste their
scanty wages, and ruin even such homes as they have—
you are to blame, you smug hypocrites, you whited
sepulchres!
That’s what the Rev. Hennessey, or any other
rev., might have said, and said with truth. But
he didn’t; he said other things; and so he gets
into the papers, and perhaps the collection will
go up eighteenpence next Sunday.
Put the Shutters Up.
The Charity Organisation Society of N.S.W.
doesn’t sound promising, and its record is as bad
as its name. In the month of April last, when
3000 men and women were crowding the State
poorhouses and 3000 men obtaining out-door
relief, when children were being run in day by
day like strayed sheep, and in hundreds of
“homes” poverty sat black, cold, desolate,
ghastly—in April, the C.O.S. managed to raise
the sum of £29 and to spend £65. One-third cash
and two-thirds loan, according to the established
Geebung policy. Twenty-nine pounds sterling to
deal with whatever distress may exist in the city
and State; for nothing less than this does the
C.O.S. propose. It starts with the assumption
that “private charity is dangerous, if not bad,
and breeds imposters and makes mendicants.”
(Oh, most contemptible spawn of a genera-
tion of hypocrites, and yet lip-pledged to
tbe faith which proclaims charity without
any qualification the foundation item of life
and the main clue on immortality !) Private
charity, therefore, should be discouraged, and
yet, lest want should exist without relief, we shall
form a society, we shall have an honorary
treasurer, and a sec. and an hon. visiting
committee ; and if any fitting cases present them-
selves to us we shall deal with them. In Sydney
they deal to the extent of £29 a month, a sum
which any one of a hundred amongst the mem-
bers must have saved by giving soup tickets when
his conscience, if he had one, must have told him
half-sovereigns would have barely sufficed. It is
a king amongst humbugs, the C.O.S. Melbourne
begat it 30 years ago, and what has it done for
Melbourne, beyond provide an excuse for mean-
ness and furnish billets for mean people ? Who
can ever forget that Melbourne case of the
woman with the sick child referred to the 0.0. S.
on Xmas eve? They would inquire: Yes.
Meanwhile she must wait. She went down
to the Domain to wait, and in the morning—
Xmas morning—walked up to seek a charitable
undertaker, for in the night the child had died.
That is one sort of fruit Charity Organisation
brings forth. But there is another, and without
it in Sydney there would be no 0.0.5. Look up
the list of officials and count the busybodies, the
nobodies, and the gold-bug bodies who figure
there—who get each a little bit of cheap publicity
out of it, and because of that and the fatman’s
necessary excuse they are there. N.S.W. never
has seen—probably never will see—a worse year
than this of 1903. The exigencies strain every
State-kept (which is loan-kept) institution to the
utmost, and it ought to prick these C.O.S. folks
into such action as would justify their existence.
It pricks them to the tune of .£29 per month. It
shows them as they are, stultifiers, not organisers,
furnishers of cheap reasons, fraudulent issuers of
spurious coin as the true gold of charity. Surely
it is time the brass plate came down and the
shutters went up. The spectacle, in this time
of awful poverty and desolation, of an office
and a charity and an Organisation and a blare of
trumpets, and a sec. and an hon. treasurer and an
hon. this and an hon. that—to raise for the actual
purposes of charity £29 a month !
The Price of Small Tariff Concsesions.
Malcolm M'Eacharn told Melbourne Chamber
of Commerce, at its annual meeting, that Vic-
torian imports had lately been increasing largely,
to the great injury of Victorian manufactures.
The Argus ought to have got great joy out of the
fact, and to have shown conclusively that Victoria
was having a far better time than usual. Instead,
however, it proceeded to -be almost violent, and
to say that Malcolm was no gentleman. Why
the Freetraders should have such a horror of
Freetrade triumphs this paper doesn’t know ; it
simply notes the. interesting fact that the Free-
trader nearly always does act violently when
it is pointed out to him that imports
are booming and that our own people
have less work and wages in consequence.
In qrdinary circumstance there certainly should
have been and would have been no such
increase. Practically no loan money has
been coming: into the country, while the
average earnings of the people have, for
the past 18 months, been less than usual.
Notwithstanding these things, many extra
thousands’ worth of foreign goods have been
imported. In other words, at a time when
Victoria was thrown to an unusual extent on its
own resources through the slamming of the money-
lenders’ doors, and at the very time, too, when
the “great natural industries” failed and the
protected industries were consequently more than
ever needed to keep the people employed,
Victoria received far more than usual of foreign-
made goods, and had to find the money to pay for
them, just when it had less than usual to pay
with. As the result of a long-sustained
policy of Protection, the State had practically
shut out foreign boots and shoes ; in 1899 only
£38,483 worth were imported ; all the rest of its
boot requirements its own workmen supplied.
The Federal tariff, however, brought down the
Victorian duty. It had been almost, but not
quite, enough to keep for Victorians the work of
shoeing Victorians; a little whittled off that duty,
already barely sufficient, opened the doors of the
State for £77,154 worth of these foreign goods in
1902. In the furniture trade the position was a good
deal worse, simply because the reduction of duty
was worse. A 30 per cent, duty in 1899 secured
for Victorians the supply of all but £32,568 of
their requirements; 10 per cent, was knocked off
the protection, and in 1902, notwithstanding that
the bootmaker and the farmer had less money to
spend on furniture, £85,487 worth came in from
abroad and had to be paid for. The clothing
trade suffered even worse again than the furniture
trade. With duties ranging from 35 to 40 per
cent, against him, the foreign worker could only
find a Victorian market in 1889 for goods
valued at £186,015; in 1902, when the protection
had been reduced to from 15 to 25 per cent., the
foreigner wrested from Victoria’s own workmen
£254,762 more trade than he had had in the earlier
year. Precisely the same thing happened in
connection with the manufacture of machinery,
brushware, earthenware, matches, and hats—the
protection was reduced, and the foreign imports
increased. More money had to be paid to the
foreigner when there was less to pay him with.
And yet the amount by which the duty was
reduced seemed in most cases small; it was just
the sort of small concession which even the Daily
Syme, when he sent his Freetrade friends, Simon
Frazer and Zeal, to the Australian Parliament,
seemed to think it necessary to make to Free-
trade. If Victoria is Protectionist it must vote
Protection. In the Vic. State Parliament one or
two alleged Freetrade members did not seriously
count. In the Australian Parliament, however,
they do count; there they can join forces with the
Calico Jimmies from other States, and the com-
bination, even if it isn’t strong enough to rule, is
at least strong enough to win concessions—the
concession, say, of a 15 per cent, duty instead of
one of 20 per cent. Victoria ought to know now
what concessions of that kiud cost; and, knowing
the price, it should once and for always resolve to
give up sending any more Frazers and Zeals to
the Australian Legislature.

Treasonable language from Sydney Telegraph’s
London correspondent re the state of Freetrade
England, and how it is being overtaken by its
Protectionist rivals:
England has reached an acute stage in its existence....
The people in the British Isles must think of themselves
first and last and all the time. They must realise that
their commercial supremacy is being challenged by
nations like Germany and the United States, which,
although they have not succeeded in compelling the
English flag to strike, have reduced it to half-mast.
London was, and in a sense still is, the financial centre of
the world, but less so than it was two or three years ago.
England has no war on its hands, it has no foreign com-
plications, it is on the friendliest possible terms with
every nationality in Europe, its rapprochement with the
United States has almost reached the stage of affection •
and yet English consols, the most thickly of
all England's securities, have fallen from 114 to 90. . .
This would not mean much if there was any prospect o
an immediate revival. As a matter of fact, the financial
centre is being shifted to some extent from London to
Paris and New York. London was the financial Green-
wich Observatory of the world. The financial lines Of
latitude and longitude were taken from London* its bank
rate and its market rate governed the Continent and the
United States. It is so still nominally, but not altogether
actually. London is nearly as much governed by Paris
and New York as it governs them. It may regain its
former undoubted ascendancy. Probably it will, probably
also only for a time.

The Unemployed.
Sydney Daily Telegraph, last week, in a sen-
sational four-column article, suddenly discovered
the “unemployed”—discussed the evil at great?
length, made a quotation from Herbert Spencer,
and came at last to the conclusion that the un-
employed exist because our present breed of men
are not like the early colonists, who would “ work
long hours for low wages and poor food.” That,
as far as can be judged, is the D.T. remedy for
the unemployed—longer hours, lower wages,
poorer food for the workers ; and it must be ad-
mitted that, from a Tree trade point of view,
it is the only reasonable remedy that can
be suggested. Our natural “Freetrade” in-
dustries, wool-growing and mining, are not
sufficiently great to maintain the present popula-
tion. It is unreasonable to hope for any great
further development in either ; indeed, the records
of the past few years have been of shrinkages. If,
then, there are to be no unemployed, the other
workers of the community must engage in other
industries. But, in doing so, they come into
competition at once with cheaper workers in
Europe and Asia—the men who work longer
hours for lower wages and poorer food ; if Free-
trade is observed, they have no bulwark against
that competition, and they must come down to
the wage, hour and food conditions of the Russian,
the Hindoo, and the Japanese. And, in practical
working, it would be found that many could not
come down to these conditions and live, as does
an Asiatic coolie, on a diet of rice, houseless,
and almost without raiment. So the unem-
ployed difficulty would not be wholly
(though it would be partly) solved even with
the Freetrade ideal of low wages, poor food and
long hours. There is another way out of the un-
employed difficulty—the way which the D.T. is
wilfully blind to, but which is yet being slowly
taken, and that is the way by which the United
States and Canada have reached to a great pro-
sperity—the way of Protection. Australia imports
from abroad annually £42,000,000 worth of goods,
the major portion of which could be manufactured
here by our own workers—articles of iron and
pottery, textile fabrics, food supplies, meats,
leather in its various forms, wood-work and glass-
ware, printed matter and liquors. If by scientific
Protection every manufactured article which
could be produced locally ynder reasonable
conditions was practically debarred from
entering the Commonwealth, employment of
a . remunerative kind, in iron works, woollen
mills, potteries, food factories, would be found
at once for every man who wants work in
the country, and for many more besides. Then
with a proper administration of the Immigration
Restriction Act, the influx of outside unemployed
of an undesirable class could be prevented, and
the want-of-work problem solved for good, except
in regard to the shiftless, the lazy and the abso-
lutely incompetent—the small residuum of every
social organisation. The remedy for the unem-
ployed evil by the Freetrade plan is long hours
and low wages ; the National Protectionist ideal
is more industries ; and to that ideal we have so far
progressed haltingly, mainly because the Labor
party shows a curious reluctance to come to a
definite choice between the two methods.
Just now, when Australia is ruined by borrowing,
by the repression of manufactures and other
drought-proof industries in order to foster
the attempt to grow wheat in a desert, by the
land sale policy and the chronic refusal of the
wealthy classes to pay taxes, and when the
one great overshadowing problem is to prevent
lack of employment degenerating into wholesale
starvation, almost every Tory is unanimous that
the whole trouble arises because more people
don’t come here. E. B. Forrest, of Queensland,
who was a very mild person when he ran against
Kingston, raged greatly on this subject at the
A.M.P. meeting, where he told the old
distorted yarn of the Six Blathers over again,
and complained that the Federal Government
was “blocking people of British blood.”
If he had said that to Kingston, the
big Minister of Customs would have
asked him to name the British - blooded
person who was blocked, and then Forrest would
doubtless have crawled under the table in his
inability to remember any single instance. The
cheerful impertinence with which the Australian
Tory assumes that people want to come to the
most drought-stricken and debt-laden white man’s
country on this earth—the country which is full
of his decayed banks—and are only prevented by
the fact that certain hatters were delayed for a
few days in landing, is something enormous.
During the 20 years 1881-1901 Queensland only
pained 6429 people (say 321 people per year) by
immigration apart from those whose passages
were paid by the State out of loan funds. When
E. B. Forrest and his friends have made their
country a place so carefully shunned as all that,
the less they say the better.

Dr. Mackellar, M.L.C., went out of his way, at
Bank of N.S.W. meeting, to make a reference’ to
the Victorian railway strike :
The attempt by a comparatively small section of the
employees of the State to coerce the authorities by a
strike with a view of disorganising the whole railway
traffic of Victoria was well calculated to destroy the con-
fidence of the world in our Parliamentary control, and I
think that the calm and resourceful determination of the
Government can scarcely fail to produce a healthy tone
in the community; for had the attempt to over-ride
Parliamentary authority succeeded, the ultimate effects
would have been fraught with disaster to the best
interests of the whole people.
And he had never a word to say in condemnation
of the “calm, resourceful” Premier who asked the
Vic. Parliament to pass an Act which would have
allowed any body (the Rats Push, for instance)
to break into the Melbourne branch of the Bank
of N.S.W., if it was suspeoted that any cheques'
for the railway strikers were passing through its
hands. Bank Presidents should eschew politics •
when they make ignorant remarks on political
subjects customers are apt to think that they are
equally ignorant on banking matters.

“Wiser Counsels Will Prevail.”
Lord Onslow, Under Parliamentary Secretary of State,
gaid in a speech at Glasgow that an idea of creating an
Australian navy was noticeable in certain quarters of the
Commonwealth, He hoped, however, that wiser counsels
would prevail. Lord Balfour, of Burleigh, Secretary for
Scotland, stated that the colonies recognised that the
disposition of their naval forces must be directed from
London. — Cablegram.
It were better had you gone slow
With your gospel, noble Onslow,
With your picture of the footman running at the
Empire’s wheel;
It were better had you stated
That this land is animated
With a strong dislike for helotry and manacles of
steel!
When you said, dear Lord, at Glasgow,
That you’d like to see our “ brass ” go
Into British naval coffers, you told but a futile
tale;
But fear not, dear Lord Onslow,
We will keep the cash we’ve won slow—
We will burst it not on Britain; wiser counsels
will prevail!
And 0, noble boss of Burleigh,
Rise and learn this lesson early:
We will run our own sweet navy when we get it
off the stocks,
Lest you man its bridge with princes,
Who —as histpry evinces—
Chiefly shine at piling cruisers high and dry upon
the rocks.
We can do that for ourself, Lord,
Therefore we will keep our pelf, Lord—
When we start our Austral navy we will steer it
through the gale.
Hope not, Burleigh, that we’ll loan it—
We shall keep and run and own it—
We will not sublet to Britain ; wiser counsels will
prevail !
When we get our navy going,
And its furnace-bars are glowing,
We will find the men to steer it —we shall breed
the hearts to drive !
When our own flag bloweth o’er us
We shall hew ourselves a chorus—
We shall write a hymn of battle that shall keep
ourselves alive!
When we’ve hoisted coal and kit in
We will not send home to Britain
For a crew of Hinglish sailors and a British
captain pale;
We will grow the crew and captain,
We will not be Empire trapped in—
We’ll be steersmen, owners, fighters—wiser coun-
sels will prevail!

Patriotism Exalteth a Nation.
♦ ♦
This may not be quite orthodox, but it is nevertheless true. It is also a fact that in N.S.W.
patriotism is sadly lacking in its people. Parochialism and selfishness are rampant eveiy-
where-in Parliament and People. Instead of AUSTRALIAN MANUFACTURED GOODS
of equal merit to the Foreign-made Article being preferred, they are only taken if CHEAPER.
The very men who cry the loudest, “ AUSTRALIA FOR THIS AUSTRALIANS ! ’’—even to
the extent of keeping out the English Hatters—are those who, from pure prejudice, call
for the Foreign-made Article in preference to the Australian production. This is not the
spirit that prevails in America, and which has made it the greatest country on the face of
the earth. There nothing is good unless it is “ ’Murkan,” and so it must be here if we are
to become a Nation. The Rosewarne Packing Co., Limited, have lately added to the other
branches of their canning business the manufacture of Potted Ham and other Meats, m
two and four-ounce tins. The packages are neatly got up, the contents are AT LEASI
EQUAL TO, AND THE PRICE IS LOWER THAN, THE IMPORTED ARTICLE. Be
patriotic enough to give them a trial, and you will find that they are REALLY GOOD.
They may be had from all the Principal Storekeepers and Grocers.
over the long, confidential beer, will understand
this. Their stories are mostly about vessels
which come in for repair with their plates so worn
that anybody can drive his foot through the hull
in many places, and which are patched up with
cement, because cement is cheaper than new
plates, and which cheerfully go to sea
again, presumably having been inspected by
the blind eye of authority, and passed without
question. In extreme old age it seems as if
a vessel’s bottom was liable in Australia to be one-
quarter iron plate and three-quarters cement,
and if the great dead weight and accumulation of
cement cracks badly when the vessel is being
wrenched about in a tumbled sea, then the
bottom might almost as well be made of glass or
ship biscuit or any other breakable. Just a coffin
of rotten plates held together by earthenware
repairs is the fair description of a kind of vessel
which appears to be quite possible on the Aus-
tralian coast. What kind of vessel the Oaklands
was this paper doesn’t know (it hears nothing
against her reputation), but every time it hears
of a wreck it thinks of the craft the dockman
describes over his beer —the one that potters
about small, obscure, out-of-the-way ports, and
“ takes a lunar ” at Providence.

Coming hard upon the abbreviated skirts
of “The Circus Girl,” at Sydney Royal, “San
Toy” shines with a serene, Star-of-Venus-
like refulgence by sheer force of compari-
son. Its dialogue, which, under other cir-
cumstances, might seem thin, looks brilliant,
and the music appears glittersome and as
catchy as a lawyer-vine. It takes more than
gorgeous scenery and a wealth of dresses to make
a successful musical oomedy, and there is in “San
Toy ” some of the genuine flavor, which makes it
all the more regrettable that the pretty, Oriental
extravaganza is this Saturday to give way to the
“ Runaway Girl,” with its unwise school-girl and
its weird assortment of Dagoes. ‘ * San Toy ” suf-
fers from some of the complaints that are common
to this class of work. Encores are allowed to run
the piece on till long after 11 p.m. Encores should
be sternly suppressed. Few musical comedies
manage to make their funny man a coherent part
of the show, and why Li was created, except to
get into ridiculous positions, is a mystery. He
gives one the idea of being dragged into the show
by the pig-tail, though it is here admitted that
Fred Graham drags him in as artistically as
possible. The Mandarin Yen How and President
of the Board of Ceremonies Sing Hi (otherwise
Bantock and Sweeney) are perfect models of dig-
nity, This writer met many mandarins in the
opium-scented town of Lawrence (M.L.), but none
of them were so dignified as Yen How and Sing
Hi. The Confucian repose of the two literati
is a beautiful example to the Emperor of China
(who is Arthur Lissant when off duty) but
that potentate doesn’t live up to the imperial
calm of his rank. Miss Musgrove, as San Toy,
justifies a lot of things, but the Emperor requires
a lot of justification. To Miss Lillian Digges, as
maid Dudley, belongs the distinction of being
the prettiest girl on the stage. This remark
doesn’t disparage the other girls either.
