The most recent upheaval of the a layers’
Club was in Pinero’s “Lady Bountiful,” at Syd-
ney Lyceum last Saturday afternoon. That strange
play, grim as a Norse saga and melodramatic
as a penny dreadful, is enough to test the genius
of the finest actors; but the Players managed to
make some of its force hit the audience and drag
heavily on the heart-strings. The sublime selfish-
ness of Roderic Heron was well represented by
Horace de Lissa, and writer heard three ladies
of mature judgment declare sotto voce that
they wouldn’t marry de Lissa: he acted the
part too well to make a good husband. As
the young man of heroic honor, Philip Lytton
was as good as when he acted the part of a poor
parson in the Theatre Royal months ago; and
that was about his high-water mark. Miss Brent
and Camilla Brent were moderately well done by
Misses Holroyd and Ahearn ; but the successful
lady of the afternoon was Miss Oriel Hotson as
Margaret Veale. A humorous side item of the
show was the enthusiastic way the audience ap-
plauded Margaret Veale when she declared that
the common people could never be anything else
than common, despite education, musical accom-
plishments, and all the rest. That applause was
significant of many things.
* * * *
This paper, which has known Frank
Thornton for a good many years as a comedian
whose business it has been to make people forget
their creditors, now begins to suspect that he has
partly repented. In the fact that, at the end of
a season of fun, at Sydney Criterion, he put on
“ Facing the Music,” it scents a subtle suggestion
to Owe’stralia that that’s just what it will have to
do—it may forget the creditor for a while, but
he’ll have to be faced, after all. So this paper
recommends Thornton’s concluding piece at the
Cri. as a Great Moral Lesson. It also recommends
it as an utterly ridiculous farce-play with a
genuine laugh every two minutes. Besides the
subtle suggestion beforementioned, it has a more
obvious moral, which is that if you’re going to
live in populous residential chambers when you’re
married, it’s a mistake to be born with a common
name like Smith or Brown. “Facing the Music”
is preceded by a one-act piece, “ The Clown,”
which is as bad as the other is good, or more so.
Nevertheless Carden Wilson drags into it some
clever imitations of well-known mummers. After
Friday of this week Thornton will remove hi 3
goloshes and his other jokes to Adelaide, and
thence to the West.
* * » *■
Fitzgeralds’ Circus will pack up its bag of
lions, and put its performing whale away in its
box, and tie the box up with its serpent, and
leave for other scenes at the beginning of next week.
Meanwhile it is making a supreme effort to surpass
itself in these last few days, and the dog which
turns 20 somersaults in 20 seconds is growing giddy
in his determination to give the audience a good
twelve-penn’orth of inverted dog for its shilling.
Fitzgeralds’ on this last visit has come so near to
being the best circus Sydney has ever seen that it
deserves to carry good luck with it wherever it
goes. It struck Sydney at a time when the city
badly wanted cheering up, and the sight of its dog
upside down in the air has done something to
lighten the load. It reminded Sydney of George
Reid’s political somersaults, and though he
struggles over himself in a fat laborious way and
the dog doesn’t, the resemblance was sufficiently
close to recall old times.
* # # *
The gruesome Face which looks out of the
Window at Sydney Lyceum, and discharges from
its mouth an arrangement of sounds which freeze
the marrow of the onlookers, will utter its nine
piercing yells on Friday night for the last time.
“ Man’s Enemy,” which, if The Bulletin
remembers aright, is a base aspersion on liquor,
is to be revived on Saturday.
* * * *
“ Oh What a *Tight ” concludes at Sydney
Palace Theatre this week, and the Giddens co.
takes its fine talent for humor elsewhere. On
Saturday Miss Maggie Moore opens at the Palace
in ‘‘Struck Oil,” and this being the sort of
weather when any sort of combustible is wel-
come, it is hoped that she will strike the public
fancy also.
* * *■ *
If Sydney Dental Hospital Concert Commit-
tee could see its way to repeat the programme it
lavished on a half-filled hall last Saturday night
this writer thinks it would make something worth
giving to the charity. The night was wet, and the
fact that the programme would be of such excel-
lence had not sunk into the wooden head of the
public. A pupil of Mr. Burnes Walker,
Miss Zara Leston, sang Allitsen’s _ “ Song of
Thanksgiving ” —her first appearance in public—
and created an admiring stir. Miss C. Brandon-
Usher used her magic fingers on the Steinway to
Lisztian magic music. Mr. Weir’s “My Queen”
was warmly encored. Mr. Bertram Flohm, a
new and very harmonious-voiced reciter with an
uncommon repertoire, assisted. Mr. Rivers All-
Sress, Miss Maud Dalrymple, a kiddy named
lay Atkins, and Messrs. Bentley, T. Rainford,
Elijah Thompson, Truman, Atkins, and Mrs.
Woodforde also used their undoubted talents in
the cause of a worthy charity. And the show
they gave was worthy of the charity ; the trouble
is that the audience wasn’t worthy of the show.
# # * *
“ Bikerena, a new American idea, proved
to be a good and exciting “turn” at Sydney
Tivoli last week. Champion cyclists Gudgeon
and Megson, pedalling furiously on real bicycles,
gave impetus to clock-work machinery which
recorded the strength of their respective efforts.
The machine gives all the effect and excitement of
a real race, responding to every spurt of the riders,
and the “ barracking ” is as lively as at a cycle
meeting. There ought, however, to be some
netting protection to the stage, if the force of
its rider’s efforts carried one of the cycles off the
machine, there would be one or two sudden deaths
in the orchestra or stalls. An attractive addition
to the Tivoli programme is Miss Bella Perman in
an agile dancing turn.
* * * *
“ Secret Service,” which brought Frawley
and co. to notice, at Her Majesty’s (Melb.), last
Saturday, has the name of its American author,
Gillette, stamped upon every act. More melo-
dramatic and domestic than a play by Sardou, it
is constructed after the method of that French
master-craftsman. There are no cheap sensation
scenes, where the painter and mechanist supply
the success. The strength of the finest situations
lies in their silent effects, whilst the weakness
of the drama is in its comedy episodes and its
occasional redundancy of talk. As a dialogue-
writer, Gillette is far inferior to Sardou, but he
equals the other dramatist in his art of working
up the interest to points of legitimate intensity.
Three plays by Gillette have been staged in Mel-
bourne. The first was “ Held by the Enemy,” a
story of the American Civil War, produced some
16 years ago, at the Bijou, by Nelson Decker;
afterwards revived, at the Opera House,
by William Rignold ; and later, in a
rough but creditable style, by a luckless
management at the old Alexandra. The next
Gillette work to strike Australia was that in-
genious shocker, “ Sherlock Holmes.” Both plays
arc recalled to mind by “ Secret Service ” (which
came between them in America and England),
seeing that its hero is a military Sherlock Holmes
who gets held by the enemy. That is to say, he
loves a female member of the enemy, and acts
accordingly. The author of those three plays
seems to dwell affectionately on the idea of a
calm, brave, resourceful man being unable to
attempt to escape from a girl.
* * * *
It is the time of Civil War in America.
The end thereof is very near. An officer in the U. S.
secret service has bluffed himself into the Con-
federate city of Richmond with a view to making
things easy for the besiegers. His object is to send
a bogus message from the War Dept, telegraph
office, and he gets control of the office through
the daughter of a Confederate general, in whose
house his false, deceitful tongue and restful de-
meanor have made him welcome. But the
chivalrous Sherlock Holmes-like nature of the hero
will not permit him to take professional advantage
of his opportunity. The girl has stood by him
after she knew him to be a spy. He abandons
his designs on Richmond City in consideration of
her kindness, which doubtful virtue proves its
own reward in the 4th act. Not being a human
miracle, same as Sherlock, this audacious schemer
gets cornered at last, and when he is about to be
shot, a benevolent Brigadier-General comes along
and announces that the bogus telegram was not
dispatched. So the nice girl squeezes her hero’s
hand, and her relatives suddenly approve of
him for a reason which is too long to mention, and
the unfortunate villain of the piece is scoffed off
the premises. The villain’s sin is not very
apparent. He also loved the girl, and because she
didn’t reciprocate, he pitted his own secret service
talents against his rival’s, and outwitted him, and
wished to shoot him when he got a genuine patri-
otic excuse for doing so. The drama teaches that
successful patriotism as regards War affairs may
be contemptible villainy, whereas another fellow’s
dereliction from duty may be a noble thing, pro-
vided there’s a lady in the case. Women make a
lot of difference in this world.
* * * •
If Gillette’s clever drama fails to prosper
at Her Majesty’s, the Melbourne public must
take the blame for their general indisposition to
give their minds to tragic stories, and their
pretty general lack of interest in America’s great
Civil War. There is nothing to draw the multi-
tude except the play as a play. The two scene-
sets are merely passable ; the average quality of
the performance seems just) good enough.
Daniel Frawley treats the leading part as
Cuyler Hastings would treat it, and both of them,
presumably, have formed their quiet method after
the “original” author-actor. Frawley has a
fleshy, Napoleonic face, and a George Rignold
head of hair. Not a magnetic actor, he gets
through this part with ease, and manages to hold
the house in his silent periods, even when a period
lasts more than five minutes. Miss Van Buren,
who plays the heroine, is a buxom charmer, with
an hour-glass figure ; too frilly and affected at
the outset, more convincing when her troubles
come, Plimmer is satisfactory as the “villain.
He has to droop his head and bear a lot of abuse all
the time, and in the 3rd act he is unjustly sat
upon by a fool of an officer; nevertheless he
succeeds in appearing as though his misfortunes
serve him right. Other serious parts fall to Miss
Susie Vaughan, Stanford, and Wallace Shaw, who
wears a black complexion and talks an incompre-
hensible niggah dialect. An old niggah woman,
played by Miss Hill, is an irrelevant comic char-
acter. Also there is much frivolity by Miss Eva
Dennison, as a 16-year-old damsel from across the
street —the counterpart of a character in “Held
by the Enemy.” Miss Dennison plays the part
winsomely in an exaggerated style, whilst Hugh
Ward tries his best to realise the character of a
boy enslaved by the too-coquettish
hoyden. Ward at the age of 19£ is a severe strain
on the imagination of his old admirers.
* * * *
In an ordinary way, one performance of
farcical comedy sandwiched between two massive
pieces of Shakspeare would probably be a financial
failureandanunpardonableoffence. “OnChange,”
staged at Melb. Princess’s for Saturday night
only, was something out of the ordinary way, and
it scored a particular success. Robt. Court-
neidge, having been brilliantly starred (or
rather cometed) in the advt., was expected
to shine. He shone. He careered across
the firmament for one night only, and was
called upon for a speech at the finish. In days of
yore Courtneidge seemed to get inside the
character of the old Scotch professor without
stretching it to its fullest dimensions, but on
Saturday he made it a great character part. And
there were other reasons why, in the language of
the sometimes truthful daily pressmen, “the fall
of the curtain was the signal for an ovation.”
With the exception of Courtneidge and Mrs.
G. B. Lewis, all the principal performers
were members of the imported Shakspeare co.
This lapse into light up-to-date comedy brought
out the evidence of their versatility. Miss Nora
Kerin, Lathbury, Norman Partridge, Miss
Cynthia Brooke, Croker King, and other people
who helped to play “The Dream” on Friday,
showed talent of quite another sort on Satur-
day. Also Miss Margaret Cellier, who didn’t
get a part in “The Dream,” made a graceful
first appearance in this bright and fleeting show.
When Courtneidge’s speech had been rounded off
by three volleys of “ hooray ” the stage was
cleared for further rehearsal of “As You Like It.”
* * * *
Spectacularly considered the “ As You
Like It ” production at Melbourne Princess’s is a
larger triumph of artistic endeavor than even the
“Dream.” Perhaps Shakspeare’s ill-built poem
hardly deserved such beautiful scenery and so
much pains. The question is not worth discussion ;
so long as there is a demand for gorgeous panto-
mimesand expensive melodramas a manager may be
excused for giving Shakspeare a handsome show.
This production is a feast of stage pictures. The
painting, lighting, and groundwork effects are
fine. The well-dressed figures take their proper
places. Every detail has been thought out. Two
of the finest pictures are the scene of the wrestling
match, in the first act, and the last scene of all,
where Rosalind speaks her epilogue in the fore-
ground of a breezy landscape, which is a master-
piece. Between these, there are five forest scenes,
bolder in design than any previous Forest of Arden
staged in Australia. The acting in this case, as
in the poetic parts of the “Dream,” is specially
likeable. It seems true to the spirit of the play.
The characters, with the sad exception of a
young Messenger who descends upon the final act
like a ton of modern bricks, speak their lines
poetically. Norman Partridge, if sometimes too
assertive in Orlando’s elocution, is always manly
and spirited. Miss Nora Kerin is the prettiest and
youngest we have seen in the part of Rosalind.
She plays it on the sprightly side. The surface
effects of her performance are charming and
would be still more so if she moderated her smile
and walked without bending her knees. Miss
Dora Rignold supplies a grave and sympathetic
Celia, who makes a well-defined contrast to Rosa-
lind. The accent of Miss Maud Milton’s Audrey
is more rustic than her appearance. Touchstone,
who clowns in the flesh of W. H. Denny, is
efficient but not brilliant. B. H. Everard scores
heavily as Jaques in an orthodox style. The
banished Duke (A Croker King) makes a favorable
impression. Small parts of Corin and Phebe are
brightly and youthfully played, and the scenes
between old Adam and Orlando are excellent.
* * * *
Mark Hambourg’s genius had so impressed
Melbourne that the second Saturday night con-
cert drew a great multitude to the Town Hall.
Some of the people massed themselves at the
back of the platform, and what with the vast
attendance and the immense enthusiasm the
Town Hall didn’t know itself for the same place
where Gerardy froze a year ago. A pianist who
can crowd that building in defiance of the winter
is a magician, with a divine right to look as extra-
ordinary as he pleases. Owing to this full recog-
nition of Mark Hambourg’s merits there are to be
two more Melbourne concerts ere he sails for
Maoriland. On Friday afternoon he will invite
another warm greeting, and on Saturday night
the final rush for seats will eventuate.
** * *
At the Royal these are the last nights of
“ The Price of Peace,” unless something happens
to give a new topical interest to Bland Holt’s Par
liamentary drama. What it needs just now is
the kind assistance of a Premier in Australia or
abroad, who would drop dead whilst struggling to
hurl invectives at his honorable friend on the
front Opposition bench. The robbery of a cipher
telegram from Joe Chamberlain to Irvine would
also be a timely advt. for Holt’s show, and the
incident of a society bride-elect refusing to devour
her prey after bringing him to the altar would
keep the “Price of Peace ” on the bill for another
three months. If none of those things occur
Premier Walter Baker will cast off his benevolent
white whiskers, and the discursive drama of
British political life will be succeeded next week
by “The Breaking of the Drought.”

John F. Sheridan, the widow of the late
O’Brien, returns to Melbourne next Saturday
(6th) after a considerable absence. He has hung
out his respected banner at the Bijou, and the
entertainment he will submit (D.Y.) to an anxious
public is “The Lady Slavey,” which musical
comedy has not yet appealed to Melbourne’s
tenderest feelings.

Napoleon Boffard, the ornate but busi-
nesslike tenor, is fixing up a Living-Picture Con-
cert to happen at Melb. Exhibition Building on
Monday, Bth June, which is the date of a certain
little assistant-potentate’s first appearance in this
breathing world. Mrs. Palmer, Mdme. Keller-
mann, Hattenback, Rebottaro, and among others
Madame Leon Caron will implore Providence to
bless the Prince of Wales. Also a chorus, and ze
Great Napoleon himself will join in this beautiful
demonstration of French, German, and Italian
loyalty to the personage aforesaid.

** * *
At Adelaide Royal the tuneful nonsense
of “The Chinese Honeymoon,” which runs on
aimlessly till the clock strikes eleven, and then
shuts up without coming to any definite end,
seemed to hit the local taste exactly. May
Beatty had six or eight recalls every evening in
“I want to be a Lydy,” and the four statuesque
bridesmaids consistently disregarded Lauri’s gag
that they ought to go and put some Clothes on.
The Chinese princess with the American accent
was also popular. “The Thirty Thieves” is
bringing the Musgrove season to an end. The
Tivoli is to re-open shortly with Willoughby and
Co. in farcical comedy.
Among some bits of parchment, centuries
old, held by a Christchurch (M.L.) resident, is a
rule “limiting servants’ and artisans’ wages,
under an Act of Elizabeth,” and written about
1620. It is signed by certain justices and a clerk
of the peace.
“ In China, the Russians and the French
carried off the palm for outrages on women
during the original march, and subsequently the
Germans similarly distinguished themselves . . .
In a village through which a body of Germans
had just passed, three girls were taken by our
troops out of a well into which they had been
thrown before the Germans left.”— George Lynch ,
in his newly published ‘ * Impressions of a War
Correspondent
N.S.W. Works Minister Owe’Sullivan
was wanted as a witness in the Melbourne libel-
case, Ogier v. Norton. Owing to pressure of poli-
tical business he wrote to the Judge re a post-
ponement, and the language of the Judge
about the impropriety of this easy and
economical proceeding was withering in its
severity. Owe’Sullivan should have instructed
his solicitor in Sydney, to instruct his agent
in Melbourne, to hire a barrister, with a long
skirt and a lot of hair that didn’t belong to
him on his head, to explain the matter to the
Judge—and then it would probably have been ex-
plained much less lucidly than by letter.
From a Spectator report on the Navuloa
Mission Station, Fiji:—
The subjects taught are all those that will be useful to
students as teachers afterwards. First and foremost has
been put down the life of Christ, of which as complete a
study as is possible in the first year is made. Then comes
theology, which always secures their deepest interest,
though the Fijian has no doubts. And who would disturb
him in his happy faith ? Biblical criticism, of the Old
and New Testaments, finds of necessity a place on the
curriculum, while sermon criticism and homiletics have
a place also. Questions of genealogy are the most
frequent of all questions that are put to the missionary,
for to a Fijian pedigree is important. What was the
name of Lot’s wife or Peter’s mother-in-law are typical
questions, and to one who has no distinct taste or ability
for climbing genealogical trees such questions are often
puzzling and unwelcome. The climax was reached when
the writer was one day asked the depth of the well into
which Joseph was cast.
Why do people not go to church ! Pro-
bably one reason is the Rev. Archdeacon Bartlett,
of Goulburn, who recently denounced the Vic-
torian railway men from the pulpit as being at
the beck and call of a few demagogues who had
but their own ends to serve.” Presumably, the
holy gentleman never looked into the merits of
the case, and just cheered with the rich mans
party, regardless whether, in doing so, he was
helping to circulate a disgraceful slander or not.
The leaders of the Victorian strikers sacrificed
their positions and their pensions on a point o
conscience. Would Archdeacon Bartlett or his
kind do that much? As to why people do no
go to church, it is, at least in part, because there
are honest and upright men in the commum y
who don’t want to be found dead in the same
paddock with that servile, time-serving, t ut ;
hunting institution.

* * * *
The balance-sheet of the Great Southern Con-
sols G.M. Co. (Vic.) for the last half-year shows that the
underground electric haulage plant, which was supplied
and erected about seven months ago by the Austral Otis
Engineering Coy., South Melbourne, sole representatives
of Ganz and Co. Ltd., Budapest, Hungary, has effected a
saving of 60 per cent, in manual labor required to operate
the mine. This excellent result, and the great satisfac-
tion the plant has given, has influenced the directors of
the Berry Consols Extended Company in placing an order
for a similar plant with the same firm. The plant will
consist of a generating station, three underground loco-
motives, and a complete electric lighting installation.
This order furnishes further evidence of the high esteem
in which Ganz’s apparatus is held in Australia.*
If you want to borrow money, go to N.S.W.
Mont de Piete Co., Ltd., 74 Castlereagh-street, 74.
Eustace Bennett, Manager. *
* * * *
Mr. Mephan Ferguson, of Melbourne, reports
that there has been a great improvement in the general
outlook of the engineering business since the new year
started, and that his pipe making works in Melbourne
and Perth are working full-handed. Amongst the orders
now being executed at Footscray works is one for 7 miles
10 chains of 18in. diameter light steel spiral-riveted pipes
for the Victorian water supply ; and at Perth works a
contract is in progress for over 6 miles of 10}in. diameter
spiral-riveted pipes, flanged joints, for the Greenbushes
Tin-Mining Syndicate. About two miles of these pipes
are to stand a test pressure of 8001 b. to the square inch.*

merely surface shores), situated in any part of
Australasia requiring further capital for development and

entertained.*

The WILD CAT
COLUMN
What is referred to as “a serious difficulty”
faced the Federal Govt., the other day ; and the Federal
Govt, dodged it promptly. Under the Public Service Act
Federal officers must assure their lives with an “approved”
society ; and the alleged difficulty was to discriminate
between the rotten societies and the sound ones. The
Govt., however, would not discriminate ; it decided to
“ approve ” everything that might come along—from the
best of them down to the Eternal Improvident office that
was started last week or so by some other office's office-
boy. Probably the fact that the Eternal Improvident is
“approved by the Governor-General” won’t get for it a
single civil service policy ; in fact, it won’t get ap-
proved for that purpose at all What it will want
the approval for is to be able to flaunt the fact in
its advts., and use the approval as incontest-
able proof of its soundness when it calls on Mrs.
Rafferty to canvass for a four-penoe-a-week policy.
When the Eternal Improvident fades out of sight Mrs.
Rafferty will probably denounce the Governor-General as
having been in the swindle with the ex-office boy ; and
won’t be so very far wrong. This paper, in fact, has
hopes of seeing the lady waving a heavy fist on the steps
of Govt. House and demanding her money back. When
that happens the Govt, will stop the farce of “ approv-
ing” insurance societies promiscuously. Or if it isn’t
game to approve of some and pass no opinion whatever
about the others, and if Parliament doesn’t think that so
much gameness is called for in a Government, then it
might very well alter the law—it might command the
officers to assure their lives, and stop short at that.
* * * *
Manager Teece, of the A.M.P., writes that it
isn’t possible to get from the society’s report information
as to the average extent of the policies issued last year
in each State. What, however, was shown in this
column last week, was the nett addition made to each
register during the year. Thus the number of policies in
N.S.W., on Dec. 31, 1901, was 45,164 ; on Dec. 31, 1902,
47,796—an increase of 2632. The sum assured on Dec. 31,
1901, was £14,420,956; on Dec. 31, 1902, £14,868,585, or
£447,629 more ; so that each added policy in this State
represented an average addition of £l7O to the gross
amount assured. It isn’t always easy to explain matters
in two or three words at the head of a column ; yet, in
view of the preceding columns, it seemed that the head-
ing “ average amount of new policies ” would be under-
standable. Of course, the total number of policies issued
was much greater—as a matter of fact, while
the nett addition was as shown, 7508, the
gross was 15,591, 8083 policies becoming void. The
distribution of these 15,591 policies it is impossible to
ascertain ; all that can be said about them is that the
average sum assured under them was £237. In the list
of the society’s assets given last week £371,865 was
credited to “ office furniture ” instead of office premises.

Australian society’s securities become less valuable, instead
of becoming more valuable as securities in a healthy
country should.

A weak feature of the Colonial Mutual is its

The correct course, however, would be to calculate th
interest earnings, not upon the assurance fund alone, but
upon the whole funds—investment fluctuation fund
included. The securities are supposed to be worth
the full amount for which they are included as
assets ; in other words, the society is supposed to
have securities of the full value set out and the
amount to the credit of the fluctuation fund into the bar-
gain. And reckoning on that basis the interest return
was last year at the rate of £3 19s. 4d., against £3 17s. lid.
for 1901, £3 17s. lid. for 1900, £3 19s. 4d. for 1899, and
£4 Is. Bd. for 1898. Why the earnings are so low isn’t
apparent, either, from the accounts as published. The
holding of public securities (which might be expected to
return alow rate of interest) isn’t by any means heavy,
although it is satisfactory, from one point of view, to see
that the amount is steadily increasing ; in 1896 Govt, and
other debentures represented 6J per cent, of the assets,
whereas on Dec. 31 last they represented nearly 12J per
cent. Probably the explanation, or a good part of it, of
the low return lies in the item “freehold and leasehold
property,” which now represents just under 35 per cent.
of the assets. This item is still increasing, but not now
rapidly. In view, however, of the very large amount
(£677,376) even now represented, and in view of the
large capacity of this class of security for fluctuating, it
is by no means certain that the amount now to the credit
of the investment fluctuation account (£85,967) is sufficient
for all purposes.

At Bendigo (Vic.) last week, in an insolvent
estate, a grocer’s retail wine and spirit license was sold
by auction. There are only four such in Bendigo, and
they cannot be increased, though they may be trans-
ferred. The license, after brisk competition, was knocked
down for £6lO. The annual fee is £35. The license
m question is possibly a remnant of Michie’s Single
Bottle Act.
* * *
N.S.W. Full Court has sensibly decided that
the protection given to life insurance policies, to a
certain amount, against the bankruptcy laws, does not
apply to a sum borrowed by a bankrupt on the security
of his policy. The protection is given with the idea of
encouraging the citizen to leave some provision for his
wife and children in case of his death ; any act (such as
borrowing on the “surrender value”) which endangers
the continuance of the policy is against the obvious
intention of the law.

Three historic N.S.W. homesteads offered for

failed to elicit even a nod ; James Milson’s Elamang was
£l5OO short of the reserve ; and Charles Cowper’s old
home, Wivenhoe, is still unsold.
* * *
The expensive Melbourne libel case of Staines
v. Coombs and Co. (the commercial inquiry agents)
resulted in a strangely illogical judgment by Justice
Hood. Plaintiff Staines, manager of a Melbourne tea-
room, represented the dual grievance of himself and the
lady proprietor of the business. Last year they heard of
damaging rumors in circulation re the financial standing
of the tea-rooms, and they were evidently justified in
believing that the ill report emanated, or got a good deal
of its repetition, from the local private-inquiry firms.
In order to settle this question Staines employed another
man to go in the disguise of an ordinary bona-fide
subscriber and procure a report from Coombs and Co. as
to the “credit” of the tea-rooms. The report stated
that Staines was addicted to betting at pony meetings,
and his alleged reckless expenses were recklessly
defrayed by the lady who employed him. Judge
Hood found that this was an untrue report, but
inasmuch as Coombs and Co. had issued it in good
faith to a person who was conspiring with
plaintiff to get the untruth for libel action pur-
poses, the plaintiff was not entitled to damages. His
Honor dismissed the case without costs to either side.
The air of Melbourne is thick with inaccurate rumors of
the sort this particular tea-shop suffered from, and it
seems, from Judge Hood’s showing, that the law will
give no satisfaction to a victim who traces an injurious
rumor to an authoritative source. A commercial inquiry
agency is a privileged libeller because it is authoritative,
and lives by supplying statements concerning other
people’s business. The libelled trader has no chance of
getting the libel delivered, save by false pretences,
nevertheless the false pretences invalidate the libelled
person’s claim for damages, or even costs, against the
libeller, provided that defendant has, in good faith,
repeated only the hearsay of the man in the street.

S. Australia, which hasn’t of late been having
any drought to speak of—on the contrary, has had a
better time than usual—has now imported a shipment of
wheat from South America. Our “natural industries”
are booming all right!
♦
Victoria’s railway muddle grows worse every
day that Bent rages at the helm of affairs. Though half
the passenger trains have been abolished, leaving so
many more locomotives available for the goods business,
the goods traffic has practically reached a deadlock. At
Williamstown ships are regularly kept waiting four or
five days for the lack of trucks to receive their cargoes,
and this state of things gets worse instead of better with
time.

Apparently the variation in the income-tax accounts for
the fact that 20 per cent, means more one year than it
does the next. Since 1897 the profits have fallen from
the stupendous £213,547 of 1898 to a loss of £46,936 last
year—while just as steadily the disbursements to share-
holders have increased from £14,501 to over £35,000.
Even now, with an absolute loss on record, the ex-
travagant dividend of 20 per cent, is maintained. The
explanation offered for this is that the losses were really
the result of unremunerative business accepted prior to
1902. At the best this means that there wasn’t
any real profit for 1901, and that the dividend
in that year was not earned ; and the only amends
the co. could make for that was to either “pass”
a dividend for 1902, when the miscalculation was
discovered, or at any rate reduce it; and it did neither.
In 1898 the co. plunged into a business as to which there
was no experience to serve as a guide. This business was
created by the British Employers’ Liability Act of 1898.
With practically no experience to go upon, most of the
old conservative companies stood off it ; the Ocean, a
comparatively' new concern, and struggling for a front
place, took the new business freely. It says now that it
really didn’t want it, but that it was forced into
taking it by the belief that otherwise the co.’s
chief agents would have transferred the whole
of their business to co.s that would take it. The premium
rates have now, however, been revised, with the result
that, after the struggle, the insurance in respect of over
20 millions sterling of wages has passsd to other co.s.
The gross income will, therefore, probably show a decline
in the future, but as this should Lie the result of the
weeding out of the most unremunerative business, pro-
fits may be expected to revive again. Notwithstanding
the year’s loss, the reserves, considering the co.’s age
and subscribed capital, are unusually large. Of these
reserves £361,788 is represented by British, colonial, and
foreign public securities, and most of the balance by
American and foreign railway bonds.
Broken Hill Prop, put up a record for the
last four weeks : it mined and milled 51,000 tons of ore.
This probably isn’t far from a world’s record for present
production, either. The Alaska Treadwell, the great
’Murkan producer, mills about 30,000 tons a month ; no
Rand mine quite reaches 25,000 tons.

The first step towards the completion of the
Great Oobar deal has been taken. The first deposit has
been paid, and it is stated that it will not be long before
the full amount is put up. The purchase price Of the
Great Oobar and allied properties is £1,150,000.
If underwriters part with this amount, the
chances are that the public will be asked to partici-
pate in a “ good thing ” at £2,500,000. A working capital
of £250,000 is talked of. Rumor has it that the trouble
with some dissatisfied shareholders has been overcome,
and that nothing now stands in the way of the comple-
tion of the deal barring the first essential—Bull's cash,
with which he is chary of parting. Sid Joseph is entitled
to all credit for working the deal to the present stage.
Only those who have been behind the scenes can have
any idea of the many difficulties which he had to en-
counter. There were complications within complications,
and every move seemed to suggest more law. Luck has
been with Joseph to some extent. He had the advantage
of a low copper market on which to buy the mine at this
end, and now has the pull of a good copper market to
assist him in selling it at the other end, in addition to
which there have been most important developments at
the mine.
* * * *
Outside the Westralian goldfields, the develop-
ment of the N.S.W. coke industry has been the most
striking thing in connection with Australian mining
during the.past ten years. The output in 1892 was 7899
tons. Since then :
1893 .. .. 17,858 tons aB9B .. .. 82,222 tons
1894 .. .. 34,458 1899 .. .. 96,530 „
1895 .. .. 27,630 „ 1900 .. ... 126,213 „
1896 .. .. 26,351 „ 1901 128,882 „
1897 .. .. 64,202 „ 1902 .. .. 126,872 „
Ten years ago N.S.W. coal was pronounced unfit for use
in smelting, and probably, as then produced, it was ;
little care had been taken to wash the dirt out, and the
product was heavy in ash. The present Under-Sec. for
Mines (then Govt. Geologist Pittman) did good work in
repeatedly drawing attention to the possibilities of the
industry. Even then, though, N.S.W., so used to
making for itself nothing which it could import, did
little in the matter; the big strides have been
mainly the result of Vic. enterprise, working through the
B.H. Prop., Mt. Lyell, and Sulphide cos. The slight
falling-off in quantity last year doesn’t mean that the
local article is losing ground ; on the contrary, a smaller
proportion than ever of foreign coke was used, and the
stuff will soon be out of the Australian market altogether.
The explanation is that the lower prices of metals have
blown out one or two of the smaller smelting plants,
while—more important still—the developments of the
past 12 months in smelting pyritic copper ores at Mt.
Lyell and in desulphurising Broken Hill silver-lead ores
have been extraordinary. A large part of Mt. Lyell ore
is now made to practically smelt itself ; only about a
tenth of the fuel formerly used is required. The sulphur
in the ore is made to serve as fuel. In connection with
silver-lead the process is different, but the principle is
the same; the ore, concentrate, or slimes are roasted
either in a desulphurising furnace or in an open kiln
before they are put into the smelter. Once set alight
the mineral “cooks” itself, so altering its character that
one smelting furnace now does the work that was
formerly done by two

In his annual report, the N.S.W. Under-Sec.
for Mines makes a slashing attack upon the prospecting
vote, and declares that it does the mining industry no
good. Yet, on top of that, and issued with it, is the
report of the mining inspector in the Oobar district; —
“ The Crowl Creek mine has continued to develop in a
most satisfactory manner. . . . The mine already pro-
vides employment for over 150 men. . . . The discovery
of this mine may also be ascribed to the prospecting vote,
as the first prospectors of this part, Shuttle and party,
received Govt, aid, and the discoverers of the Crowl
Creek were attracted by the small patches Shuttle and
party were finding. Where formerly there was no work,
150 men and their families are now supported.”
* * * *
Considering the all-round laxity of supervision
and the general disregard of regulations exposed by the
report of the Mt. Kembla commission, the surprising
thing is that the butcher’s bill in the N.S.W. coal industry
isn’t larger than it is. Last year it cost one human life
to win every 56,000 tons of coal ; but that was a gloomier
result than usual, in consequence of Mt. Kembla. For
2b years the average has been one victim for about every
165,000 tons. Even this, though, is a good deal less
satisfactory than the English experience ; a 20-years’
average there is about one corpse for every 191,000 tons.
Modern French and Belgian figures are ratherbetter, again,
than these. But, no matter what the supervision may
be, in the experience of most, and probably all, countries,
far more miners die from accident than any other class of
workmen. In England and Wales a small fraction over
every fifth person who is killed is a miner. After the
njiner, but a long way after, come fishermen, cab-drivers,
and painters. Yet, in spite of that, the fisherman’s is
nearly the most healthy of all occupations, being beaten
only by the parson’s, the farmer’s and (on English and
French experience) the grocer’s. Heaven only knows
why it favors the grocer like this, except it be that it is
the grocer’s sand that kills and that the grocer doesn’t
put in the sand for his own consumption.

The old Sydney Harbor Collieries, Md.
(London), is in process of disappearance ; and it is an-
nounced that a new co. with the same name and a
capital of £400,000 in £1 shares has been floated in
Sydney to take over the whole concern. What really has
happened is that a co. has been registered in Sydney,
G. R. Dibbs, Jas. Inglis, and five others having the
necessary one share each to secure registration, the old
co. taking the other 399,993 shares. The English co. had
issued 16,000 ordinary and 18,734 preference shares, £lO
each ; these shareholders will be entitled to take
up the new shares, with a liability of ss. each. The
issue, to the extent of 320,000 shares is guaranteed
by the New Century Trust for a trifle of about
£BOOO. The old co. had about £45,000 of deben-
tures out; the new co. has power to issue up to £130,000,
including the old £45,000. These debentures have a
currency of ten years from this date. The Birthday
shaft has been bricked to the bottom of the sump
2937 ft. —and a pair of parallel drives have been put into
the upper coal seam. The seam consists of an upper and
a lower band, divided by shale. The upper band, from
which, in the course of the driving, nearly 2500 tons of
first class mineral was won, improved from about 2ft. 4in.
to over 2ft. Bin. as the shaft was left, and there was also a
slight improvement in the lower band, which is, however,
under Ift. thick. The object of the reconstruction is to
sink the second shaft, with a view to reach the seam
where it is thicker,
* * * *
As to the profitableness of hauling from great
depths (the problem of most interest in connection with
the Sydney harbor colliery), the 1902 report of the big
’Murkan copper co., the Tamarack, comes opportunely.
It works five shafts —3408 ft., 4356 ft., 4892 ft., 4450 ft.,
4938 ft.—and last year made fair profit on ore that aver-
aged 20‘91b. refined copper per ton of rock hoisted, or,
as we should reckon it, on just less than 1 per cent. ore.
* * * *
Some of the virtuous utterances of the Mel-
bourne mining conference are calculated to make a cat
smile. At last week’s meeting, a letter from the Gipps-
land Mining Board was read. It condemned “ the system
of floating mining companies in England and elsewhere”
but it omitted to throw light on the location of “ else-
where,” which is presumably far beyond the Mount
Deddick radius. Then ex-Minister Burton complained that
“many ventures are put upon the market by mere
paper men, who care nothing for the real interests of
mining.” Where, O where, are the promoters who study
any real interests of mining outside of their own ven-
tures? All mines have the same title in the sight of
Heaven —“ The Number One Reward.”
* * * *
King Lansell, of Bendigo, is pulling round again,
so he may yet witness a cherished desire -the discovery
of payable gold at a depth of 4000 ft. It may not be long
before that is an accomplished fact, as the New Chum
Railway shaft is fast approaching that depth, and “ en-
couraging indications ” are being met with. Lansell has
never figured as a philanthropist in the ordinary sense,
but in his pluck and enterprise he has been a public
benefactor of high degree. But for his dogged determi-
nation in testing the deep ground, Bendigo would have
been almost a deserted field by now.

Madame Berry West (Vic.), in its half-year to
May 6, paid £4050 in divs. (4s. 6d. per share). The total
profit was £4662, equal to about 5 2-3dwt. of every loz. of

Details of Mount Lyell and North Lyell amal-
gamation scheme show that the Melbourne co. has much
the better hand in the deal. The days of Mt. Lyell as a
profit-earner, run on its own, were numbered. Ore has
been consistently decreasing in value, and the quantity
available is certainly measurable Coincident with
decrease in value there has been a decrease in work-
ing costs, thanks to Sticht’s magnificent management,
but they have been practically reduced to a minimum.
North Lyell leases undoubtedly contain the bonanza of
the field. It will now become the copper mine, ai d
Mount Lyell the flux mine. When the combined pro-
perties are in full work there will be an enormous output
of copper, and a drop of £lO per ton in value will not
materially affect profits, as it would do with'Monnt Lyell
solus. North Lyell people were probably eager to accept
any proposal which would extricate them from the quag-
mire of debts and difficulties into which they were
plunged through the mistakes of management and the
interference of theoretical “ experts.” The most gratify-
ing feature of the scheme is the decision to retain Sticht’s
services. He stands to-day without compeer in the Aus-
tralian mining world as an all-round administrator.

MERE MAN : “I will go to the club to-night! ”

M.M.: “ Well, the Seventh Day is the day of

Those “spontaneous offers of assistance
from loyal Victorian citizens” during the railway
strike boiled down, when it was all over, to fierce
demands for compensation— for, in many cases,
merely offering to do work the claimants were un-
fitted to perform. Many of the persons who said
they had come to Melbourne to offer their aid
were squared by bonuses of £lO and all expenses,
but some claims are still unsettled. One extra
loyal individual claims £lOO and expenses for
offering to act as fireman and finding his services
unnecessary ; Bent is understood to be lustily, but
so far unsuccessfully, bellowing at this grasping
patriot.
* * * •
The other day the Maorilander, who
already had the choice of 213 religions, looked
into the Christchurch police court and caught a
fleeting glimpse of the 214th. It only seemed
like an ordinary maintenance case till the lady
explained to the fatherly beak why she couldn’t
eat the same victuals and shelter under the same
mosquito-curtains as her husband. It was all,
she explained, a matter of affinity ; and her
religion, “Our Father’s Church,” went nap on
affinity every time. She didn’t mind cooking his
chops and sausages for him, she said ; but as her
husband wasn’t her affinity, the relationship
must stop dead at the frying-pan. The husband
didn’t embrace the idea with any enthusiasm ;
whereupon the wife explained that the proper
thing for him to do was to go out and see if he
couldn’t find an affinity of his own. But the plan
of having an affinity and a wife in the house didn’t
appeal to him either. He was a man of small
means, and both of the ladies would want three
meals a day, and there wasn’t any telling when
the affinity might change her mind and join the
wife, whereupon he would have to go out and look
for another affinity ; and it occurred to him that
with an affinity and an ex-affinity and a wife in a
small house there would probably be trouble. Up
to that point the fatherly bench seemed to have
a good deal of sympathy for the husband; but
when the latter proceeded to fire the wife out
(whether to make room for an affinity or not
didn’t transpire), the bench made the usual order
for £1 a week. So “Our Father’s Church”
scores, and its disciple is relieved of the task of
cooking the chops.
• * * *
A hapless deserted wife, at Port Melbourne,
recently, solved, in the simplest of all fashions,
the great problem of “How to live.” Like a
similarly-situated wife at Richmond (Vic.), she
had lately been refused State help for her large
family of small, deserted children, and, hopeless
and despairing of making a living for herself and
three little ones where, just now, it is hard enough
for a vigorous man to do it, she wrote the local
constable to see to her babies, and took her own
life. Then the State promptly took charge of the
orphans. The woman practically levelled a charge
of constructive murder at the soulless, miserly
Irvine Government, which lately desired magis-
trates to quit mothering so many children on the
State. She practically sacrificed herself to save
her children; but that sort of heroism is wasted
on the Icicle Government. It has as much sense
of shame and as much feeling as the cabbage
Bent grew in his better days.
• * • •
Balmain, a dirty, dishevelled Sydney
water-side suburb, with a corporation, 32,000
people, <£71,000 of debts, <£Booo of uncollected
rates, a park, and a corporation tip in the park
(almost every park has a corporation tip in it
somehow), has an epidemic of typhoid on hand.
Just at present its typhoid rate is nearly four
times the Sydney average, and the corporation
smiles a beaming smile on its tip and wonders
what on earth is the cause.

There is very little in Sydney Telegraph
nowadays except columns of tearful blather
because the State will no longer carry the cheeky
mendicant’s papers gratis on the railways.
♦ * • *
The past summer has been notable in
Melb. seaside suburbs for the vigorous manner in
which cleanliness has been suppressed by the law.
The early dirty Christians never attacked the
vice more bitterly. Small children of three
have been solemnly arrested and charged
with indecency for floundering in the
waves, and the grown man who dared
to bare his legs to paddle did so only
at the risk of losing his liberty.
The howl of the ancient virgins
who take objection to people
washing themselves is mostly
bogus, even from their own
standpoint, for the regu-
lar bathing places
around the coast are
so secluded that even
the casual visitor
stands very little chance
of being shocked by dis-
plays of manly form unless
she hunts for them. A Brighton con-
stable describes how he was once
approached by two gaunt elderly women who
claimed that he should tramp to Black Rock—three
miles distant—and arrest some men who were
bathing on the beach. “ But they’ll be dressed
by the time I get there,” he pleaded ; “ how will
Ibe able to tell them ? ” The foremost com-
plainant grew earnest. “ I saw their noses above
the water; I think they’re Jews,” she explained.
The dirty Gentile’s anxiety to run in the clean
Jew!
* * * *
A few days back the new-chum parson of
the fashionable Pitt-street (Sydney) Congrega-
tional pulpit wanted to know what the Labor
party, with all its boasted influence, had done to
improve the social condition of the people; and,
parson-like, he reckoned up the social condition
of the people by the number of its pubs. By
what the parson himself would certainly say was a
special dispensation of Providence, the Attorney-
General’s Department had in hand at that moment
a return dealing with this very subject; and this
return, published three days after the new-chum
had made his foolish remarks and asked his more
foolish question, show that, during the past 20
years, the number of pubs, in the Sydney metro-
politan district have been reduced from one for
every 293 persons to one for every 607 persons. It
seems now to be about up to the Rev. Pitt-street
to refer to himself as a meddlesome blunderer
who, in his blindness, was guilty of attacking the
very party which has been doing the thing that
he says wants doing most.
DOUBLY FALSE.
RABBIT: “’Ullo, how’d yer cop it, Dido?'
DIDO : “ Yer see der Turk wuz gain ’ with Robbo,
en’ she sez if I could lick 'im she'd walk wid
me again. Well, I did, ’en now she’s goin
wid Pickles, coz she can’t stand the smell of
the medicines on me ’ead.”
The 40 odd M.L. legislators, who have been
drifting about for a month in the South Seas,
reached Raratonga on April 24, and some news
about them has come to hand. About three days
out from Auckland they set up a concert com-
mittee with power to murder any music it laid its
hands on, a recreation committee, a cards com-
mittee, and probably a bob-in committee. Every
morning there was a large Sandow class, “ fol-
lowed by cold tub and ‘hosing down.’” “Some
of the members brought works appertaining to
the South Seas and files of Parliamentary papers
dealing with Island questions, and studied them
closely.” This heavy mental exercise enabled the
litterateurs to make a concise summary of their
surroundings when the Mapourika nosed her way
among the coral reefs, and they remarked : “ The
natural resources of these islands are capable of
immense development.” The excursion boat
seems to have had a mixed cargo of champagne
and Maoriland flags. If Mr. C. H. Mills
(Minister of Customs) sighted a native he set sail
after him, ran him down, and “presented him
with a Maoriland flag.” On April 27, during a
heavy gale, “there were several narrow escapes
from falling cocoanuts.” Most of the legislators
were hog-fat when last heard from.
• * * *
Melb. has an Infectious Diseases Hospital,
but it isn’t working because there is no money to
furnish or maintain it. In the meantime the
Children’s Hospital won’t take infectious cases, so
that all the accommodation for young (and poor)
sufferers from diphtheria in Melbourne is five cots.
Even with two or three kiddies to each bed (hideous
overcrowding), this only mops up about 300 cases
a year; the rest have to trust to luck in the slums.
* • * *
An article in Sydney E. News of May 23
on the strongly self-reliant qualities of the Aus-
tralian people is melancholy reading. The doctrine
that we are utterly helpless without foreign loans;
that we can t possibly be expected to pay enough
taxes to keep our accounts square, but must have
deficits and more loans as a matter of course; that
we can’t possibly, by reason of our natural help-
lessness, expect to manufacture our own goods
but must import; that we can’t expect to have
our own navy, being too helpless ; that every
Fat Man must lean on the Government while the
Government leans on Cohen—all this is taught the
Australian people by their papers to a degree un-
known in almost any other civilised country. And
yet there is hardly another country in the world
where the daily paper article and the political
speech about the wonderful self-reliance of the
people recurs so regularly.
The K.O. clan in N.S. W. is much troubled
over the question of the preference given to Hugh
Pollock, “called” in 1890, in his capacity of
Solicitor-General. The K.C.’s appear to be
making a mountain out of a mole-hill. When the
Solicitor-General was a politician —as were
Darvall, Lutwyche, E. Wise, Dailey, Hargrave,
Faucett, Isaacs, Josephson, Salomons, Windeyer,
Innes and Reid—the precedence was admitted and
all these men led in court. Pollock is purely an
official, but it is difficult to see how the fact of his
not being in politics affects his legal billet.
About a recent horror :
Melbourne’s newest sensation was in two sudden acts
On a Tuesday morning Mrs. Stott and her two babies of
3 and Xi years were found dead in their bed, killed with a
tomahawk. Mr. Stott, who had retired with his family
as usual the night before in the single room they rented
was missing. On Thursday evening—6o hours after the
murder—the driver of a Oollingwood train comine
through the Spring-street tunnel, heard a shot and felt a
slight bump. He reported the matter and started on the
return journey. At the first stopping-place (Jolimont)the
guard, noticing a continuous bumping underneath his van
investigated, and discovered the remains of the much-
wanted husband, Stott, entangled in the brake gear
The post-mortem showed the cause of death to be a
half-ounce of lead in the brain from the revolver subse-
quently picked up in the tunnel, the rest being simply an
extra precaution to make sure of death. Stott is said to
have been a reasonably kind and fond husband and
father, but he had had exceptionally hard luck. Coming
to Melbourne from Wood's Point, he got a week’s work— at
14s. a week and his tucker—and probably the hopeless-
ness of his outlook got on his nerves. Also his surround-
ings were calculated to drive any man mad. The family
lived in one room of a four-roomed house in a terrace—
really, a two-roomed house, with two small cupboards at
the back. The front view was a narrow street in
Prahran with similar dens opposite; the rear view
a yard as big as a table-napkin. In this four-
roomed mansion dwelt nine persons. Melbourne
promptly started, in its own quaint way, to assist
the police to find Stott, even if it made a stupendous ass
of itself in the process. Reports poured in wholesale as
soon as it was known Stott had “escaped.” One youth
saw him walking near the Yarra in deep thought, waving
his arms in an aimless manner. Two women over Toorak
way said they saw similar irregular proceedings about
the same time ; they even got a field glass to view the
details of the expected suicide, but before they could
focus it the man disappeared, and then a couple of arms
were extended from the river and a dark objcet was sesn
floating away. They identified this apparition as Stott. A
railway fireman, who knew Stott years ago, travelled to
St. Hilda with him fully six hours after he was alleged to
have floated himself down the Yarra. A Park
employee came along with minute details of an
unkempt individual who had left a bag of scones in his
care on the Tuesday and had promised to return for
them “ God knows when,” and whom he had accidental y
met next day. He then returned the scones, and was
informed the suspect had got a job at a certain factory.
Feeling sure he had got Stott, he followed his man for
several miles, sending cyclists, tram men and others for
the police, but when a constable finally arrived, lo! the
suspect had disappeared. This trail was strenuously
followed up till a thoughtful ’tec dropped in at the
factory the man had said he was working at, and
behold ! the man was there, and he wasn’t Stott at all;
moreover, he wasn’t at all like Stott. There were a lot
more identifications, including one of a suspect at Ballarat
who was actually run in and locked up for the night.
Altogether Melbourne got quite interested in Stott.
» * • *
Finding that supine State Parliaments
won’t take measures to prevent Australia’s Bung
adulterating Australia’s beer, the Australian
Government has moved in the matter. A pro-
clamation has been issued forbidding the importa-
tion of hop aromas, hop oil, hop extracts, hop
flavors and hop bouquets, whether in their own un-
disguised character, or compounded with other
materials used in brewing processes, or as ad-
ditions to beer. That’s pretty thorough, and if
strictly enforced will do a lot to prevent
Australia’s stomach being corroded before its
time. Australia would be greatly benefited if
similar prohibitions were extended to half-a-
hundred other trades.

MEDICAL EXAMINER : “ And at what age did
your grandfather die? ”
APPLICANT FOR ASSURANCE : “ One hundred
and five."
M.E.: “ And what was the cause of his death?"
A.F.A.: "Well, I suppose he thought it was a

A very gradual lady asked Judge
A’Beckett, of Victoria, for a decree nisi lately.
She was married away back in ’63, and her
husband, a market gardener, went to market one.
day in ’Bl and failed to return. She said he was
such an objectionable husband that she was rather.
glad of his disappearance, and omitted to make
any strenuous efforts for his recovery. She didn’t
want to marry again, but had decided to ask for
a divorce to make sure he wouldn’t turn up and
disturb her old age. She got her decree. The
next petitioner on the list said her 12 years of
married life had been highly unsatisfactory, and
in ’9B her husband also unaccountably disappeared.
He owned the amazing name of Patrick Parsons
Percy Persse, which, perhaps, accounted for his
eccentricities.
IN THE NIGHT-WATCHES.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Here in the soft luxurious warmth,
With wifely arms in the dark about me,
I, the faithless of soul, am pressed
To the trusting; heart that could never doubt me.
But, ah ! that other —
The love forbidden,
That Life concealed
And Death has hidden.
Against my forehead her low sighs stir,
Around my shoulders her curls entwine.
Like birds night-blinded her white hands creep
To nest content in the clasp of mine.
But, ah ! that other—
The fingers cold,
The warm fingers
Under the mould.
In murmurs drowsy with love and sleep
Is tenderly whispered a name—my own—
By a mouth of Springtime, a mouth as fair
As a crimson blossom from God fresh blown.
But, ah ! that other —
The ashen lips,
Where wet o’ the grave
For ever drips.
E., M.L.
TRUE CONSISTENCY.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
I often lie on the sunlit strand,
Stretch’d out at ease upon the sand,
Away from the city’s bustling mart,
From toil and toilers far apart,
With head supported on my hand.
And thus, while listening to the band—
Which comes, I think, from German-land—
With lazy limbs and happy heart,
I often lie.
I am, by trade, of legal brand,
And thus my very work is planned
The same soft pleasure to impart ;
For, practising my lawyer’s art—
As you, of course, will understand—
I often lie !
N.S.W.
S. ELLIOTT NAPIER.

“Grant Hervey ” : Melbourne girls must
be very languid if there be only two of them
(“F.C.,” B. 2/5/’O3) possessed of the ability to
step off a Bentopolis tram-car whilst same is
in not very violent motion. Melbourne cable-
trains run at a fixed speed of about seven miles an
hour (I nearly said “year”); “F.C.” says 12
miles, but writer doesn’t believe that anything
runs as fast as that in Yarratown, unless it be
Thomas Bent in pursuit of an engine-driver to cut
down his wages. It takes a real smart hand, how-
ever, whether male or female, to alight from a
Sydney electric - tram whilst same is “ all
out” on the down-grade. I knew one girl,
though, who regularly climbed down opposite her
shop-door, midway between King and Market
streets, with the same airy grace with which a
fly steps off an elephant. She always got off on
the wrong side. One day, she forgot the up-car
on the other track, and she died shortly after-
wards in Macquarie-st. hospital. She left no
successor in the business.
♦ * « *
“ Cheetah ” : Met an innocent old dame
the other week when journeying per Bent’s
rockibus-cum-puff-creaker to the city. She told
me that she hadn’t seen the metropolis since the
“happening” of her third kid—33 years ago!
I’m afraid I told the poor old soul a dreadful lot
of lies. I know for certain that I told her Bent
was a slender, kind-hearted man about 6ft. high.
When we reached Spencer-street I helped her out
with her carpet-bag (a treasure saved from her
last trip to the city, 33 years ago), and a candle-
box containing three chicken roosters, a present
for her sister’s children. She was being sent,
along with the rest of the starving stock, to
Gippsland, till things improved round the ’Maine,
she said (she hailed from Castlemaine), and was
bound for Wy Yung. Went to send a wire, and
when I got to Collins-street found her looking
very lonely. “I’ve been waiting,” she said, with
a quiver in her voice, “ for them to put the horses
in them carriages, but they whip off without
them!” She’d never seen cable trams before.
Then I took her in hand, and piloted her to the
Victoria Coffee Palace —carpet-bag, roosters and
all, for the night—and left her (4.30 p.m.) on the
steps ringing the bell!
• * * *
A single man who applied recently to a
Brisbane charity organisation for relief put his
case thus :
(1) He had never been employed by anyone for long.
(2) Whenever he got a job some one would libel him to
his employer. Result —the sack.
(3) A certain gentleman would be willing to give him
regular employment-only he was dead.
When one’s only hope of a regular job is in the
cemetery the case is a hard one.
The Que-en Victoria Memorial in Mel-
bourne is commissioned to James. White, the
Sydney sculptor, on a .£6OOO contract. The
artist’s original design, estimated to cost £BOOO,
will be carried out m cheaper material—copper
repousse instead of bronze for the figure, and
freestone instead of granite for the base. These
proposed economies are a painful reflection on
the sincerity of national “loyalty.” Bobbie
Burns, Scott, and William Wallace (especially
Wallace) have been dead a long time, yet there
is no difficulty in raising a couple of thousand
pounds for a statue to any one of those deceased
Scotsmen nowadays. Queen Victoria’s death
reduced the Victorian community to a state
of mourning some two years back, and a
memorial fund of nearly £6OOO soon resulted from
the appeal of an officially grief-stricken com-
mittee. But already the late monarch is as dead
as Queen Anne. Another .£2OOO would enable
the memorial sculptor to execute his design in
handsome style, yet “it is thought improbable
that the deficit could be raised by re-opening the
subscription list.”
* * • *
Lyttelton (M.L.) took its sinall-pox visit
very seriously. The State health officer rushed
down from Wellington by special steamer,
arrived about 4 a.m., and got the local paper to
run out a special with a vaccination proclama-
tion. The lumpers at Lyttelton, who had
handled cargo out of the Gracchus, were ordered
to be lymphed, and when they objected were
given the option of 21 days’ quarantine. So they
accepted, and got the sop of three-days’ extra
pay. Their clothes were taken away in a disin-
fected cart, and burned in the furnace at the
waterworks. Disinfecting parties raided the
houses of suspected “ contacts,” smoked out the
inhabitants, commandeered their suits and petti-
coats, and cremated them in the backyards.
Government replaced incinerated costumes at its
own cost. The Gracchus crew, including 72
Lascars, were carted to Quail Island in sterilised
boats. There they had to strip and stack their
garments at a given spot, and run in the nude to
a clothes heap about a quarter of a mile away.
And, after all, that is the best way to treat a
disease such as small-pox—seriously.

N.S. W. Presbyterian Assembly acts sanely
enough in protesting against the best billets in
its dour and dry old kirk going to imported
pastors. Some members want to carry a motion
making imported men do three years in the
country before they become eligible for city life.

In a case where a milkman was charged
with stealing a milk-can at Melbourne t’other day
a rather cool custom came to light. Accused
stated that one of his customers had forgotten to
leave out a can for his morning’s supply, and he
had taken the can from prosecutor’s verandah
rather than awaken the neglectful one and ask for
a vessel. He said that it was quite the recognised
custom for milk-carters to take a non-customer’s
can from the gate or verandah in such cases. His
employer confirmed this statement in all good
faith, and was almost doubled up with amazement
and wrath when an unsympathetic beak fined him
£1 for the theft. Apparently the milkman is
almost like an importer.
BY FAR THE QUICKEST.
THE QUICK ONE : “ How can I get into the
hospital, sir?”
POLICEMAN : “ Well, it all depends. If you want
to get there quick you'd better drop dead —
that's the only way.”
The Australian Tommy (permanent man)
gets three “meals” a day—one is a meal, the
other two are feeds of bread-and-scrape. Once,
after the “ A ” Battery had done something good
(on the Show Ground), a pleased officer ordered
the battery steak for breakfast! On the first
steak morning, the “cook’s mate,” carrying a
glad, proud expression and steak, had just entered
the messroom when 20 hungry men sprang at him.
He dropped the dish and ran, and the men had a
riot on the floor. Penalty—Bread-and-scrape for
ever.

Rushcufcter’s Bay Park, Sydney, a portion
of which is now to be
built on, earned an
evil reputation at
the very opening
of the convict era.
The water of the
bay at that time
covered most of
the park, the
place being a
mere swamp
with a fringe
of rushes.
Phillip sent
a party of
convicts to f
cut rushes
there one
day, and
they perpe-
trated acrime '
on five abo-
riginal
women which
ranks as one of
the prize horrors of Austra-
lian history. The next rush-
cutting party, consisting ol
six convicts and two soldiers,
was ambushed and slaugh-
tered by the natives. Then
more soldiers were sent, and the aboriginals
round the bay were butchered promiscuously.
The bay ought to be a ghost-haunted spot; an
awful amount of tragedy was condensed there-
abouts.

Arrangements have been made at the new
W.A. Law Courts, now about to be opened, for
preserving the dignity of the “Inner ’’ Bar. The
K.C.’s are to have a separate table from which to
address the Court, and a separate robing room.
The Westralian Inner Bar consists of only four
K.C.’s. Premier James, absorbed with politics,
is at present almost out of practice ; Pennyfather,
who got a sort of ex-officio K.C.-ship, as Attorney-
General, now practises in Fremantle; Burt .is n
England ; and Haynes, K.C., will thus have f e
lordly privilege of the special table and room, f r
the present, to himself.
Works-and-Railway Minis-
ter Leahy went nosing round
Dunwich, the old-age asylum
of Queensland, the other day, in
the hope of saving a
threepenny bit
here and nine-
pence there
for his dis-
tressed
State. He
ran up
again s t
many pe-
culiar old
fogies.
One stout,
old matron
assured him that she
hadn’t felt so hungry
since the time o.f pan 1
O’C onnell. She
weighed about seven-
teen stone. Euchre,
for matches (only one
box per week being doled
to each man),
is a great pas-
time among
the men. Some
gamble for
higher stakes,
however. A favorite gam-
ble in the higher circle
is called “over the hill”
(to the boneyard). The
names of a dozen pros-
pective corpses are
shuffled in a hat; the
lucky drawer of the
first to get “over the
hill” scoops the pool.
A few there are who,
now and again, invest
in Tatt’s. sweeps, and
a shocked Minister of
the Crown professes sur-
prise at the possibility of a ticket
getting into the State under any cir-
cumstances. It has happened that prizes
have gone to Dunwich upon occasions,
whereupon, a tender, watchful Government
promptly “boned” long arrears for board and
lodging. In two prize-winning instances, how-
ever, the winner got away promptly off the
mark, and showed the island retreat a pair of
swift, if somewhat ancient, heels.

Stated by a small girl in the Adelaide
police-court that her mother “ always kept
bricks under the bed.’’ When the P.M. re-
covered his breath, he asked : “What for? ’ and
learned that they were “to settle the Old Buffer
with.” And as the lady was shown to have had “five
sixpenn’orths ” during the day, and to have taken
up a pickaxe to her husband when he came home,
the Old Buffer’s severe use of an axe-handle upon
her was held by the Court to be a natural and
proper proceeding. This is a pretty cold world
for the Old Buffer sometimes.
THE HIGHER CRITICISM.
“ Entirely self taught .”
“ How fortunate —she has nobody to
blame but herself.”
Dannevirke (M.L.) is a parson-ridden
town. A parson is mayor, a parson sits on the
bench and fines the small local drunk, a parson
sits on the Education Board that bosses the local
school, and two parsons are on the licensing bench
to help keep an eye on the drink problem. And
even with all these parsons the millenium hasn’t
arrived in Dannevirke yet, though it is liable to
be seen coming through the bush at any moment.

When we see such an ominous sign :
That peril’s ahead, there isn’t much doubt;
Perhaps it’s a slip on the line.
We feel just the same when we get a bad cough
Our safety we haste to secure,
By buying the stuff that will soon shake it off, .

OVER THE SLIPRAILS.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
no.
Over the sliprails we leaned that night
Watching the sunset pass ;
Over our heads the star shines bright,
Under our feet the grass.
Down by the pool where waters cool
Mirrored the spangled sky,
Over the sliprails I kissed her then,
Kissed her and said good-bye.
Over the sliprails I lean to-night
Watching the sunset pass ;
By the waters deep she lies asleep,
Over her head the grass.
Over her head the grasses spread
Tangled and rank and dense ;
Ah ! she may not know where she lies below,
Down by the stockyard fence.
KATHLEEN WALKER.
