A WOMAN’S LETTER.
Sydney, June 2, 1903.
Mr Dear Arini,—
There was a small attendance of women at
A.J.C. Prince of Wales Birthday meetings on
Saturday and Monday. Frocks were mostly of
the hairy zibelines and shaggy skye-terrier tweeds.
Which reminds me of a Great Smoke jokelet.
Motorists there affect overcoats which were first
the property of some animal with hair like Ham-
bourg’s—a Shetland foal, a Persian lamb, or a bear.
A coster, after watching an automobilist getting
into a huge, hairy garment, exclaimed, admiringly,
“ It’s lovely, matey ! Cut us off a pup.” One of
our best known good-dressers wore a big, flat hat
laden with a harvest of scarlet hawthorn berries.
Another good dresser, but a woman of no impor-
tance, carried a facsimile of it, and it is safe to
predict that one at least of those women cast off
her bird-food trimming for some other form of
decoration soon after she got home.
I want to start an association for the Advance-
ment of Assassination, or I desire to be, for one
hour, that Beast in Revelations who had seven
heads and 10 horns. My first midnight dagger,
or my first rush, like a numerous rhinoceros, would
be directed against Mr. O’Sullivan—for one reason
only. He has set his myrmidons on to cut down the
scented scrub, the odorous shrubs, the sapling
gums and native fuchsias that make beautiful the
Spit Road and Middle Harbor foreshores where
the punt punts. The Minister for Works —there’s
no doubt about his thriftiness —will use the cleared
spaces for advertising hoardings to help bring in
revenue. Tenor Edward Lloyd, for instance, said
the Spit Road, with its fine sea view and
its curly descent to Middle Harbor, was
worth the journey to Sydney. Every week thou-
sands of sightseers go down there and pay the
tram and punt fares. But who will do the journey
for pleasure when the route is disfigured by
advertising hoardings ! Why can’t the Minister
spare the scrub and put a tax on feet instead ? A
decimal of a halfpenny on feet —both feet, all four
feet —would bring in millions.
From the time he returns from his cruise, State
Ex. Rawson and wife are booked for engagements
right into September. On the 3rd and 4th of that
month the grounds of Cranbrook will be given
over to the District Nursing Association to run a
charity fete, or some such jamboree.
As to the Canadian actress now visiting us—in
the multitude of (rumored) suitors there is safety.
You hear remarks like this : “ She’s engaged to a
man with a lovely motor-car ! ” “No, it’s not to
that one ; it’s to a man with a little black
moustache.”
Concerning that Sydney small boy who watched
his mate drowning, then walked off and said
nothing to anyone of the tragedy; Solomon left
it on record that there were three things which
were “too wonderful for him ; yea, four, which he
knew not.” Then he propounds his puzzles, which
are not worth guessing. If his “ wonder ” had
gone to five things, and the fifth had been the mind
of a child, it would be worth more consideration
than all the other four together, “He just
splashed about for a bit, singing out,” said the
dead boy’s mate; “then sank.” The boy, when
confronted with the police, said he confided the
story to his mother and that she threatened him
with a beating if he told anyone. The mother
denied all knowledge of it, and those who have
kept any register of the workings of their own
childish minds will believe her. Fear—an obscure
dread of being considered accessory to the boy’s
death and therefore deserving of punishment—
kept the lad silent to the person who might inflict
the punishment. But if he had met another small
boy, a friend of his and of the drowned lad,
he would almost to a certainty have confided the
secret to him. It was too weighty to carry alone
for long. There must have been a keen struggle
between the boy’s physical fear and his natural
childish desire to be the centre of a dramatic
situation. The biographer of the mind of a child
has yet to come.
In a recent visit to a kindergarten, Lady Tenny-
son referred approvingly to the fact that caning
and other forms of physical punishment have no
place in the Froebel kindergarten system. This is
cheerful reading ; we are getting away from the
corrosive belief that the more you wallop your
son the better you work out his salvation.
“The superior quarrelsomeness of women.” An
English Judge used the phrase when commenting
on the number of trumpery dressmaking disputes
brought before him. It was a most unusual thing,
he said, for him to have cases between tailors and
their customers, and he complimented the male
person on his inferior quarrelsomeness. A com-
mentator suggests as a possible explanation the
superior skill of the tailor to that of the dress-
maker. A curious little fact which somewhat
bears out the Judge’s suggestion is the number of
compulsory apologies that appear in the daily
(Sydney) press. Almost without exception women
are concerned : “I, Mrs. Smith, apologise to Miss
Brown for the things I said about her to Mrs.
Jones, the same being without foundation.” Pro-
bably men would settle the slander with their
fists. Women use the tonguwand the “Beak.”
In “Are You a Mason” and “ O, What a
Night! ” (the latter now in its last days at Sydney
Palace) we have two plays in which the central
idea is the deception by their husbands of suspi-
cious, nagging wives. Audiences go into spasms
of laughter at the make shifts, the stratagems
and the cowardice of the male in his efforts to
outwit his worse half. As to the husband being
master in his own house, in comedy that is but a
phrase, with moths, not truth, in it. To see
someone good-naturedly fooled you should see
“ Oh, What a Night! ” Miss Munro’s Xantip-
Seism in her version of the devoted thorn-in-the-
esh wife is so realistic, that one finds oneself
wondering if the old Hebrew who sought one
woman in a thousand and didn’t find her had a
harem of such wives as Mrs. Paillard. As in the
“ Mason,” MissMollison fills in “ What a Night ”
a pretty, yet unimportant role. In appearance
Miss M. reminds me of Julie le Breton, the heroine
of Mrs. Ward’s “ Lady Rose’s Daughter.” Mrs.
Ward constantly reminds you of the incomparable
Julie’s thinness—her “miraculous slenderness.”
She seems to insist upon it as one of the rarest of
charms when associated with grace, and in the
illustration of the story, Julie’s white attenuated
arms and the delicate bones of her chest and neck
are as much in evidence as Miss Mollison’s when
she wears, in the “Night,” an evening dress of
pale-green spindrifty fabric, almost dropping off
her ethereal shoulders.
The four school girls in the “Night,” with
their blue blouses, dark skirts, sailor-hats, satchels
and floating hair, are real chocolate creams. The
tallest of the four is Ethel Gunn, a Melbourne
girl.
Sydney National Gallery trustees hung a new
picture last week. It is one of Alfred East’s
serene, poetic oils, the “Valley of the Lam-
bourne.” A large canvas, soft blue sky with
white clouds, deep blue hills, great trees to left
and right of the sluggish river, a red farm house,
a Riverina-looking gate, and white ducks, not
swans, paddling in the deeply-shadowed water.
The piece has an early-summer afternoon air of
drowsy calm. East was the first English painter
to go to Japan and make extensive studies of the
Jap. national life and landscape.
There have been some inquiries as to the iden-
tity of the Mr. Foster of Sydney who has just sold
areputed Gainsborough in London for 900 guineas.
Trustees of the N.G. say that they had heard, on
good authority, that there was such a picture in
Sydney, but that they had never seen it or its
owner. It is stranger than fiction the way a few
valuable pictures or engravings find their way to
Australia. A well-known Sydney man had re-
cently sent him from Melbourne by one of his agents
a box of “old paper pictures,” bought for a few
shillings in a local auction mart from amongst the
effects of a deceased missionary—a very old man
who had missioned and died on one of the South
Sea Islands. Well, “ the paper pictures ” (there
are about three hundred of them) turn out to be
valuable engravings illustrating the engraver’s art
from the earliest times till now, and covering a
period of four or five centuries. One is by Lucas
van Leyden, and is dated 1494.
“Elamang,” the Milson’s Point estate of the
late James Milson, was auctioned last Saturday.
Fifteen acres of valuable site. It fetched from £6
to £l2 15s. a foot frontage. But the big stone
house was passed at a reserve of £7500. Curiously
enough, an evening paper of the same date as
that of the land sale, in mentioning old time
Sydney memories, chronicles a yacht race of 50
years ago. with James Milson, jun., as umpire.
The “ junior ’’ is identical with the handsome old
man who died lately, aged over 80.
A new disease has fallen upon the life of man,
but only a few will be afflicted with it. 1.5.0. it
is named, and a batch of gentlemen in all States
of the Commonwealth are the first sufferers.
Someone who isn’t tired will soon give it a mean-
ing other than Imperial Service Order. In
S’Africa during the war the C.l.V.’s were known
contemptuously as the “ Can I Ventures.”
A certain Sydney. hostess is qualifying for a
padded cell, so .mad is she at the action of the
young gentleman who quitted her large and
valuable dinner-party at the finger-bowl period in
favor of a Golden Gate contest between the
Terrible Turk and the Speckled Gosling. She
has been trying to induce other hostesses to
boycott him. Very likely, indeed ! Why, he
owns a motor-car.
Such a flax-on-fire state of excitement over an
incident in the honeymoon of a diamond-dusted
Australian bride and groom. Groom jumped or
fell out of an upper storey window what time the
graveyards yawn. Dense shrubs saved him from
death or broken bones ; as it was, he was severely
scratched. His friends say that he is a sleep-
walker.
St. Vincent’s Hospital ball occurs on Tuesday,
the 9th. It promises to be as brilliant as a
meteor. Hon. secs., Mrs. Tom Dalton and Mrs.
O’Gorman-Hughes.
One of the worthiest charities Sydney possesses,
the Montefiore Home for Aged and Infirm Poor,
will help its funds with a ball on June 11; hon.
secs., Mesdames Phillipstein and Wilson.
Melbourne J. Macnamara, whose sudden death
on his way to London last week shocked his many
friends, started practice a quarter of a century
ago at Deniliquin. In 1883 he opened in Sydney,
and in the intervening 20 years got together a fine
practice. He kept away from politics, and left
public questions severely alone; he was tactful
and full of common sense, and the result was
financial success. In nearly all the big trans-
actions of Sydney during the last ten years Mr.
Macnamara had a finger, and he and" his wife
figured at all prominent social functions. Outside
his profession Macnamara found amusement on
the turf, and what he did not know about horse-
racing would hardly be worth troubling about.
One of our society women who has a Napoleonic
talent for making use of other people and their
chattels, came away from a friend’s afternoon
at-home lately, and calmly stepped into a private
carriage which was waiting outside the gate.
Coachman and footman looked at her in amaze-
ment, never having seen her visiting at their
missus’ place, but she drew the rug over her ample
knees and ordered them to drive her to , as
their mistress would not be out for another half-
hour. They did so, but the carriage was scarcely
out of sight when its owner required it. Her
indignation was of the speechless kind, for she
had only a how-d’ye-doing acquaintance with the
carriage-snapper.
Writes my Melbourne correspondent:—
“ The Cliveden widow, ’tis said, laughs at the
pity lavished on her newly-married daughter by
some of the latter’s dearest friends on the score
of the bride’s probable ‘wiping-out’ when she
comes to spread her plumes (regarded so reveren-
tially here) on the same terrace as the dazzlers of
Russian diplomatic circles. Fears on the new
Mrs. Lindsay’s behalf do seem rather groundless.
Not to speak of her substantial private income,
the Australienne is equipped for the dress-fray
with nearly two thousand pounds’ worth of ex-royal
lace, willed to her by her late father. Her bride-
groom’s people armed her with a tiara of diamonds
and sapphires, necklet of diamonds, and betrothal
ring of ‘ ancestral brilliants,’ while her husband
contributed pearls, and her own mother more
diamonds. A group of Australian kinsfolk
donated cheques, to be spent in impressive furs
when the recipient gets to Europe.
“The adoption of babies by fashionable and
naturally unencumbered dames has proved in
many cases more than a nine days wonder. One
western adopter, who brought her borrowed infant
from the Big Smoke, boasts that she has now spent
£3OO on its swaddling clothes. Friends help to
pamper the child who can only, so far, turn wide
blinking eyes on the corals and the golden baubles
that glitter in its crib and go-cart. It is an
endless source of joy to the sporting dame, and
her dogs are dismissed from heel when nurse and
the London baby come into sight. In another
case, a practical dame is going to bring up the
child by a patent system which is guaranteed to
turn out a perfect article from all points of view.
Poor woman ! Poor infant !
“ An old lady describes our late Jap visitors as
* rather coarse. ’ She had asked several of them,
so she said, how they liked the English style of
evening dress for ladies. In each case the reply
was something like ‘ltch-kuma.’ That is Jap
for ‘very nice,’ I’m told ; but the ancient dame
said the offenders put such stress on ‘ Itch ’
that she was driven to interpret their meaning as
* ticklesome ’ —which would have been blasphemy,
of course, in connection with the shoulders of
women old enough to be the culprits’ grand-
mothers.
“The smart world attended at Her Majesty’s
Salon on Tuesday noon. Miss Van Buren, whose
great round eyes had been gazing solemnly at us
from choice posters all the week, was billed as
guest, and she guested alluringly—in black. The
Melbourne womankind, with its commissariat
department out of the general drawing, was
perturbed at this gathering to see the beauty’s
abrupt bust and twenty-two inch waist. The
smartest snorted, and scorned the idea of any-
thing but the ‘ straight front.’ With the saucy
Van Buren face there was no quarrel. It is too
much one of pretty appeal and a retrousse charm
—a charm that should make her Madam Sans-
Gene role irresistible. The fine nose has an
uplifting, right at the tip. The mouth is soft.
The chin is pert. A good type of imperfect
beauty.
“Another of the co., Miss Denison, is of the
Ruby Madden type of chubby beauty, and her
up-to-date style included a show of much soft
throat, a little chest, and a cavalier feather that
plunged mid-way from the back of the flat hat-
rim. A stout male comedian, with a highly-
polished and rather pinkish pate, was the laugh-
maker of the day. The Misses Young and Carrie
Moore made a pair of richly upholstered host-
esses, Carrie being quite graceful in black
shimmering raiment, while Florence was struc-
tural in a profusion of fine clothes.
“Melbourne’s gilded youth jests in the Ally
Sloper fashion. When half-a dozen of the species
had pressed Carrie Moore in vain to give just one
more tootle, one of the number urged : ‘ Come
along—you can only clear the room.’ And back
came the hot answer, with a head toss : ‘ Thanks,
I’ll not try.’ Strange how the little stars, who
ought to be sated with the flattery of immature
moths, hotly resent a mothly joke at their
expense. Aide Kilpatrick, off duty, frisked
quite recklessly. Plump Guy Madden beat all
his leaner friends easily in persiflage with stage
girls.
“ A remarkable thing about that theatrical
‘At Home’ was the complexion competition.
And in this the society girls carried the palm.
From the loftiest stage-star to the obscurest, the
make-up was deplorable. Some had a complete
cream mash, inches thick; others had a high
cheek bloom that Nature had never planted in
such a spot. A few sported a general pinkish
coating that pointed the finger of reproach at the
chrome-colored neck. None of the Society girls
made such garish mistakes. They all showed
beautifully - balanced complexions—ivory skin,
faint cheek flush, soft eye-glow, defined brow-arch.
Over the dainty work an embellishing veil was
drawn, and Nature itself would be deceived at
the borrowed bloom. One smart dame, Mrs.
Ernest Raleigh, who wears her pallor as Provi-
dence sent it, stood apart from all these tinted
Venuses, and yet looked attractive.
“ The Sultan of Johore lent his dusky presence
to the circle of the ‘ Secret Service ’ show on
Saturday night. With him was his white bride
and an Indian brother. The Sultan’s golden
smiles were “ off ” this night. The audience pre-
sented glasses, but the cave of Aladdin remained
closed. Those advertised molar gems have
annoyed his chicory nibs, and he no longer make*
them the beacons of sociability.
“ At that ‘ Secret Service ’ first night, the great
feature of a large, flash audience was the skv
scraper height of the thatches. Down in the
stalls, the Misses Landale (with an aide in towl
soared hairily with their extension frames, until
their two tall hair-buildings made one dizzy to
view. Several of the same smart clan wore their
stuffed thatches at an aggressive altitude And
from these hard, stiff, false-looking structures one
turned with joy to look on the sweet ringletted
charmers moving through the ‘Secret Service’
scenes. Miss Van Buren wore a pair of finger
curls on each side of her neck, the rest being
bound in a ribbon at the back of the head. This
style matched admirably her Sweet Simplicity
tones and French-Canadian charm. Miss Denison
too, broke up the line of ample chest with
two graceful ingenue ringlets; and a tall, fair
inaudible beauty, who occurred too seldom, like-
wise sported the Romney curl.
“ Leon Caron came m for some farewelling favors
on that Friday night. Florence Young threw
him a rose with deadly aim—it hit the baton-
leader on the first vest button. He was equal to
the occasion and chivalrously pressed the scented
shot to his lips. Then Carrie Moore pelted a
handful of her presentation blooms, and George
Lauri finished the deal with a blow of a bouquet.
“There is plenty of talent among the Jap
middies. Several have sketch-books furnished to
date. One good-looking engineer man has a
charming series of painted views, from a sunrise
in Australian seas to pier-scenes, garden-glimpses,
and 20 color schemes translated to the sketch
E ages during the western trip round. ‘I regret,’
e said, using his English carefully, ‘I cannot
make picturesof Melbourne, where the people are so
kind. Itisacuriousthingllook everywheretomake
a memory-picture of the bay, I cannot. Only in
the Botanical Gardens I see such a pleasant sight
that I have in my heart how to make one picture,
and I will call it, like you poet,
‘The Lady of the Lake.’ You like my idea—is it
not ? Then I shall take much trouble to make it
very, very nice.’ This same Jap sings, dapces,
lectures, and is the poet of the Hashidate.
“At the Wednesday Hambourg matinee, when
a choice audience lent ear to one of the most mag-
netic of all the performances, a stout party was
anxiously guiding Marshall Hall to a coign of
vantage. He lifted his heavy brows in a half-
questioning, half-amused appeal. ‘Why this
trouble?’" *1 want to find a view for the playing.’
He broke out into a huge laugh. ‘Bless you! I
don’t want to see his hands —I want to hear him
play.’ And down he plumped, with his harassed
host, on the nearest perch. But the majority of
the Hambourg devotees showed, at all the con-
certs, an insane desire to pick the seats in view of
the acrobatic hands. I only know one pure wor-
shipper who used to listen with eyes closed, giving
the whole mind to the melodies. The flying
fingers are really a distraction.
“Courtneidge’s one-night resurrection as the
Scotch Professor in ‘On Change’ drew a cordial
crowd. Old admirers came to sample anew the
qualities of their former favorite, while younger
play-goers were interested in the personality of
the manager, who has steered to artistic and
financial success ‘ Midsummer Night’s Dream.’
Mr. Courtneidge, who has not acted these seven
years (since he became manager of the Princess
Theatre, Manchester), seemed nervous enough,
but did not allow his misgivings to dilute the racy
‘ reek ’of his auld Scotch drama. The book-
worm |Dominie (who, when a blatant broker
boasted that he himself would write a book
when he found time, exclaimed in horror:
‘ ‘ Man, ye’re becomin’ blasphemous !”) was
ably supported on Saturday by Nora Kerin
and Cynthia Brooke as giddy, girly wearers
of divers delightful frocks. Miss Cellier, of the
composer’s clan, made her first Melbourne ap-
Searance as a tall, dark stage daughter. Mrs. G.
i. W. Lewis, as usual, made the boards seem
homely and cosy.
“In the d.c. the Cliveden widow headed a
family party, and in her all-white toilette could
only be distinguished from her youngest daughter,
so far as youthfulness of get-up went, by the fact
that Missie’s hair is still hanging down her back.
Mrs. Rupert Carrington sported an impressive
black cloak with chic triple collars of point
lace, gold gimp and black ruching. Barrister
Coldham shared the manager’s box with Mrs.
Courtneidge, her bright, little daughter, and tall,
picturesque Miss Rignold, of the dusky hair and
splendid eyes. Miss Milton, who did Helena in
‘The Dream,’and is regarded by many theatre-
goers as the crack actress of the company, looked
a sensible, blonde lady in sequined wrap of
emerald green and black velvet bow in her
smoothly-coiled fair hair. Her escort was Alfred
Bucklaw, of the Wilson Barrett profile, who, in
‘The Dream,’ took the part of Duke Theseus.
Bottom annexed most notice in the d.c. He’s a
stout, spectacled, reddish comedian—a born
comedian, so far as looks go, with a figure that
looks stiff and stocky with strength.
Yours affectionately,
AKENEHI.

feeble to air and purify the blood—too soft and sticky to fight off deadly lung disease.

lead straight to hopeless decline and a consumptive’s grave.

PERSONAL ITEMS.
The estate of James Milson, of Elamang,
North Shore, paid £15,634 as probate duty.
Rev. F. W. Macdonald, ex-president of
English Wesleyans, now on his way to Australia
to represent that body in connection with Metho-
dist centenary celebrations, is Blood-bard Kip-
ling’s uncle.
Still attending daily ar his place of busi-
ness, in Adelaide, auctioneer-brewer F. J. Botting,
now 84 years of age. He erected the first wharf
at Port A., and was a director of the first S.A.
building society.
One of the quaintest results of the Yic.
railway strike is the decision of the local Dublin
graduates to have Irvine made an LL.D. “in
recognition of his services.” The idea of an Irish
University honoring a man for coercion is magni-
ficent.
Old Rabbi Davis, a venerable-looking gen-
tleman of 75, who has expounded the law and the
prophets for half-a-century, retired from the
service of the Great Synagogue of Sydney a few
days ago. He has been an eloquent preacher in
Sydney for 41 years.
Four Queensland MinisteTs, Cribb, Leahy,
Philp and Gray (M.L.C.) are registered as share-
holders in the new Brisbane Daily Mail —about
to be launched under the pilotage of Editor
Buzacott. The Courier folks are trying to look
as pleasant as they can.
Gov. Rawson is the first Yice-regal
person to visit Norfolk Island since Hercules
Robinson called in there in ’74. On that occasion
Robinson gained great popularity by reducing the
ruinous fine the men had been called upon to pay
for parentage without marriage.
Solicitor Melbourne J. Macnamara, who
died on the voyage to England, was son of the
Sydney merchant in whose house John Mitchel,
the irreconcilable Irish rebel, found shelter, when
in Sydney, after escaping from Van Diemen’s
Land. Mr. M. J. Macnamara was born in 1853.
The late James Dick, Australian mine-
owner and Glasgow leather-merchant, cut up a
food slice of his million among his employees.
lis managers and clerks got from £3OO to £5OOO
each, his cook £2OOO, every female worker who
had been 30 years in his factory <£3oo, and others
on a scale to correspond. He gave the men who
had been managing his Australian mining inte-
rests the option, in most cases, to purchase them
at a very low price.
THE HUNT COURTEOUS.
“ Well! why do you come home at this time of
the morning? ”
“B’cause ’shtime ’ry ryspechtable poshon washin-
bed!”
A ruffian who received, a long sentence
last week in Brisbane for housebreaking and
stealing, stated proudly that he was a returned
contingenter, and carried a decoration. Strange—
a man gets five years in Queensland for the very
same sort of thing that got him a decoration in
Africa.
N.S.W. Premier See, who has had the
courage to wrest from the daily newspapers the
huge State subsidies they enjoyed so long, is him-
self the largest shareholder in one daily paper
and a considerable shareholder in another, there-
fore he evidently put the public interest before
personal considerations.
Deceased, Mrs. John McPhillamy, of
Mount Tamar, rear Bathurst (N.S.W.), aged 70
years. She was a daughter of the house of
uargin, pioneers of the West with the McPhill-
amys, and was the mother of Mrs. C. E. Pilcher.
Her husband represented West Macquarie in the
reformed Parliament of 1858.
Alleged Freetrader H. J. Saunders, of
Perth, has been appointed by the W. A. Protec-
tionist Premier to the vacancy in the Senate
caused by the retirement of Senator Ewing.
Saunders is generally understood, however, to
have sworn off the fiscal issue till next election, he
being merely a stopgap person.
Democrat Trenwith came well out of the
Vic. strike trouble. After a 30-mile drive from
his Mallee home at Waitchie, and a longer ride on
a railway tricycle, during which he managed to
fall off and damage his back, he reached the
House in time to speak forcibly and vote against
the villainous Strike Suppression Bill.
After all, The Bulletin finds, the parsons
were not so unanimously on the Pontius Pilate
side of the Victorian railway strike issue ; many
of them—a conspicuous example, just brought to
notice, is Rev. W. S. Rollings, of Sale (Vic.)—
spoke out bravely and honestly, but the press, the
lying press, suppressed their speeches.
Mogg, one of the candidates for the late
Capt. Staughton’s seat in Vic. Assembly,
announces himself as a staunch supporter of the

sent “ high ” rates of income-tax, and Q . strong

Obit., at Fern Hill, Canterbury, Richard
Smith, late of Sergeant’s Point, Little River,
Braid wood, aged allegedly 107 years. No public
character, whose birth-date can be verified, ever
lives to that age. Among all the sovereigns of
Europe for the last 800 years, among presidents,
peers, people whose lives are insured and so on,
the centenarian is as rare as the dodo.
F. B. Suttor, newly appointed President
of N.S.W. Legislative Council, was, in the 1891
election, returned to Parliament as a Labor can-
didate ! That was the time when Labor looked
like sweeping the polls, and an over-enterprising
election secretary at Bathurst took credit for his
smartness- in securing Suttor’s nomination as a
Labor candidate. The Labor brand was after-
wards repudiated on both sides.

Once more the rumor is being circulated
that S.A. Judge Boucaut, who is 72 years old,
and has held office for a quarter-century, intends
to retire from the Supreme Court bench, but from
the best possible authority comes an emphatio
denial. One S.A. Judge (Bundey) has sat for
over 18 years, and he will not be entitled to a
pension until 20 years are up. Otherwise, he would
step down, for he is always ill.
From John ’Aynes’ Newsletter, pub-
lished by John ’Aynes’ son, about John ’Aynes’
brother :
In a Westralian court, Haynes, K.C., asked a witness
“ Who killed the sheep ? ” “ Oh, ask the sheep,” inter-
jected Mr. Harney, his legal opponent. “ Very well,”
responded Mr. Haynes, who seems to have the ready wit
of the family , and looking squarely at his legal opponent
—“ Who killed the sheep ? ”
And the modesty of the ’Aynes’ family, too !
Augustine Stow, who died at Adelaide
last week, aged 70, was at the time of his death
Registrar of Probates, Commissioner of Inland
Revenue, Public Trustee, Curator of Convicts’
Estates, Deputy Registrar of Customs, and
Chief Clerk of the Supreme Court. He had
also been M.L.A. for three years, M.L.C. for
three years, Chief Secretary (under Premier
Strangways) for 18 days in 1870, the son of a
parson, and the brother of a Judge. Altogether
he had been a great many things—most of them
things with very little remuneration attached.
Nobody’s reputation was more enhanced
by Vic. railway men’s strike than that of Frank
Anstey, Labor M.P. for East
Bourke Boroughs. Unlike
Dr. Maloney, he didn’t stone-
wall Irvine’s Bill; he analysed
it—and that was the cruellest
thing anybody could do to it.
His speech then got very close
to oratory. In some respects
he showed to even better ad-
vantage at the meeting wl ere
the news of the surrender was
given to the men. White
and quivering with emot.on,
he sprang to the platfoim
when a hostile demonstra-
tion against the two leaders
had begun. He frankly
declared that he believed
they had made a mistake;
but with a fine burst of rugged
eloquence he pleaded with
them not to be disloyal to
their leaders. Clean-shaven,
under-sized, a seaman by
trade, Anstey is still well
under 40; with brains ap-
parently as well as a tongue,
he seems marked out to take
a leading place in a party not
too conspicuously well - led
hitherto. He has the further
distinction of having, at the
last elections, scored the greatest
number of votes polled for any
candidate in Vic. His score was 3465.
“ J.S.” meets an old friend :
Met a man just now who thankfully stated that he
had got a Government billet at several shillings a day. A
few years ago he was fairly well-to-do, and purchased
from a well-known politician a bit of land on which to
build a home. When everything was completed and he
had parted with his cash, he found that his new property
belonged to the Grown, and that the vending politician
had not the remotest claim thereto. He was promptly
ejected. He sought the advice of a better-known politi-
cian—a legal one. Politician No. 2 advised costly civil
proceedings against No. 1. After very long litigation,
which exhausted him financially, he got a verdict, but
never managed to collect the money. Politician No. 2
really didn’t know what to do next for his client, beyond
presenting him with a bill for more than twice the sum
originally in dispute. Then plaintiff went bankrupt, and
now in the interval of earning a few shillings a day he
occasionally visits the grave of one of the politicians
above-mentioned —the other is, unfortunately, still alive
—to read the beautiful epitaph.

F. E. H. W. Krichauff (almost 80 years of
age now) is still to be seen in Adelaide. He was
a member of S. A.’s first State Parliament and 30
years ago was a Minister for 18 solid days under
Premier Strangways. S.A. is full of men who
were once Ministers for a week or a month. Two
notable samples were Henry Gawler and Matthew
Moorliouse who climbed to the dizzy eminence of
“hoffis,” lasted just nine days, and never came
back.
It isn’t very surprising that the Vic.
Opposition can’t be bothered with serious political
business. Its alleged leader, Peacock, besides
being Grand Panjandrum of the Freemasons, is
manager of no less than ten Vic. mining cos., be-
sides other prospecting concerns. He only gives
the tip of the tail of his mind to public affairs,
and it isn’t a very strong mind at best. Peacock
has brains without grit. In the days when his
brains were visible Turner and Isaacs supplied
the grit.
George Allen Mansfield, architect, of
Sydney, in an address before the Institute of
Architects, recently said : “I never expect to see
any Federal capital built in my time.” Mr.
Mansfield is getting well on to being an octo-
genarian. He is the son of the Rev. Ralph
Mansfield, who arrived in Sydney in 1820, and
preached in Sydney, Parramatta, Hobart, and
Windsor until 1828. In 1829 he became editor of
the Sydney Gazette, the first newspaper pub-
lished in the State. In 1831 he conducted the
Colonist, in the absence of Dr. Lang ; and from
1841 until his death in 1880 he wrote for the
S. M. Herald, a journal which always had a han-
kering after clerical editors. Rev. Mr. Mansfield
founded the Australian Gaslight Co. in 1836, and
continued its secretary until a year before his
death, when old age obliged him to retire.
“ J.F.D.” writes :
Grantham, lately ousted from his aldermanic seat in
Hurstville (N.S.W.) Council, has had an unlucky hunt
after laurels. He unsuccessfully contested East Sydney
in ’9O in the Labor interest. Stood again against Barton,
who came for re-election on being appointed Attorney-
General, again defeated. Tried for municipal honors,
and was defeated twice at Hurstville. Then he stood
against ex-Ma.vor Sproule, and at last got in. But to
make his election as sure as possible he cast his own vote,
with the result that the wily Sproule ousted him from
his seat on the grounds that he entered the polling-booth
while the voting was in progress.
“ H.V.” writes :
The Hon. (or moderately Hon.) Thomas Bent, until the
effects of office-holding got him, always boasted of being,
as he termed it, a working man. In the 70’s, Tommy
bragged, in front of a crowd, thus : “Look at me, men •
I’m a market-gardener, and proud of it.” In the 80’s’
when Tommy held office, he went out to some pick-and-
shovel relief works for the unemployed. One man. on
grumbling at the poor pay, had his shovel taken out of
his hand by the Ministerial cabbage-grower, who imme-
diately threw up about a dozen shovelfuls of earth in
double quick time, remarking, “ That’s the way to do it,
my man.” “Yes,” replied the laborer, “That’s all very
well for 30 seconds, Mr. Bent, but how long could you
keep it upTommy walked away.

ran against the strongest men
he could pick out, hence his

an uphill fight with the
late Judge (then bar-
rister and politician)
Moorhead for the North
Murchison seat ; fol-
lowing year, he took
on a hopeless contest
for the Perth Legis-
lative Council seat
with George Shen-

the House of Land-
lords ; in January,
1902, he opposed
George Leake,
when the latter
sought re-election,
after accepting
the Premiership
for the second
time. Same year,
he ran for the
Senate, but “just
missed the ’bus ”
the yelping of the
Yellow Pup and the
Jingo ditto being mainly
responsible for his defeat.
The number of Thomson’s other
election-fights passeth the memory
of the present writer. He arrived at
last, a year ago, and sits in the Upper
House for Central Murchison. The Scotch-
man who doesn’t arrive at last is mostly dead.
The dispute between a member of the
Harris family and the N. S.W. Government, re
the land-tax valuation on a portion of the Ultimo
(Sydney) estate, is not the first of its kind. In
this case the contention is that the property
of Margaret Harris was over-valued by the
Crown, tax being claimed on £40,000 ; but, ordin-
arily, the trouble with the Harris family is the
other way about. When John Lackey was Minis-
ter for Works he resumed a portion of the Harris
estate, and offered compensation. The Harris
family claimed £117,382, but ultimately accepted
£30,000. About the same time Miss May Harris
relinquished a slice for the Darling Harbor rail-
way and demanded £21,912, but consented to take
£5125.
The daily papers ignored a funny speech
made during the strike by a barracker of
Sydney Employers’ Federation. The old man
shouted:— : “The world is governed by the
rule of three. Physicists tell us that every-
thing has length, breadth, and thickness.
Then there is the Father, Son, and Holy
Ghost in religion. So it is with commercial
and industrial enterprise. There is the capitalist,
the middleman, and the worker.” This old per-
son deserves to be remembered as the first man
who has had the courage to openly describe ex-
’bus boss Carey and God as two persons who are,
in a manner of speaking, in the same line of
business.
John Perry, recently deceased at Heath*
cote (Vic.), aged 95, had a variegated career.
Born in Essex (England), he became a cadet in
the East India Co.’s service at the age of 14.
Afterwards served in the navy for 30 years, part
of the time under Chas. Hotham before the latter
was made Governor of Victoria. Perry held a
’42 medal for service in the Chinese war, where he
received a musket ball in the thigh. He was on
active service in India, and was presented with a
handsome medal by Pope Pio Nono, who was on
his man-of-war for protection during the Italian
troubles. Old Perry was especially proud of
having borne Queen Victoria in his arms ; he
lifted her out of a boat when she was a little girl.
In ’53 he reached Victoria, and pioneered the
M‘lvor goldfield. He was a postman for 23 years,
and retired on his pension 21 years ago.
There was not an atom of ham-frill about
Judge Williams when he presided over Maori land
Arbitration Court. He sat in ordinary rig-out,
discarding wig and gown, and never troubled
over small points. His successor. Judge Cooper,
is quite another make of bicycle. His freezing
dignity, and his insistence on rigid Supreme
Court etiquette and on the deference due to his
exalted position are really appalling. One day
recently some irreverent individual chipped in
during a case, and wanted to know when on
earth Cooper was going to give his decision on
some malodorous fellmongery biz. The flabber-
gasted Judge sat erect, looked daggers at his
questioner, and said in cold, halting tones that
if counsel were present on the morrow (properly
robed) lie would give it. If they weren’t there
(prooerlv robed), he wouldn’t do so until they
wete. This pronouncement had such an awesome
effect that the chap who was arguing about the
hours in the bread and bun trade couldn’t gather
his scattered wits for at least five minutes.

Aboriginalities.
To Contributors.— In the case of ethnological and
natural history items, etc., always give date and
place of experience ;or as nearly as possible. Thus
many interesting observations will have scientific
value.
This reads like the usual agricultural lie :
The distribution of seed-wheat and fodder to needy
N.S.W. cookies is too much hampered by red-tapeism to
be of much practical aid. A printed form and declaration
is first sent to the applicant; this makes a searching in-
quiry into his circumstances. When returned to the
Department it rests fora couple of weeks, then is sent on
for report to the local “ trap ” who is too tired during the
hot weather to make any inquiry at all. As a rule he
judges the applicant’s opulence by the number of times
he comes to town, say in three weeks —if he comes in
more than twice the “ trap ’’ reckons he can do without
seed-wheat, and so reports. Even if the report is favor-
able the Department forgets all about it, and the appli-
cant gets left in either instance.
Under these circumstances it is obvious that no
seed-wheat at all is distributed. Then where did
all the seed that was distributed go to ?
» * • *
“T. ” : In the seventies, in a small
mining township on the west coast of Maoriland,
the Argus was run by an enterprising Irish
pressman. As a curiosity I kept a copy!
The proprietor, editor, compositor, printer,
and deliverer of this organ were one and
the same person. He told me he would
have called it the Times, but he had not a
big capital T. It was a single sheet,
printed on the one side, and the price
sixpence. The staff—the proprietor—
called for the paper on Monday, and the
next week’s issue was printed on the
blank side. The orthography was erratic.
Punctuation was ignored. The final letter
of a word, as often as not, was a capital.
Through over-work the editor was occa-
sionally unable to perform his multitu-
dinous duties. On such occasions the
Argus announced in big caps., “ OWING
TO THE INDISPOSITION OF THE
EDITOR THERE WILL BE NO
ISSUE THIS WEEK.” This was
distributed amongst the diggers by
the staff and sixpence paid for it.
" The last I saw of this all-round pre-
ssman was 24 years ago, when he was
wheeling his printing plant in a
barrow across Spooner’s Range to
Motueka, where he sold it for a few
pounds.
“ Deeby ” : The dying-out of the Aus-
tralian aborigines is generally attributed to the
effects of “ civilised ” vices. But most of the
deaths amongst the blacks result from pulmonary
complaints, contracted through having substituted
for the practically non-absorbent native skins the
“ Gov’inent blanket, ” which, when used as bedding
on damp ground, is deadly. Also, many deaths
result from hydatids, due to intimate association
with mongrel dogs.
“Kelm Scott”: Re aboriginal Hebrews
(B. 28/2/’03): In the Roebourne district (W.A.)
circumcision was certainly practised among
several tribes some years ago. I saw two
nigs, on Millstream station (80 miles nor’-east
from Roebourne) who had been circumcised, and
seeing is believing. Lockyer (a half-easte) de-
scribed the ceremony, and informed me that it
was a pretty general thing among four tribes in
that locality when he was a boy. Also, nor’-
west nigs, won’t eat bacon or pork; as a rule
they make a pretence of doing so, but it invari-
ably goes to their dogs. Asked several the
reason, and the usual answer was “No good to
eat.” They couldn’t —or wouldn’t —explain why.
Like Favenc, however, I can’t credit the yarn
about the goats, prayer-book, and Quaker Sunday
business.

From Australian Shooting and Fishing
Annual, 1903:
Those who have travelled much through the country
this last season must have noticed how exceptionally
tame all the little birds were. Starved out, they could
be seen fearlessly seeking food inside houses and in other
unusual places. At one place where I was camped during
the winter quite a bevy of wrens and warblers used to
come about us during meal times, picking the crumbs up
under our feet and hopping on to the table. Before we
left that place we were feeding, besides numerous small
birds, 3 magpies, 2 peewits, and a butcher bird, all of
which had become quite tame.
• * * »
At Milparinka, which is several hundred
miles out at the back of Bourke, and where the
grass is good just now and travelling easy, three
parsons turned up lately on one Sunday. And
the local youngster, who had never seen more
than one at a time before, and then only once
in a year or thereabout, got out into the dust
and stared, and said he didn’t know there
were so many of ’em in the world. He might
have thought it was a circus; only, poor little
beggar, he hasn’t the faintest notion of what
a circus is. Out there, though, when the
parsons do travel in a bunch like that tl.oy
don’t compete. They just parcel out the day—
the morning is Father Hogan’s, and the after-
noon belongs to the pale young man of the
Anglicans, and the Methody fills the even-
ing with warnings of the wrath to come. It’s
a happy arrangement; it allows of each man
taking up a collection from the whole commu-
nity (17, besides dogs). For, of course, the
whole community turns up —and if it likes the
Methody, it’s Methody till the next man comes ;
or if Father Hogan is more persuasive, it’s Holy
Roman.

“ Polstan ” : Tie extermination of black-
fish by perch (“Lin.” B. 7/3/’O2). Only the
common striped perch are so voracious. Writer
has for several years kept gold perch (large ones)
together with blackfish in a pond about 10ft. by
eight, and the blackfish appear to be all there yet.
Striped perch will clean a river out very quickly
by devouring the spawn of the native fish. The
Avoca River is full of very large blackfish and
perch. A small “blackie” is never hooked there
—a sure sign that as soon as the old fish disappear
the river will know no more of them. More’s the
pity. Writer is of opinion that one dish of black-
fish is worth two of perch.
* * * *
A curious feature in connection with
the earth-eating disease, as noted by Dr.
Macdonald, of Geraldton, is that the adult
victim, with his vitiated appetite, wants, not to
eat dirt, but to drink alcohol. The Bulletin
presents this valuable argument to its Cold Tea
friends. Dr. Macdonald maintains that the dis-
ease, which is due to the presence of a parasite in
the stomach, is already prevalent in N.Q., and is
spreading rapidly.

“C.8.J.”: Can anyone explain what is
the matter with the bees this season? Have
seen dozens of nests, and have found in them
comb only and plenty of bees, lively and in good
working order. No appearance of young bees,
as is usual. Yet the nests are free from moth.
Spencer’s Gulf (S.A.) holds one turtle,
apparently no more—it is quite out of the reptiles’
regular beat. Off and on this lone sample has been
seen for years; he has grown now to be a big
fellow, close on sft. broad. He doesn’t any
longer scurry to get out of the way of passing
vessels as he used to ; so, no doubt, his confidence
and some vandal’s harpoon will be the death of
him before long.

“ Banana ” writes from Ingham, North
Queensland, under date May 10 :
How is this for rain ? From Ist January to date this
year 103£in. have fallen (Bft. 7£in.). March record was
close on 51in. Greatest day’s record (March 27) was
13£in. Geraldton (N.Q.) and Russell River can beat us
easily. Winton and Hughenden have not had sin. yet.
The sugar cookies on the Johnstone last year were
grumbling at only having 74in. for the year. I think
they are satisfied this year.
Charles Kingston’s visit to this piebald land upset all
calculations. The sugar-squeezers and the huge C.S.R.
monopoly were aghast at his splendid reception every-
where. In the big centres of Black Labor he was treated
royally, and Queensland looks on him as the principal
Australian statesman of the day. No politician ever
created a better impression in this land of mongrel races.
Our State Treasurer has been up here on Kingston’s heels,
but the chilling reception he received must have dis-
heartened the lovers of the cheap Kanaka. There are
hundreds of men unemployed walking about the North
who are only too willing to work among the cane, but
the farmers will not give them a show ; they are holding
on like grim death to their pet Asiatic and Kanaka.
These do all sorts of work for the Colonial Sugar Co.,
even to navvying on the line, and about 90 per cent, of
the pubs, in the North employ Japs and Chinese. It is a
pity that white labor should patronise these lovers of the
Yellow Agony.

“ E.S.S.” (of the Richmond River blacks):
Have heard old hands say that, looking up
suddenly while riding, they have seen the trees
black with aborigines, who had a little habit of
perching up in trees like crows, keeping as quiet
as possums, till an unwary whitefellow or a
kangaroo happened along underneath. Either
was game to the watchers.

“ Deeby ” : I met a man the other day
who was travelling through the bush selling
“preventives.” He told me he had met with
some very severe rebuffs, but could put up with
that as he made a good thing out of the business.
When he first started, he said, he used to broach
the subject to men only, but now he generally
avoids men altogether. “ They are too bigoted,”
he explained.

“Weider”: Has any Bulletin reader
ever heard of one calf adopting another. Was
working for a dairyman once, when one of his
cows died from tick fever, leaving a 3-weeks-old
calf. Another calf, a 4-months heifer, thereupon
took charge of the orphan,
which would suck vigorously
at its foster mother’s teats
and bang her about, just as
if it were really getting milk.
The calf is six months old
now, has never been hand-fed,
and is as fat as a pig.

“Drummer” : What a country of fierce
contrasts it is ! Drove lately from Broken Hill
to Queensland border. For half the way not a
blade of grass; the other half, grass over the
sheeps’ backs. Yet in this latter country the
stock has dwindled from 970,000 to 220,000 in five
years. These three holdings alone—Cobham,
Yancannia, and Wonnaminta—lost 250,000 in one
year. To-day they could run a million and a-half.
This day twelvemonths they may not be fairly
able to run 15,000.
• • * *
“ Westralian ” : What a farce is our so-
called Government nigger-inspection ! In one
case last year the inspector camped about 10 miles
from the station I was on, and sent along for
some provisions, and to tell the boss that he (the
inspector) would arrive next day. Boss promptly
rounds-up the nigs., gives them “ big pheller tuck-
out,” new pipes, tobacco, dresses, shirts, &c., and
next morning, of course, inspector finds nothing
to reprehend. But no sooner has the official
departed than the clothes, &c., are taken from the
darkies and placed again in the store, to serve the
same purpose at next inspection.
THE LAPSE OF SWIPER JOE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
’Twas a sawmill in the ranges at the back of old Mirboo,
Where the mountain ash was highest and the mighty
black butt grew,
And the “ circulars ” were buzzing, and the “ verticals "
a-rip,
And death or mutilation only waited for a slip ;
And shakv was the tramway from the siding to the mill,
And the waggons rolled a-down it like loose boulders
down a hill.
But the brakesman was a reckless and a daring sort of
chap
Who for any sort of danger didn’t care a blessed rap.
“Are you ready ? ” “ Right-o I —start’em.” Then we’d
shove and let ’em go,
And the siding—or Gehenna—was the goal of Swiper Joe.
Now, the miller was as grasping and as conscienceless,
I’ll swear,
As anyone that ever sweated toilers anywhere,
And he’d rush us and he’d curse us all the time he
wasn’t boozed,
And wild and weird and blanky was the language that he
used ;
From the sawyers to the pitmen every man was daily
cursed,
But sometimes for variety the order was reversed.
“ Got that blinded order finished? What! condemn yer
lazy souls!
I’d hack ’em with a broad-axe in less time from curly
boles.
Grease them skids, you bloomin’ cows, there 1” and he
kept a-going so ;
But the only man that cursed him in reply was Swiper
Joe.
And when they both got going—well, the engines used to
stop,
And the millmen gape and listen, and the leaves begin
to drop;
And whether these eruptions were the cause I cannot say,
But the scrub around the sawmill had a withered look
and grey.
Joe was sacked and sent a-roaming several times in every
year ;
Then he roamed to the adjacent pub. and sat there
drinking b6er.
And the boss would go a-moping, sulky, silent and de-
pressed,
And fool around at night-time, and he couldn’t eat or
rest;
Then some morning we would miss him and his curses
fluent flow—
And we’d know that he had started on a jamboree with
Joe.
Well, this fighting sort or friendship, or whatever it
might be,
Kept a-growing and increasing both in volume and
degree,
Till a “Simultaneous Mission” drove the devil from
Mirboo;
And among the batch of sinners Swiper Joe was captured,
too.
Then, when the time for cursing in the morning came
around,
And the boss got fairly going, Joseph gazed upon the
ground ;
MIGHT HAVE FINISHED THE JOB.
FARMER DAIRYFED: “Sag, Mister Jinkins; I've
come acrost to ask you to loan me your old
grey mare to finish up my bit of ploughin’.
My old ’oman went and sprained ’er ankle
yisterday, jes’ when we only had four more
acres to finish; an’ I’m a bit too stoutish
myself for anythin’ ’cept drivin'.”
PROOF POSITIVE IN PUSH SOCIETY.
BOTTLE-O GOUDIE: “ I've jest bin dealin ’ out a bit of punch, to me missus.”
BOSS-EYE: “ ’Rout time you did. It’s been gettin ’ round that you two wasn't married at all.”
Not a syllable he uttered till the boss in wonder ceased,
And we saw that he was sobbing, and our interest
increased,
Till the boss in actual trouble showed a heart we didn’t
know,
When he said in shaky accents, “ What’s the bloomin’
matter, Joe?”
But Joseph was repentant, and declared “before his
God ”
That the boss was bound for Sheol, and was “on the
downward road.”
And so skilfully he snuffled, and so saintly he behaved,
That—l’m setting down no fiction—in a week the boss
was “ saved.”
And Joseph kept so holy he w.'.s soon relied upon
As cashier and accountant and paymaster all in one,
While the miller chased the Devil with the parsons round
about,
Till one inauspicious morning he discovered Joe was out,
And had scooted for the regions where the defalcators go.
And now a wicked, cursing boss is on the track of Holy
Joe.

Many people wonder how Bill, Sam and Dave,
spend their evenings Out Back. Listen, and on
any cart on a country road, and you won’t wonder
long. The “mouth organ ’’ is universal; and why
not? On a good instrument quite wonderful
results can be obtained. The trouble in the past
has been the poor quality obtainable, but Allan’s,
of Collins-street, Melbourne, have solved the
problem, and now furnish the best organs ever
made, namely, the “Crackajacks,” a family of
four. The “Crackajack Professional,” with 40
reeds, metal box, 4s. 6d, a real Daddy: the
“Crackajack Senior,” 20 reeds, 2s. 6d; the
“ Crackajack Junior,” a handy little fellow with
20 reeds, and the “ Boss Cracker” at a bob, is the
best Vamper ever made. Any kind of stamps
will do for a sample.*

“D.H.R.”: Talk about the gambling
instinct. A farmer, near Walgett (N.S.W.), not
long ago, put in 450 acres of wheat, on a chance of

3Jin. of it. Now he has a prospect of a £5OOO
harvest.

“ Bunny ” : Travelled lately from Broken
Hill to Menindie, Wilcannia, back to Menindie,
down the Darling to Wentworth, 100 miles up the
ana-branch of the Darling, out to Lake Victoria,
partly into Victoria and South Australia, making
numerous trips off the track, then back to Broken
Hill, and saw only one rabbit, no kangaroos, no
emus, no turkeys, and few small birds. The whole
territory is just desert, save for a few deliciously
green patches of warrigal c <bbage, due to an acci-
dental rainfall as unexpected as it was sparse.
THE LATE MAX O’REEL,
Photo, by Falk & Co., Sydney.
Unhappy Broken Hill still has its tongue
hanging out —the rain continues to dodge it merci-
lessly. It has had only one decent sprinkle in
20 months ; for two years its total record is under
7in., and most of that has come in ineffective
driblets of Bor 10 points. There has been m all
this time only enough of a flow into the reservoir
to slightly adulterate the ooze that remains there ;
and even the supply of slightly adulterated ooze
'will give out within about two months if no rain
tomes.

BUSH STANDARD OF FITNESS.
THE REV. JOSEPH : “ Yes, I heard of Conroy's death. Poor fellow, I'm afraid he was not fit
to enter into the Kingdom of Heaven."
BILLJIM : “Not fit! Great Scot! you should ’o’ seen him sit a bucker!”
Crippa Clarke takes up the brief for the
“ cocky ” :
Hasn’t he any friends ? First, we have “ the ignorant
cocky.” Then “ the unwashed cocky,” and, finally, he
is indignantly described by some hungry bush-drummer
as the “inhospitable cocky.” Well, he may be ignorant
in spots—large spots, if you like. He may be various
other things—but inhospitable? No! So far as his
limited means allow, the average cocky is as hospitable
as the next man. Counted a fair mark for ridicule by
every alleged humorist who is hard up for “copy,” the
cocky is the most misrepresented man in Australia —as
the Punch Irishman is in England. Writer now claims a
free-kick for other side. Cockies’ traducers may be roughly
divided into three classes: (1) The city paragraphist who
has never seen a ‘ ‘ cocky,” but uses the name as local
color for the funny stories he steals from Yankee prints.
He is comparatively harmless. (2) The ultra-democr.ii.ic
swagmen who despise him because he isn’t asquatier,
sponge on him for the meals the squatters often refuse,
and then write to The Bulletin complaining what a
very poor feed it was. (3) The hungry army of bush
“ drummers,” machinery, life and fire insurance agents,
book fiends, sewing machine canvassers, and the gentle-
manly hawkers of alleged tea. On a main road, and off
it for that matter, never a day passes but one to half a
dozen of these uninvited guests drop in for a meal, or a
night’s lodging ; and those on horseback, or in sulkies,
will also require a feed for their mokes. The long-suffering
cocky' is expected to welcome the daily arrival of strange,
non-paying guests with the gratified courtesy of the mana-
ger of the Australia receiving a party of millionaires.
Why, even in Sydney, where it is the recognised custom to
courteously entertain at dinner all the gentlemen who call
during the morning with simples of hearth-stone and
“ wild rabbits,” a citv merchant may occasionally forget
to take home to his table the reduced importer who haunts
his office with bone-studs and shoe-laces, without running
the risk of being held up to public scorn as a degraded
citizen. Why, then, should the humble cocky be always
expected to put aside his work for the day T , and kill the
fatted ’possum for every swivel-eyed book fiend who
wants him to buy “ The Manners and Customs of the
Australian Bull-ant” in 45 vols. And the cheek of some
of these hungry drummers would turn the edge of a meat-
axe. The swagman who asks the charity of a square meal
has quite as good a chance of getting it from the despised
cockv as from any other man ; but hospitality means
more than that, and the bush-dweller, be he cocky or
squatter, reserves that something more for his friends or
for visitors whom he knows by name, at least. Mithin
these limitations, present ivriter, having some 20 odd
years’ experience to guide him, has no hesitation in giving
thelieto those writerswholose no opportunity ofdefaming
a kindly, hard-working class. That there are black sheep,
as in all other grades of society, goes without saying ;
but, taking them by and large, and according to their
limited means, the small farmers of Australia give what
they have to give as freely as any other class. Have
known them, to take the blankets off their own beds in
winter that a guest might lie warm. The man who
accepts the hospitality of a poor man, freely offered, and
then writes to the press about the poverty-stricken ap-
pearance of the table is an unmitigated cad, and gene-
rally a liar to boot.
THE END OF SQUATTER MERINO.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
A number of persons were fined for failure to send in
their stock returns. Plenty of time has apparently been
given for this purpose, since the time for sin'h returns
expires on 31st January.— Melbourne News Item.
The drought was raging fiercely on Merino’s western run,
And the erstwhile rich and mighty Sheeponaire,
When every sheep had perished, and his horses all but
one,
Gave up the hopeless struggle in despair.
“The world is wide, and I have yet my life,” Merino
cried,
“ To measure against Fate and see it through 1
My only one remaining horse,” quoth he, “I’ll mount
and ride,
Into the open world, and start anew.”
Next morning, full of hope, he went to catch his only
steed,
But when, against the homestead paddock gate,
He found that wretched neddv, too, from thirst and
hunger freed,
Merino wept and loudly cursed his fate.
With heart and spirit broken then he slowly staggered
back
Towards his dismal home, and, waiting there,
A-holding up a skeleton that once had been a hack,
He found a man with most officious air.
, 1 You’ll find this summons, sir,” said he, “ for you with-
out a doubt”—
And handed him a bluish document.
Merino asked, in timid tones, what it could be about
Since he had always promptly paid his rent.
“Perhaps you have,” the fellow snarled —"re that I’m
not concerned :
You’ve not sent in your stock-returns this year ;
By way of demonstrating that obedience must be learned
They’ll fine you, sir,'a pound or two, I fear.”
Merino stared and wildly tore the document to bits,
And hurled them at the haughty fellow’s head !
And then he shook and trembled as a man attacked by
fits,
And yelled : " This is too much! ” and then dropped
dead I

ANSWERS to CORRESPONDENTS.
No notice (except of a hostile character) will be taken of
communications insufficiently stamped. Name and
address must appear on MS. So liability is under-
taken in any case re contributions voluntanly sub-
mitted, whether sent by post or handed in ; and in
no case will MS. be returned unless stamps (of any
State) accompany.

“ Marguerite *’ : Recently attended a de-
bating society where the young rhetorical lions
of the town struggled with the question :
“Should the sale of intoxicating liquors be pro-
hibited ?” The first young lion got up with a ton
of notes, blushed violently, and spoke :
The lunatic asylums is full of lunatics, the gaols is full
of prisoners, the hospitals is full of sick people—all
through drink. Take up the daily papers every day and
you will find it full of drunkards. A man deserves great
credit for bringing up a large wife and family. If a ipan
stops out drunk all night he does not come home.
Then he sat down, and in his confusion applauded
himself violently.
MR. THOMAS TAIT ,
New Vic. Commissioner of Railways.
Photo, by F. Lyonde, Canada.
There is a good deal of talk about the
civil service representatives to be elected for Vic.
State Parliament. Scorer and Hart are bracketted
as likely candidates. Scorer, anyhow, ought to
have a good show. Ex-civil servant Byron Moore
has been suggested, also pensioner Morrah, who
succeeded Moore as Secretary for Lands. But,
after all, the service wants a live man, not a half-
dead pensioner.
Now that Australia is comparatively sober,
will someone draft a Bill providing that all fili-
bustering expenses shall be paid by a direct impost
per head of the whole population, said impost to
be honestly christened “War-tax?” The tax
might be dormant in peace time, but whenever
Australia sends a “contingent” anywhere the
Treasurer should be bound to start collecting it
immediately, without any further Parliamentary
authority, and to keep on collecting it till the
whole war bill is paid.

HOIST WITH HIS OWN PETARD.
A curious instance has recently occurred of how
one is sometimes" hoist with his own petard.”
Some time as:o, the proprietors of Oerebos Table Salt
were prosecuted by the Police Authorities of Paris for
selling salt “ adulterated ” with Phosphates. In order to
settle the legal question definitely, the Company carried
the case right to the highest court of France, the Cour
de Cassation. Having demonstrated there that, far from
being an adulteration, the presence of the phosphates
made Uerebos Salt a valuable addition to the food, the
High Court accorded the Company a triumphant acquittal.
The document upon which the prosecution was based
was the official report and analysis prepared for the police
by the Municipal Laboratory of Paris, and intended to
support the charge of “adulteration.” In a Brochure
just published in French, containing the opinions
of eminent scientists in Paris and elsewhere, the
place of honor is given to this very report, which so clearly
justifies the claim the Cerebos Company make for their
product—though intended to do exactly the reverse—
that they evidently consider they can offer to the public
no better guarantee that their Salt is what it professes to
be, a food as well as a seasoning.*
At the Bar.
It has been the most important event in the
lives of not a few great men when they were called
to the Bar, and to each it has happened but once
in the course of their careers. Thereafter they
have practised at the Bar with honourable dis-
tinction and profit to themselves, as it was fitting
they should do after a long and arduous training
therefor. Another vastly more numerous body of
men also practice at the bar, some very constantly
and with distinction, but always with loss rather
than profit. They do not train for the employ-
ment, nor are they specially “called” to it, but
incline thereto by a sort of natural bias which
they seem powerless to effectively resist. Stay !
What are we discussing? Bars? Why, to be
sure, there are two sorts —one of the law oourts,
another of the hotels.
We once heard a distinguished K.C. assert that
good liquor is better than bad law. We will
concede the point without argument, but are
prepared to maintain that Mother Seigel’s
Curative Syrup is better than either. Miss
Helena Easton, of 50 Pacific Street, N.S.W.,
found it so. Miss Easton, who has been em-
ployed as barmaid in some of the leading
hotels in the Newcastle district, thus writes on
October 21, 1902: In the summer of 1898, when
residing in Goulburn, my health first gave way. I
lost appetite, and became weak, nervous and
languid. The little food I ate caused dreadful
pains in the chest and stomach, with some other
distressing symptoms. A doctor informed me that
my complaint was acute indigestion, but his treat-
ment did me no good. After a few months I tried
another doctor, with similar result. Then, on the
recommendation of one and another, I took all
kinds of medicines ; but not one of them did me
the least good. For two years I continued to
grow paler, thinner and feebler. The slightest
noise or excitement would cause my heart to pal-
pitate violently, after which it would almost cease
to beat, and I would feel very faint. A lady
friend from Sydney, Mrs. W. Cook, persuaded me
to try Mother Seigel’s Curative Syrup, as she knew
several persons who had derived great benefit from
it, and was sure it would do me good. Mrs. Cook
was right. Before I had finished the second bottle
there was a great change in my condition for the
better. I could not only eat, but could enjoy
food ; the roses returned to my cheeks and plump-
ness to my figure. In short, after suffering from
indigestion for over two years, I was completely
cured in five weeks.”

PURSUANT to an Order of the Chancery Division of
the High Court of Justice dated 23 June 1902 made
in the matter of the Estate of Edward Brookes deceased
and in an Action of Cooper and another against Porter
(1902. B No. 1567) the persons claiming to be NEXT OF
KIN according to the Statutes for the distribution of
Intestates Estates of EDWARD BROOKES late of The
Robinson Crusoe Inn Suffrage Street Smethwick in the
County of Stafford who died on the 19th of March 1901
living at the time of his death or to be the legal personal
representatives of such of the said next of kin (if any) as
have died since the said 19th March 1901 are by their
Solicitors on or before the 26th day of October 1903 to
come in and prove their claims at the Chambers of Mr
Justice Byrne and Mr Justice Buckley at the Royal
Courts of Justice Strand London England or in default
thereof they will be peremptorily excluded from the
benefit of the said Order.
Tuesday the 10th day of November 1903 at 11 of the
clock in the forenoon at the said Chambers Room No.
262 is appointed for hearing and adjudicating upon the
claims.
Dated the 7th day of April 1903
O. BURNEY Master

Country Orders by post from all the colonies promptly
attended to.
London Truth exposes a new system of
post-mortem blackmail. When some well-to-do
or well-connected person dies a letter is addressed
to him, purporting to come from some woman,
who expresses surprise that she has neither seen
him for some weeks, nor received any money for
household expenses. She trusts he is not ill, and,
with many expressions _of affection, assures him
that our dear baby ”is quite well. Sometimes
it pans out a good deal of money for self and the
dear baby.

“ Yanco ” : A casual Australian strolled
thoughtfully into a wayside pub. and called for
some whisky. On being handed the bottle he
asked for some sugar. While the barmaid was
busy getting this for him he leisurely took a small
parcel from his pocket, carefully unfolded it,
and tenderly placed a great hollow molar
on the bar-counter. He filled his glass with
whisky, filled his one-time tooth with sugar, and,
meditatively at it, said with emphasis,
Now ache, you brute, ache! ” Revenge is
sweet.

One Rogers, a J.P. of Malvern (Vic.),
recently edified Hobart with a lecture on bar-
maids. He was a violent man, and even peaceful
Hobart nearly threw its cobblestones at him
before he was finished. Some extracts from the
Hobart paper report:—
, Some of these hotels employed 12 barmaids. Could
they he properly employed to serve drink—so many of
them? (Interruption.)
He referred to them in many cases as decoys.
(“Shame!”)
The speaker here read a very “strong” letter from an
alleged barmaid, speaking against her vocation and its
temptations. A girl who spent her youth as a barmaid
would probably spend the balance of her life either on
the streets or doing washing. (Interruption.)
He could not repeat the shameful language indulged
in. (Voices: “That’s false. That’s a lie!” And dis-
order.)
The language could not be repeated in that building.
(Disorder.)
Seven-tenths of the barmaids of Launceston lived an
immoral life. (Disorder.)
. It is a fact that in some instances it was made a condi-
tion that the girls should occupy the same bedroom as
the landlords. (A voice : “You ought to be pole-axed.
It is not true.” Disorder.)
Just fancy a Minister of the Crown going round from
house to house with business men of Launceston and
jeering with the girls, and behaving in a most improper
manner. (Loud interruption and cries of “ Who is the
Minister ?”) He would not saj\
About this time several 3 r oung females left the hall.
Mr. Rogers referred to a number of girls in Launceston
who were “nothing better than prostitutes.” (“Oh,”
and great disorder at the back.)
The speaker next told the story of a public servant in
Tasmania W'ho hunted a barmaid from here to Victoria.
He referred to certain barmaids here who went into
certain places where they were confined. (Applause,
and derisive laughter.)
The speaker gave some more anecdotes of a Hawklet
character, and a number of women and girls got up and
precipitately left the hall, amid loud cries from some of
the men to Mr. Rogers of “ You ought to be ashamed of
yourself! ”
Mr. Rogers then told a story about a barmaid “ who
had her fifth child,” etc.
Mr. Blanchard appealed to the chairman, saying he
thought that gentleman ought to ask the young ladies to
go out of the hall, and not listen to such stories as these.
The chairman though the speaker “ should not go so
fully into particulars.” (Applause.)
A number of other girls and women here left the hall.
At this stage more women and girls in large numbers
got up and trooped out of the hall.
The chairman hoped that all the young girls would go
out, and many of them did so, as well as older ones.
The teetotal lecturer at his worst seems about as
morally undressed an entertainment as Australia
possesses.

Which is—if he has got the sense—
To hasten to procure
The thing that sells for eighteenpence,

Dear Bulletin, —The Brisbane daily
papers the other day contained eulogistic refer-
ences to the test made by the Post Office officials
of an ingenious penny-in-the-slot automatic
stamping machine, invented by a Mr. Holling-
worth and made by a Mr. Jackson. The notices
concluded by the statement that no official decision
had been arrived at, but that the authorities ha,d
erected the machine at the G.P.O. for the public
to decide as to its efficacy or otherwise. The
place where it was to be seen was set forth.
Two days afterwards I went to inspect the
machine. It was not where I expected to find it.
Thought I might have made a mistake, so hunted
all round the buildings—no machine. I had
business at the money order counter, and asked
the cash guardians where I could find the machine.
They were very civil, but did not know—it was
not connected with their department. They
advised me to try at the delivery window. I did.
There they looked for it in pigeon hole M, pigeon
hole S, and pigeon hole P, but could not find it
and said they had never heard of it. They
advised me to try the Dead Letter Office. I
went there and was asked when it was despatched,
and where from, and whether it was sufficiently
stamped, and a lot of other questions. I explained
as well as I could, but it seemed that though the
machine was lost they knew nothing of it in that
branch of the Department. I tried the stamp
issue department but it was not there. The
official in charge had heard of it and did not
think much of it—he did not look as though he
thought much of anything. He could not tell
me where I would be likely to hear of it. I
asked a policeman. He pointed majestically to
a door I had overlooked and said “ You ashk in
there, young mahn, an they’ll be afther tellin yez
ahl about it.” I asked in there and was curtly
told that it was a place from which to send
telegrams, not a public inquiry office. Then I
asked everyone I saw—clerks, accountants,
telegraphists, letter-carriers, mail-cart drivers,
sub-officials, officials, major officials, bosses,
strangers—everybody. No one knew. Finally I
struck a small but intelligent telegraph boy.
“ That penny-in-the-slot merchine ” he said “ Oh,
I’ll shew ’tyer. Come erlonger me,” and he
led me to the spot where I had originally looked
for it, and showed me a mark on the wall and
said “ That’s wher ’twas.” “ But where is it
now ?” I said. “ Lord knows,” he replied.— Ne’er-
do-Weel,

The story of creation and destruction and
the beginning and the end of all things :
The Great God rested from His labors, for He had
created many beautiful thing’s and was awearied. On the
high balcony of heaven He rested, smoking His pipe and
contemplating His works. For long mused He ; then*
rising, knocked His pipe on the golden parapet and for a
moment watched the live sparks floating through space
and expiring one by one. And behold, those sparks and
that moment were all of time and the visible universe,
and their burning and dying had compassed the myriads
of generations of men and star-angels—their births ana
deaths—their boundless hopes and fathomless despairs.
And He had seen it all in His glance and felt it all in a
pulse beat And the Great God went again to His labors.
The excursion by 8.8. Victoria to the South Sea
Islands has been organised for the purpose of visiting a
large number of places of interest—principally mission
stations —which are not in the trading routes. Passen-
gers will fully appreciate the unique opportunity now
available for cruising 7000 miles of the Pacific on a 3000-
ton ocean yacht. The Victoria leaves Sydney on 25th
July next. The prospectus of the tour sets forth a pro-
gramme far in advance of that of any previous excursion
to the Dreamlands of Coral and Palm. Excursionists
will spend eighteen days ashore. Cardinal Moran and a
clerical party go with this excursion on a tour of inspec-
tion of the mission stations.*

of Buddhism.

MY HEAVEN.
[FOR THE BULLETIN. ]
" Our heaven is here, if we only knew
Where to seek for it so a poet said ;
And the bard spoke truth. I have proved it so,
But mine has been here, and it now is fled.
And so little I ask when all is told—
A bridge, a girl, and a moon on high.
So very little, yet all earth’s gold
Will not buy these three, and I’ll tell you why.
There are moons and bridges and girls galore;
There’s but one of each in this heaven of mine ;
The bridge fell a year gone by or more,
And was washed by the floods o’er the border line.
There are moons and girls in abundance still,
But the girl I want in the graveyard lies ;
And a hundred orbs may the domed skies fill,
But the light has gone out of my blinded eyes.
V.&W.
