POLITICAL
POINTS
What progress has been made in the
investigation of that alleged gigantic Customs
swindle in New Guinea ?
The dirt-eating disease is said to have
broken out badly in Queensland —just as if our
K.C.M.G.’s haven’t been visibly suffering from it
for the last 30 years.
The most humorous political item wired
from Europe this year is the announcement of
Kaiser Bill’s dismissal of War Minister for
“boundless arrogance.”
A short Bill dealing with rings and trusts
is to be brought forward during the current
session of the Federal Parliament. How the Act
will affect the cable trust of the Australian news-
papers is a question which will be watched with
interest.
If Irvine’s Bill had passed, and Cardinal
Moran had said in Melbourne what he said in
Sydney, Dill Macky might have had the great joy
of seeing Constable O’Hooligan escorting the Red
Hat to Little Bourke st., with no thought of bail
possible.
Owe’Sullivan’s list of the most violent
retrenchers of the House, who are also the most
aggressive cadgers for expensive public works in
their own constituencies, is expected to see the
light during the first night of the forthcoming
censure debate.
The Vic. railway, stationmasters and por-
ters were loyal and didn’t strike. And, after all
Bent’s effusive promises about the reward of
loyalty, almost his first act is to put a large num-
ber of'them on half-pay. Some were only getting
30s. a week, and are now cut down to 15s. ! !
The undignified squabble between the
Federal Representatives and members of the Vic-
torian House of Fat (caused wholly by the inso-
lence of the latter), at the opening of Federal
Parliament, points again to the fact that there is
not room enough in Australia for so many Houses
of Parliament.
Now that the British Tory Government
has declared for old age pensions, Australia’s
ventures in that direction will perhaps cease
to be accounted quite so “ revolutionary ” and
“socialistic”. Surely anyone who calls Balfour
and the Cecil family socialists and red upheavalists
must be off his head.
Chief Commissioner Tait arrived in Mel-
bourne from Canada last week, under the impres-
sion that he had come to be a practical manager
of the Vic. railways. By this time, perhaps, it
has become evident to Tait that a practical
boozer, speechmaker, jobber and hail-politician-
well-met person is the sort of Tait that Bent
wanted.
The height of legal absurdity was nearly
reached, the other day, when a learned counsel
gravely ’asked his Honor to reserve for the Full
Court the point that it had not been proved that
Maoriland was “ a British possession.” And his
Honor, instead of requesting the legal light not to
be a blithering idiot, solemnly noted the point for
future argument.
Melbourne Women’s Political Association
has carried a resolution —
That, being opposed to industrial war, we strongly
urge the passing of an Industrial Arbitration Act.
Same association, which seems to have good,
sound democratic sense, protested against Irvine’s
senseless tyranny in the partial disfranchisement
of the railway servants.
On the N.S.W. railways all manufactured
goods pay an average freight of 14$. per ton for 100
miles while the rate for the farmers’ produce
varies from 3s. to 3s. 8d. On a rough estimate
this is a concession, or grant, or bonus of £800,000 a
year to the farming interest. N.S.W. has always
done this sort of thing, but has always denied that
it protects the farmer in any shape.
Postmaster-General Drake has explained
to a deputation of ex-cohentingenters, doing
temporary duty in the Federal public service,
that even all the hens they stole, and the farms
they burned, and the women and children they
threw out to perish on the cold veldt, don’t con-
stitute reasons why they should be exempt from
the ordinary regulations of the service. Much
dissatisfaction is felt in consequence.
At the Conference of . nagging .. State
Premiers one of the grievances advanced was that
the Australian Government had not yet paid for
buildings and .properties taken over from the
States. The rasping Joe Cook was just
starting to speak about this grievance in the
House, the other day, when Treasurer Turner
flattened him out with the intimation that he had
been asking the States for 12 months to decide
how much they wanted for the properties, and the
bills hadn’t come in even yet.
A point emphasised by the See Govern-
ment in defence of its determination to withdraw
the always illogical and at present illegal subsidy
to the daify newspapers, is that in 1891, when the
Post-office paid the railways for free carriage of
the papers, £2500 a year was fixed upon as the
payment, that being calculated as equivalent to
quarter parcel rates. Since then the railways have
grown and the newspapers have grown, and the
present charge made (£9900 a year) is calculated
on the old basis —quarter parcel rates.
N.S.W. Opposition leader Carruthers
announces that the party he leads is a fresh and
vigorous young party full of life and energy, and
with a great future before it. It may be so, but
to the naked eye it is just the same elderly and
rather slimy Carruthers, the same aged Brunker,
same old Affleck, and Haynes, and Jessep, and
Nelson, and Whiddon, and Young, and Barnabas
O’Conor—same old baldheads and has-beens and
extinct volcanoes.
Ballarat, at the beginning of the railway
strike, raised a huge meeting to denounce
Irvine and Bent. It was the biggest demon-
stration of the strike period, and, in point of
numbers, outweighed almost all the anti strike
demonstrations put together. But the Melbourne
papers suppressed all mention of it, and devoted
the space it should have occupied to relating how
15 sharebrokers met together and approved of
Bent with enthusiasm.
The death of four M.L. Legislative
Councillors—Grace, Swanson, Morris, and Whit-
more—within a few weeks of one another affords
Premier Seddon an opportunity of rewarding
deserving friends. Rumor has it that one or two
promoters of the “national purse” business will
get their reward when the next batch of “lords”
is created. The brewing brotherhood is also
understood to be pressing for more adequate
representation of Beer in the Council.
Dry Dog Reid, throwing his “Yes, Mr.
Watson ” gibe at Premier Barton, says he never
made any bargains with the Labor party when he
was in office in N. S. W. But he was quite willing
to do it, though. When he wanted the tea duty,
and the Labor men didn’t want it, he sent in a
message that he would give them old-age pensions
in 12 months if they would agree to the duty. The
reply was that they would vote for the duty if
their Yes-No friend would give them the pensions
at once, but that they weren’t taking any of his
p.n.’s at 12 months.
Indignant correspondents have been filling
the Perth (W.A.) dailies with wails because none
of the looal Labor members turned up at the
public crawl given to the visiting Jap mariners,
and these absentees are accused of being ignorant
and prejudiced, and are invited to cultivate the
virtues of “their distinguished visitors.” Most
of them, however, are goldfields men, and see so
much of the partially dressed Jap woman as she
leans out of the window and appeals to the passer-
by, that they have little desire to cultivate her
brother’s acquaintance.
NOBODT'S DOG.
Vic. Treasurer Shiels, being urged the
other day to start a public debt sinking fund,
replied : “It is absurd to think that we should
place on this generation the whole burden we incur
on behalf of posterity.” And, on the other hand,
Shiels might note that it is a villainous swindle
for this generation to use up all the public assets
in the shape of State lands, and then leave
posterity to pay the whole bill for public works
that this generation has used till they are worn to
mere scrap iron.
Governor Rawson’s visit to Norfolk Island
was different, in some respects, from that of his
illustrious predecessor, old Governor King.
Almost the first act of King was to order a man
to be led by a halter through the village and given
36 lashes for stealing the doctor’s rum. He
ordered a boy of 15 a hundred lashes, and in-
scribed on the records, “I think it will have a
good effect.” A woman was given 50 lashes for
drink, and 100 lashes next day for stealing shirts,
and another woman got 110 lashes for seditious
language.
“Adelaide”: Curious to see all
the old Female Suffrage jokes and argu-
ments dished up again over the Federal
franchise—all the pictures of women
wearing trousers, and the suggestions
that husband and wife will quarrel
about politics and so break up the
happy home. We went through pre-
cisely the same stage in S.A. about
nine years ago. Women got their
votes, there was the usual difficulty
about enrolment (many of them saying
that they hadn’t asked for a vote, and
wouldn’t use it), all kinds of forecasts
were made as to ultimate results—and
there seems to have been no ultimate
result at all! For several years after
the change the Labor party gained a
few seats; latterly there has been a
slight reaction, but there is nothing to
show that the women’s vote caused
either movement. Woman has got ab-
stract justice in the matter of the
suffrage, and she has got it without _
breaking or losing or demoralising anything.
Though the Vie. railway men’s strike
didn’t cost the unions that were affected a very
great deal of money, it won’t be able to compare
in that respect with the Westralian strike of a
year or two back. The unions that time weren t
called upon for a penny of strike-pay, and the
only expense they had in buying off blacklegs
(which often runs into a lot more money than the
public have any idea of) was that they had to pay
one man’s fare to Bunbury in order to get him out
of the road.
Leader of N.S.W. Opposition Carruthers,
now that the strike is over and sympathy is of no
earthly use: —
He would never deem it necessary nor wise to exas-
perate a body of human beings by doing disagreeable
things in a most disagreeable way, and he thought that
that was a mild way of expressing the opinion of most
unprejudiced men of Mr. Bent’s methods.
When the strike is over and public opinion is
swinging anti-Irvine ! What cowardly minds poli-
tics make.
S.M. Herald the other day assured the
public that next Federal election will be the grave
of the Labor party in Queensland, and that the
party only achieved its successes in 1901 because
of the over-confidence of the other fellows. It
seems just about as probable that the Herald’s
friends will find a grave. McDonald Paterson,
for example, got in at Brisbane by the votes of
2465 people out of 12,980 on the roll, and Edwards
represents 3753 out of a possible 12,277 voters at
Oxley. Such tottering minorities as those are
liable to go at any moment.
Many representatives of the stupid Party
of Fat, who loudly shrieked to the Icicle Govern-
ment to stand firm at all costs during the recent
crisis, are now urging that gang of spoliators and
wreckers to try and close on the funds of the
Drivers’ and Firemen’s Association to compensate
for the loss inflicted on the State when Bent
forced them to strike. Alas ! Another curse come
home to roost! If the Party of Fat had helped
to pass—instead of blocking—an Arbitration Act,
and the enginemen then struck, the Court would
probably have seized the £70,000 as damages—or
a good part of it—as a matter of course.
Bent, it will be remembered, recently
threatened that, if the Victorian railway servants
did not leave the Trades Hall, an order for the
construction of 20 locos.—for which the State
workshops had tendered at the lowest figure—
would be sent abroad. If that threat is fulfilled
now as part of the Government’s revenge on its
servants, the Amalgamated Society of Engineers
(which has branches in every civilised country)
might follow up the order and declare it a
“ black ” job wherever it is sent. The society
could probably block the order at any engine-shop
in the British dominions or in the United States.
Irvine’s infamous Bill, the willingness of
the Vic. Assembly to pass it, and the eagerness of
the Council to rush it through, has thoroughly
alarmed the sleepy democrats of the State.
When they awake they pretty well all go one
way—towards the Labor party. The suburbs are
being dotted with new branches of the political
Labor organisation. If only Syme had not grown
so fat the Liberals might have scored now. But
Peacock stands for Vic. Liberalism to-day—
Peacock ! The man who led the railway men,
not to a compromise, but to an “unconditional
surrender.” Cornelius Job, or Knox of the race-
course hat, or any other obvious Tory could have
done as much for them as that.
“ R.M.,” who is evidently optimistic :
Westralian Labor party proposes running candidates for
all Federal seats except John Forrest’s. The Coolgardie
and Perth representative seats are held with a
firm grip by Mahon and Fowler. Kalgoorlie, which
was lost to the Labor party last election, should
be a safe Labor seat ; and at Fremantle a Labor
man should have no difficulty in ousting the
antediluvian Solomon. The vast extent and scattered
population of Westralia make the Senate seats
a gift to the Labor party. The fattest of fat Senators
couldn’t afford to travel the whole of his electorate, and,
even if he'could, the Federal Electoral Act wouldn’t let
him. The limitation of election expenses makes it im-
possible for a man not backed by an organisation to win
a Senate seat; and, in Westralia, there is only one poli-
tical organisation in sight—and that’s the Labor party.
This (copied from London Daily Mail) is
the sort of thing one finds even in the Common-
wealth’s Foreign-trade press nowadays. (Laun-
ceston Examiner extract):
So far has the fetish of freetrade carried us that in
every walk of life, political, commercial, and private, the
bitter truth is that, in dealing with Britain, you had
better be her enemy than her friend ; that, just as out-
casts of the Fatherland are welcomed in our streets to
make day and night hideous with their music, and all
our best hotels are run by foreigners, so our ships are
largely manned and officered by enemies of the home-
land, who do not scruple to boast openly that their one
end, their one aim, is the downfall of Britain. But since
interference with this undesirable state of affairs means
a tampering with the Shekinah of Freetrade we must
stand aside with bated breath and see our national income
decrease, our expenditure increase, our manufactures
ruined by hostile tariffs, the cream of our trade oversea
filched from us jeeringly. But, unless as a nation we are
doomed, there will be something very like a revolution
before long, when once the significance of present
occurrences has soaked into the minds of our workers.
THE NEXT MOVE—IRVINES REWARD.
The cost of land-stealing ! Britain’s army
and navy cost £35,000,000 in 1895 ; the estimated
cost for 1904 is £64,000,000.
A wearisome cable heading in the Aus-
tralian dailies is—“ Severe strictures by the
Times.” The Vic. engine drivers’ strike was
another occasion for strictures by the pompous
old London journal. Of all newspapers in the
land where the cable liar earns his wages of sin
the Times seems the most constantly troubled
with strictures, and they’re always severe too.
Why can’t the Times indulge in slight strictures,
for a change ?
Sydney Telegraph is wonderfully gritty
and independent with one side of its mouth. It
despises the dependent unemployed
The men of former generations who wrought their own
prosperity and that of this country, did so in the teeth of
great difficulties, and without any thought of calling on
the Government wherever a log had to be shifted off the
path of progress.
But with the other side of its mouth it makes
morning hideous because the Government will
not give a free railway ride to its tons of parcels.
It is only charged even now one-quarter as much
as the draper or grocer pays, but that does not
satisfy it ; it calls on the Government to carry its
wares for nothing.
The Federal Labor party has decided that
it won’t move in the matter of Robert Reid, the
alleged senator, who sits in the Federal Parlia-
ment, despite the fact that his election was
distinctly illegal, and despite the recent con-
viction of his firm for defrauding his country.
The Labor party reckons Reid isn’t its
business. The Bulletin suggests that there
is a great future before any party which
will make the cleanliness and respectability
of politics its business. Also that it is
especially the Labor party’s business to suppress
the bumptious illegalities of its special enemy
Irvine, who unlawfully shoved his political hanger-
on into the Senate.
Amusing to read day by day that “ a
branch of the People’s Reform League was formed,
and officers elected, at Dry Dog Hollow on Thurs-
day night.” It doesn’t mean that there is any
great public “movement” (everythingis “amove-
ment ” nowadays), but merely that the same old
crowd of hucksters have held a meeting and called
certain of their number Secretary, President,
Vice-President, Member of the Executive, Grand-
Delegate, Principal Drunk, and other high-
sounding names. These people will vote just the
same way as they have always done—for the party
M'Millan approves of ; so they don’t represent
any increase of strength for the Carruthers push.
But they are a “branch.”
Apropos of “Joey ” Carruthers’ perpetual
wailings about the “Friedman case,” wasn’t there
an episode connected with the Ministry of which
Carruthers was a member which is much more in
need of resurrection ? Hasn’t the public still a
very ugly recollection that a prominent member
of Parliament (opposed to the Ministry to which
Carruthers belonged) was arrested for no reason
visible to the public, though his partner was cer-
tainly supposed to have broken the law ; that he
was haled to the lock-up on a Saturday afternoon,
and every effort made t > prevent him getting bail,
in which case he would have been locked up over
Sunday ; that the case was pushed to the utmost;
and finally, when the matter came to trial, the
Carruthers gang had no shred of real evidence to
produce against him, and not apparently even a
reasonable suspicion ? Carruthers belonged to the
Ministry which was responsible for that most
disreputable act, and it is one which calls for 100
times more explanation than the Friedman case
does, and which has also been calling a great deal
longer. In fact, the Friedman case doesn’t call
for any explanation and never did.
“ Ilex ” writes :
As a set-off against “ E.G.B.’s" experience (8., 16/5/’O3)
of the treatment of Vic. State school teachers, let me
relate what occurred not 12 months ago. A teacher in
charge of an important school in a mining township made
himself so objectionable generally that some of the
parents interested wrote complaints to the Department.
The correspondent of the alleged Board of Advice refused
to allow his own daughter to attend the school owing to
some improper questions which the teacher had put to
her. One morning the ped. turned up at school with a
damaged countenance which he accounted for by
announcing to the pupils that he had. unintentionally,
collided with a door in the dark, said door being in
reality a man’s fist which had (intentionally) collided
with his face during the progress of a drunken brawl in
the street. Subsequently the ped. was an unwilling
guest of His Majesty, the result of his having insulted
some women in the street. Valueless paper bearing his
name was all over the town. The inspector was sent up,
and he found the school records in complete disorder.
The delinquent was brought before the P.S. Board, but
he wasn’t sacked, or made a “temporary” like “E.G.B.’s”'
acquaintance. He was put in charge of another school
and made to step down one class. He had not such a
long service as the other either, but —he was Grand
Jumbuck of the Order of Foodie Fum, and he had in-
fluence. The Board of Advice correspondent afore-
mentioned was a brother Foodie Fum, which fact had its
importance too.

Once more the casual Australian. Two
youths went fishing in Sydney harbor and onefeil
in and was drowned before his companion's eyes.
The survivor trudged off home, had his tea, and
then mention d to his mother that his friend had
“fallen in. ’ His mother naturally inquired if he
got his clothes wet, and the son assured her that
he did. A day or two after a policeman called on
the woman, in his search for the missing lad.
The boy was cross-examined, and then he supple-
mented his first remark with the news that when
his companion fell in he never came out again.
The loss of the steamer (Jaklands is ascribed
in some quarters to the fact that she was carrying
a large consignment of tombstones on top of her
coal cargo. Superstitious people object to tomb-
stones and argue that the steamer had encountered
worse storms than this one (she safely weathered
the Maitland gale) for 13 years, and it wasn’t till
the monuments came on board that she went
down. Practical people point out that monu-
ments are unwieldy cargo and liable to shift and
knock a hole in the hull. The most conspicuous
feature of the disaster was its suddenness.
Apparently a big accident of some kind happened
all at once in the interior of the vessel, and the
tombstones are suspected.

Two cases were heard in a Sydney Police
Court last week on one day. One was that of a
book-keeper at a city drapery establishment,
through whose hands thousands of pounds a year
passed, and who was short in his books for some
hundreds. The other was that of the accountant
of a firm of middlemen, whose signature was
unhesitatingly accepted to large cheques. These
unfortunates,'the book-keeper and the accountant,
each received 255. a week, on which hideous
remuneration they had to maintain the dignity of
their positions, hide their misery, and keep up
appearances commensurate with their bosses
incomes—not their own incomes. Is there any-
thing more abject than the dignified, pot-hatted
office serf of a rich trader ?

THE GIN GIN ESTATE FARMS
11,000 ACRES.
Will be told by PUBLIC AUCTION, by order ef the Trustees in the settlements ef

THE GIN GIN ESTATE is situated on the Mt. Perry Railway Line about 23 miles
from Bundaberg, Queensland, about hours train from Brisbane, with a frontage of
over 3 miles to the railway line and about miles to the fresh waters of the Burnett
River.
THE GIN GIN ESTATE which has long been coveted by Agriculturists has
now been SU3D9VIDED into 103 FARMS
of various areas, well watered and drained and containing SOME «f the REST
AGRICULTURAL LAND in this already famous District.
THE SOIL CONSISTS OF LIGHT AND EASILY WORKED BLACK AND
CHOCOLATE VOLCANIC LOAMS and by analysis has been PROVED EQUAL
TO, AND IN MANY CASES SUPERIOR TO THAT OF THE CELEBRATED
DARLING DOWNS with the additional attraction of A BETTER RAINFALL
due to its proximity to the sea eoast.
CLEARING WILL COST PRACTICALLY NOTHING as the estate has nearly
all been ringbarked for 14 years and you can start ploughing right away.
CROPS OF ALL CEREALS, SUGAR, POTATOES and ONIONS grow in
profusion and for general farming and dairying the land is unsurpassed.
MAROONDAN RAILWAY STATION IS RIGHT ON THE LAND and
allotments in the TOWNSHIP OF MAROONDAN will be sold immediately after
the farms.
The Gin Gin Central Sugar Mill (which cost £55,G00) is elose to the Farms, also
a Creamery and a Butter Factory.

Instalments with interest at 4 per cent.

Saved a Leg.
A Queensland man suffering from Blood
Poisoning narrowly escapes losing his leg.
Mr. Arthur F. Muston, suffering from blood
poisoning which would not yield to external
remedies, and who feared he was going to die,
tells of the benefit he derived from taking l)r.
Morse’s Indian Root Pills. He writes:—“l
consider it a duty that you should know, also
the public, of the benefit I have derived from
taking Dr. Morse’s Indian Root Pills. Some two
years ago I was in Townsville, Queensland,
suffering from blood poisoning that caused a
running sore to develop in my right leg which
would not yield to external remedies. I was in
the Rockhamption General Hospital, and later in
the Mackay Hospital, and had an operation per-
formed, and at one time feared I would lose my
leg. About this time Dr. Morse’s Indian Root
Pills came under my notice, and I began taking
them regularly. I was very weak, and thought
I was going to die. After taking them for about
three weeks I noticed a great improvement. I
became more cheerful, and felt quite another man,
the sere rapidly healed, and to-day I am as sound
as a bell. I can safely recommend Dr. Morse’s
Indian Root Pills. In my case I tried several
doctors and numerous other remedies without
getting substantial benefit. There seemed to be
something about the pills that drove the poison
entirely out of my system. I am very thankful
that the pills came under my notice, and I cannot
speak too highly of their blood-purifying and
healing properties.” Dr. Morse’s Indian Root
Pills purify the blood and cleanse the system of
all its impurities. They are a permanent and a
positive cure for biliousness, constipation, and all
liver and kidney disorders. Packed in amber

SPORTING NOTIONS.
The export of interesting racehorses to England
and India gives the Australian turf the appearance of a
trial ground for the information of foreign buyers. Two
out of last four winners of the A.J.O. Derby have left the
country, also three out of the last seven Melb. Cup
winners, and one out of the last two Maribyrnong Plate
champions. The latest Caulfield Cup victor has not gone
yet, though he was said to be leaving some time ago, but
Great Scot, the Australian Cup horse, sailed for Bombay
last week.
« * » *
Sport is booming nowhere as it is booming in
Westralia. The two goldfields clubs, Boulder and Kal-
goorlie, are competing keenly. Boulder Club, as here
stated, made about £3OOO profit for the year ; an equal
amount is now to be put into a stand. The Kalgoorlie
Club, on receipts of £19,738 for three meetings, made
£4610 clear, besides spending £4752 on the course. All
prizes go up still higher for next season.
Framed receipt in Dan. O’Brien’s Criterion
hotel, New Plymouth (M.L.), sets forth that Messrs.
Hunter and Nolan received £651 “for the bay colt by
Musket from Mersey,” afterwards known as Carbine.
*‘* * *
Cabled information regarding betting and
“form” of Rock Sand pointed to him as the English
“ Darby ” winner. This is the second time Rock Sand’s
owner has scooped the same event—previous occasion
was 13 years ago, when Sainfoin won. Rock Sand has
already gathered a fortune, and, judging by engage-
ments ahead, looks like adding materially to the sum
piled up. Last year R.S. won £7474, and since has closed
on something in excess of £ll,OOO.
* * * *
Australian Trotting Association’s method of
probing apparently “ stiff ” running certainly has the
merit of originality, and would prove the right thing in
some instances. At recent meeting on Richmond (Vic.)
racecourse, stewards were doubtful about the genuine-
ness of favorite Honora’s shaping, whereupon they tried
her out themselves in company with two other nags—
one trotting and t’other galloping. Result, no action
taken.
* ♦ » *
Haut Star—winner of principal event at A.J.C.
meet Saturday—is the hag which won big race at Moore-
field Saturday before, and which, the owner complained an
hour or so previously' to handicapper Quinlan, was being
unreasonably heavily weighted. Getting away with the
two plums at two successive meetings isn’t by any means
a bad performance for an overburdened horse.
A. J.G. authorities have at last been galvanised
into life. The devious practices of jockeys and trainers
had become so aggressively 7 glaring that a blind man
could feel them. In last race at Moorefiela, Saturday
week, actions of one runner were noticed by hundreds
who thought, surely, the stipendiaries would get to work,
but they didn’t. However, last Saturday’s A.J.C. meet-
ing saw stewards of the day (not stipendiary) alert.
Graffnell’s showing in the hurdles caused his jockey’s (P.
Fallon) disqualification for 12 months; and trainer (M.
Thompson) and jockey (J. Brooks), connected with crack
mare Australia, who won Princess May Stakes, were
“sent up” for two years because of the nag’s inconsistent
displays. Two weeks before Australia finished fifth in
Tatt’s. Flying Handicap. Pity of all this is that rarely
are the right people punished. Trainers and jockeys
have to get their living, and consequently must do as
they are told.
* * * *
First of two days’ A.J.C. P. of W. Birthday
meet, Saturday, had bleak, showery weather, and punters
again beat Mo badly, every winner carrying a load of
coin—two were absolute first favorites, and other three
lined up only a “point” less in demand. Hurdles went
to VVootton’s Bizet after an interesting race all the way,
with Merry Jest and Jaques second and third. This is
perhaps the third time Jaques has been genuinely backed
and, though capable of doing better, he has invariably
disappointed his party. In Novice Handicap, one mile
(for nags that had not won a prize exceeding 50sovs. and
with apprentices up), only Highflyer and Lord Cardigan
were invested upon with any spirit, and they had the
finish to themselves. Kogarah-trained and owned High-
flyer triumphed “ on the post,” a head to the good.
Although promoting body 7 offered 200sovs. for First
Steeple, only four nags stripped and but two finished ;
which goes to prove how poor N.S.W. is in leppers.
Signet won handily, with Monarch second. Big run on
Australia—almost at “the death”—sent her out a hot
first choice for Princess May Stakes (6fur.), and she won
from 18 others, but only through jock, on Speculum 11.
being caught napping. Majority of the starters were
well backed. Ellesmere Stakes (ljm.) closed the pro-
gramme. Fireclay, Haut Star, Naphro, Canvee and
Dromedary found most support in a field of 15. Dis-
appointing Milner led till entering the straight and then
“chucked it” palpably. Down to the judge’s box Haut
Star and Circe were at high pressure, and former, lasting
longest, won half a neck best.
Adelaide Racing Club’s Birthday meeting
opened at Victoria Park, Saturday. Though weather
was fine, attendance didn’t reach expectations. Tote
investments (£11,575) showed a falling off of £2OOO com-
pared with same day last year. Remarkable jumping
success (Corral) of recent S.A.J.C. meet fell and Sarasati
won, beating favorite (Cynig) easily. City Handicap
went to Stuart King, who, as a yearling, was bought for
60gs. and subsequently won Fulham Park Plate —first
2yr. old race of the season. Present owner (Mr. C. Smith)
paid 300g8. for Stuart King, who is a gelding. Emilene
secured the Steeple (about 2m.), without being fully ex-
tended, in fast time of 3min. 59Jsec. Carlyon-Lady Noris
nag St. Vincent and Aurate disputed favoritism till
eleventh hour (when weight of money sent latter out
most in demand) for Adelaide Stakes (7fur.), and they ran
first and second as mentioned. Issue was never in doubt.
St. Vincent got home easily and with something in hand.
Winner humped the steadier of 9.10 ; he is full brother to
Trowbridge, among the best of last year’s early per-
formers. Royal Artillery and Bombastes are half-brothers
of St. Vincent. Heavily supported Ballarat-prepared filly
Sweet Nell, of whom much was expected, went under to
outsider Combat in Park side Stakes (lm. lfur.) by half a
length. Combat paid £l3 10s., which indicates how poor
his prospects were considered.

Cross-country rider B. Whalley sustained a
dual fracture of the left arm through Isis’ fall on
Saturday ; this after an enforced spell from the game
since September last, when Monarch’s cropper in the
first steeple of A. J.C. Spring meet caused the breaking of
an arm.
* * ♦ *
Bot fly brought about the death of trotting
stallion Globe (Huon—Lucilla), at Forbes (N.S.W.)
t’other day. Among his get were Globe Junior, Victor,
Singer, The Gift, Globette, Olaf, etc. Owners refused
£4OO for Globe a little time before they lost him.
* * * •
Crack little cross-country nag Frantic 11. had
his last jump in Steeple at Randwick, Saturday, when,
through misjudging take off, he struck both stifles on
logs opposite judge’s box and came a heavy cropper,
sliding yards on his shoulders. Subsequent investigation
disclosed a shattered stifle and fractured spine, which
necessitated his destruction. Latest owner (Mr. W.
Anderson) only got Frantic recently, and since then he
won S.T.C. ’Chase. Said that £250 was, a day or two ago,
refused for the nag. Rider (Burtenshaw) had his collar-
bone broken. Same event was responsible for another
casualty. Two jumps further than that which brought
Frantic down saw Isis (after lepping doubtfully so far)
turn a complete somersault.
• * • •
At the recent Tweed River races The Cardinal
ran first in the Two-y ear-old Race, with Dill Macky second.
* * * *
“ Tot ” Glasscock, of Kirk’s Bazaar, Melbourne,
died last week. Years ago, “Tot” was one of the best
of professional-amateur steeplechase jockeys, and it was
then that he acquired the beginnings of the lung com-
plaint which carried him off at 42.
Q.T.C. meet ended Saturday. Tas.-owned and
Sydney-trained Ivan won Moreton Handicap (lim.)
Among 19 starters were Contest, Bridegroom, Ilderim,
Amber and Heronry. Atop of two previous successes at
the meeting Grafton-Olive Branch colt, Fitz-Grafton,
proved too much for the opposition in the Royal Stakes
(Im.) which included Balfour, Whitebait, Purser and
Bridegroom. Prior wins were Stradbroke Handicap and
Claret Stakes.
» * * »
Dear Bulletin.— Re current spieler argument.
There are two seats —one in Hyde Park and one in Outer
Domain —known amongst domainiacs as the “ Spielers’
Rests.” To avoid being seen together in the streets,
every morning a number of well-known spielers, mags-
men, confidence-men and decoys assemble there to
confer and draw plans and specifications for their day’s
work. Judging by their appearance, the Sydney spielers
are a poor, ragged, filthy 7, dissipated-looking lot, who
exhibit little outward financial signs of “two-up” or
skill of any kind. They seem to be starving more than
living on the “game.” Meanness and cowardice are
written on every' line of their vile faces. How they can
gain the confidence of anyone is past this writer’s com-
prehension. Yet to hear them brag, one would think
they could show points to Dante the Great and Oinque-
valli in the now-you-see-it-now-you-don’t line. If their
professional brothers out-back can’t show better results
for their alleged skill and cleverness—well, spieling isn’t
the game it’s cracked up to be, and little better than
bar-lunch snatching.— The Domainiac.
* * * *
“ Battler ” : Some wild men from Melbourne
have started a Maoriland Association of Australian Foot-
ball at Christchurch. A peaceful park nearly became a
crimson battle-field when the new ball-bouncers had
their opening practice. They casually' stole the ground
of a party of shin-padded, chest-protected, long-nailed
Rugbyites, and seemed surprised when these latter
bruisers came on the scene, gritting their teeth and pre-
paring to rend the trespassers asunder. The Mel-
bournians left. They' have since invited the natives to
join the pacific association, but there’s not enbugh blood
and skin flying in the new game to tempt the fierce and
gore-spattered youth of M.L.
* *
The proposed M.L. Rugby football team for
England hasn’t got over the expenses portion of the tour
yet. There’s a deal of opposition to the idea, and the
Imperialism of Seddon doesn’t enter into sport ;
keener interest is manifested in the premiership
contests than the English tour. Still a team (and a
good one, too) could be raised if only some kind of a
guarantee could be obtained. Last year some supporters
of the game promised over £2OOO towards expenses ; but
somehow none of them left his address, and this modesty
is the real cause of the deadlock.
* * *
Sydney Evening News the other day published,
as a joke, a mock-serious account of a brutal football
match. It was obviously intended to satirise Sydney
D.T.’s silly descriptions of boxing matches. But the
paper joked with such difficulty that the article was
taken seriously, and the office was invaded by' a host of
footballers, barrackers, and other influential personages
demanding the writer’s scalp. The News, therefore,
apologised at great length, and explained that its report
was intended in a purely' jocular spirit, and that it never
dreamt of giving offence, etc. But the News hasn’t yet
apologised for its yarns about strike outrages, nor has
the D.T. for its more frenzied fictions on the subject. An
honest worker struggling for his rights may be insulted
with impunity in Australia, but not a footballer.
* * * *
Fitzroy (Yic.) - N.S.W. toeball match (Aus-
tralian rules) on Sydney Cricket Ground, in bleak
weather and rain showers, last week, proved heaps more
interesting than did much bigger Fitzroy v. Collingwood
struggle on same ground previous Saturday for Victorian
premiership. This was because onlooking 2000 could enter
into the rivalry 7, and also through N.S.W. making a far
better showing than was anticipated by the most
sanguine. At one period N.S.W. had 7 goals to
Fitzrov’s 6, and the crowd ’thused accordingly, but this
scribe’s view was that Fitzroys didn’t reach out over
much—they looked to be aiming at supply ing an attrac-
tive show more than an exhibition of what could be done
with the opposing team. All the same, however,
N.S.W.’s shaping impressed very much, considering Aus-
tralian rules have only been revived in Sy'dney a couple
of months. Three-quarters time saw less than a goal
difference between the combinations, but ultimately
match finished Fitzroy 8 goals 22 behinds (70 points),
N.S.W. 7 goals 5 behinds (47 points).

The growth of “Empire.” The much-coveted
first century ” in English cricket falls this season to
B. Jaya Ram, a Madras “colored pusson,” in a fixture
London County v. Eighteen of Sydenham. Slowly the
dark blot grows on England’s athletic world, and an
English cricket team in future must assuredly find p'aoes
for a few Hindus, at any rate. Last season W. G. Grace
scooped the honor, but in this match he notched 40 runs ;
and erstwhile Australian Poidevin scored a similar quota.
* * * *
Broken Hill—where demon trundler Jones
hailed from—unearthed about the best country batsman
for the year. Clem Wellington (or, as he is known on
the Barrier Ranges, “the Barrier Trumper”) smashed
all records held in that district in very short time, and
closed the season with an average of 157"5 per innings,
the highest score being 238 not out. When it is noted
that the performances were put up on slate and concrete
wickets, with scarcely a blade of grass on the awfully
uneven playing area, the brilliance of the batting
becomes more apparent.

Adelaide being the strongest lacrosse centre in
Australia, and the University the backbone of the game
in Adelaide, something of a sensation has been caused
by the defeat of the students—9 to o—by0—by Sturt, a nearly
new club. N.S.W. and Victoria both play on the Ade-
laide Oval at the end of June. As a sign of the game’s
progress, the S.A. Cricket Association, which controls
the Oval, now issues its w'inter fixture-list as “Pro-
gramme of Football and Lacrosse.”

“Ye olde Englyshe game of bowls ” has been
making rapid strides lately in Brisbane, and an asso-
ciation is being formed, having for its principal
object the selection of teams to do battle with those of
the southern States, and the arranging of such contests.
Three rinks from Maryborough, composed largely of
veteran play'ers, visited Brisbane at the end of May, and
defeated Booroodabin Club by 8 points, but were defeated
by the South Brisbane Bowling Club (a new club whose
green was only opened in December last) by 10 points.
The victory 7 of the S.B. B. Club w'as an unexpected event,
and deserving of every praise. The Maryborough men
w'ere at a slight disadvantage in the game against 5.8.,
as regards the green, which W'as very keen, and totally
different in its play from that of the Booroodabin Club,
on w'hich the visitors had previously played. One of the
games played between Maryborough and S.B. was remark-
able for the small number of points scored—a game of 21
heads resulting in a total of only 28 points—l 2 to Mary-
borough and 16 to S.B.
» * *
“ Ike : Stated in B. (23/5/’O3) that the short
rowing boat has come to stay on the strength
of the fact that all scullers now 7 use ’em. Accord-
ing to recent trials in Melbourne, it will be a
long time before eights and fours of the Neilson
type will be universally used in Australia. Time tests
and observation of experienced oarsmen, to say nothing
of ignominious position of both short boats in recent
inter - State contest, have decided strongly against
them. The idea of the designer is that ’ the boat
travels faster over the top of the water than through
it. All very well as long as the boat stays on
top, but whereas in sculling the peculiar ‘ slide-first ’
style does not bury the nose of the boat, and it is
possible to keep ‘ punching her ’ along the top of the
water, in rowing the direct weight of bodies over the
slides and the swing of the 8 bodies together buries both
nose and stern of the boat, unless provided for by
greater support fore and aft in the water, which short
boats cannot give without undue width and consequent
resistance.
World’s sculling champion Geo. Towns hasn t,
so far, responded to Dick Tressider’s challenge beyond
pleading that domestic and business exactions preclude
the possibility' of training y r et awhile ; also the getting of
the side bet is a difficulty not easily surmounted. Hinted
(only) that the “top sawyer” of them all may name a
date in coming Spring. Probably there’ll be no race in-
side six months and possibly none at all ; yet ’twas
authoritatively stated that if Tressider beat Pearce,
Towns would be on hand immediately to make a match.
Not a few ascribe Towns’ hesitancy to knowledge of what
he could do with Pearce compared with what he saw
Tressider do opposed to the same man.
* * * *
“ Old Timer ” : The Clarence (N.S.W.) is again
to the fore with a rower (J. Philp) who wants to meet
Tressider in heavy boats for £lOO aside. Mick Rush —a
great man in the seventies and physically superior to most
we have had in same line, learned his sculling as he rowed
a butcher’s boat about that same river. “Pros” Coulon,
who promised to surprise the world but died (ay’oung man)
of swamp fever nearly 30 years ago, lived on the Clarence.
Donald M’Donald, Elias Laycock (who scooped the big
Hop Bitters prize in England) and Harry Searle all hailed
from the same locality.
• * * *
Our old friend, Professor Miller, the champion
athlete with the splay feet and inelegant figure, is off to
England, intending to end his days there. It seems a
mistake for this native Australian and representative
muscular citizen of Melbourne to seek an uncomfortable
change of climate in a country where he hasn’t any
records —but he’s off, any'how. Lord Mayor Gillott,
Purves, K. 0., and a mob of old admirers gave the
swarthy Prof, a smoke-night. They talked of the times
when he wrestled Donald Dinnie and Clarence Whistler,
the American inventor of the “ hammerlock,” who cele-
brated his victory over the Australian champion by T
chewing champagne glasses. The Bulletin will always
remember Miller by Hop.’s illustrations of a wrestling
match in Sydney between the huge Professor and the
comparatively small Mons. Victor. The terms of the con-
tract required Miller to throw Victor ten times, or more,
in an hour, which he duly did. Hop.’s pictures were
drawn from the point of view of a sympathetic artist, who
didn’t doubt that Victor would get thrown, but was
wondering where the big man would throw him to.
* * * *
Second meeting of Sydney Kensington Coursing
Club, last week, proved more of an inter-State character
than many Australian Water'oo Cups. Big event (Ken-
sington Stakes) fell to Victorian owned and trained The
Hermit, who never gave whilom N.S.W. champion,
Braddon’s Blot, a hope. The Hermit shaped promisingly
all through last season in Victoria, and this year—though
beaten by Royal Fusileer at opening meeting of Kensing-
ton C.C. —met same hound again in latest event and
turned the tables completely. In Orange Cup, a few
weeks ago, The Hermit won three courses, and then
succumbed to the brilliant Rooty Hill performer, Great
Heart, in an exciting tussle. Runner-up (Braddon’s
Blot) had a narrow squeak with winner’s kennel-mate,
w r ho stumbled at a critical moment. Orange-owmed
puppy Cannongate made his first appearance in public
and secured Puppy event (meeting Victorian bitch, Nar-
row Escape, in the decider) so cleverly' that he should be
worth watching. Similarly owmed Lavender won the
Novice Stakes well. Feature of second day’s proceedings
was the large number of heavily-backed dogs that went
down. No less than seven of eight favorites, in first
round of Novice event, were beaten.
* * * *
Kensington Coursing Club has adopted Ameri-
can idea of weighing dogs before each course. Until con-
tending hounds are placed in charge of a responsible
official in kennels at the slips—say an hour before w'anted
to run—weighing the dogs is the only possible check on
crooked work, as by' this means the big “ feed ” or drink
necessary to hamper a hound’s movements can be easily
detected. On first day (Thursday 7) The Hermit, prior to
his initial run, scaled 62£lbs. and later in day weighed
exactly the same. Friday saw him open up 631b5., and,
though running twice subsequently, he never varied an
oz. Cannongate, before each of his three wins on Friday,
pulled the beam at 65£, 65J, and 651bs.
» * » *
Probably the shortest fight held under modern
conditions in Australia took place on Saturday night in
the Queen’s Hall, Melb., when Dick Kernick knocked out
heavyweight Joe Cullen in one round. The fight didn’t
last the full 180 seconds by' a long way. The men had
hardly come together before Kernick punched Cullen
twice quickly with his left, and disconcerting him with
these, shot in a heavy right that took his point
and finished him. The actual fighting apparently did
not exceed ten seconds, and certainly the time occupied
by the counting out of Cullen seemed longer than the
period of combat. Kernick is a big powerful fellow, 6ft.
high, and well proportioned. A few years ago he made
his first appearance in the ring, when he made a clever
showing. His aversion to punishment W'as said to be
responsible for his lack of success then.
* * * *
“Soldier” Jack Thompson and Arthur Cripps
(holder of the title) are matched to fight again, July 14,
for middle-weight supremacy of the continent. Thomp-
son has little chance of turning the tables, except by a
fluke, which is unlikely with a cool, methodical boxer
like Cripps. When Thompson challenged, he expressed
readiness to find any side-wager Cripps might be pre-
pared to cover, but the time to put money up found
Thompson without even a “ fiver ” to bet, hence ’tis
strange that the second match was made. It will, how-
ever, prove exciting, and there’ll probably be many who’ll
give Thompson a “good chance” of retrieving lost
credentials.

M.L. Boxing Association has intimated that
its team for Australasian amateur championships in
Sy'dney during September will be made up of premiers in
feather, light, middle, and heavy divisions, and probably
a bantam rep. may be sent along. Victoria and Queens-
land will also send champions, consequently the gathering
promises to prove ahead of anything the continent has
yet known in amateur boxing. At M.L. Association’s
annual meeting last month it was told that branches had
been established at Wei ington and Wanganui and were
being formed at Auckland. Nelson, and Dunedin. First
competitions for local championships were exceptionally
successful and realised £lO4 clear profit.

and effeot that odds of 2to 1 were laid on him Fra*,
used a heavy right, but Higgins merely shook his S
and kept administering heavy body punishment narti™
larly over the kidneys. It proved an even thin® from
the fifth to the ninth, when successive thumps
dazed Fraser and he was very much second-best «uh« 7
quently though his showing was plucky enough Th.
13th round brought the end. Three times Fraser bumnert
the boards hard, and on the third occasion he
prone—done completely. u

The details in connection with the DunW
Inter-Club Road Premiership, run under the auspices
the N.S.W. Cyclists’ Union, comprise a very comS®
sive series of scratch racing for the winter season Thr
courses have been decided on : the usual road race on
from Homebush to Granville and back twice distant
about 20 miles ; from foot of Gore Hill, North Svdnev t
Pymble Railway Gates and back twice, distance ahn.u
22J miles ; and a course starting from near the toll bar
Randwick Road going towards La Perouse and taking in .
circuit with Long Bay and Maroubra, distance about m
miles. An entry from all the Metropolitan Clubs il
anticipated and the keenest enthusiasm is being shewn
as not only does the competition carry with it the title of
Road Premiers for the year, but also the holding of the
Dunlop shield and gold medals for individual riders in the
teams. At the conclusion of the team championship an
additional race will be run for the individual road
championship of the year—the winner carrying the title
of Road Champion and also championship medal worth
£5 55., presented by Dunlop Tyre Company. Amateur
circles are now alive with men in active training and the
promotion of a competition of this nature bids fair to
create that healthy rivalry which is so necessary towards
the promotion of road sport.*
* * * *
People who take an interest in cycle matters
will learn with regret that the Dunlop Tyre Co. has had a
dispute with the League of N.S.W. Wheelmen, which has
Caused the company to abandon this year its now famous
road race from Goulburn to Sydney. Whenever the
matter of money is introduced into a sport, a great propor-
tion of the public begin to look upon that sport with a
sinister eye, and to cast aspersions on the reliability of
time-tests and records. The first question is “Was the
record bought or how was it obtained?” It is on this
point that the dispute has arisen, and the strangest part
of the business is that the League, which is avowedly
organised for the purpose of maintaining the honor
of the sport, has sought to coerce the Dunlop
Tyre Co. into paying a substantial sum to the
referee, timekeepers, starters, and other Leivue
officials who attended the attempts of F. °S
Beauchamp to lower records. The company refused
to pay anything but nominal fees, on the ground that the
League officials should act in an honorary capacity where
their services are employed in connection with records
The League met the co.’s refusal to pay a sub-
stantial sum by refusing to grant sanction for the
proposed Dunlop road race unless the co. first paid the
“records” money. The co. thereupon stated its
ultimatum in these words: “We must, however,
impress upon you (the League of Wheelmen) that we
positively decline to pay your officials one single farthing,
and consider that, acting as they are supposed to do in an
honorary capacity, they should neither ask for nor accept
any payment."

A musical and dramatic agency has been started
at Argent Chambers, Hunter-street, Sydney, by H.
Musgrove and Co. (Harry Musgrove, long' business
manager for J. C. Williamson and Geo. Musgrove), who
are prepared to negotiate theatrical engagements,
arrange for tours, concerts, &c., and attend generally to
all kinds of matters musical and dramatic.*
The Bulletin has received a tiny and tasteful
booklet, describing a visit to the famous South Australian
vineyards of Penfold and Co. (started in 1844 by the late
Dr. Penfold, with the view of producing a first-class wine
for the use of his patients). An interesting account is
given of the wine-making operations there carried on
upon a gigantic scale, views of the vineyards are given,
and the celebrated cellars are fully described and illus-
trated.
Kodak Kodoid Plates are the latest novelty
introduced by Kodak Limited, 284 Collins street, Mel-
bourne. Claimed for these new flat films that, while
lighter, thinner, and safer than glass plates, they are
nearly 20 per cent, cheaper than any other cut films.
They are also very rapid, with a very fine-grained emul-
sion. The K.K.P. consist of a light, unbreakable film
which is attached at the edges by clips to a card support,
and they are so devised that they can be used in
ordinary plate holders or sheaths wherever glass plates
can be used, and in exactly the same way. They are
much thinner than the average cut film, and can there-
fore be printed from either side, and they do not curl in
drying.*

Jenkins Iscariot, of S. A., who has hitherto
done well out of the powerful Methodist vote, is
falling from grace. One of the most precious
Methody institutions is the “tea and public
meeting ”; and the person who, having taken
part in the worldly tea, dodges the spiritual after-
meeting, marks himself as among the lost. This
is what Jenkins did the other day at the biggest
of such gatherings; whereupon the high priest
accompanied his regrets by the remark that if
such proceedings tired the Premier they would
provide a stretcher for him in the future.

NOTICE TO CONSTITUENTS,
THE Sydney Office of The Australasian Mort-
gage and Agency Company, Limited, has
received cable advice from its Head Office in
Edinburgh that the Scottish Courts approved on
the 26th May, 1903, of the Company’s Scheme of
Reorganisation and its proposals for the future
carrying on of its business. The Company has
accordingly been taken out of liquidation and its
selling and general business, which has suffered
no interruption during the liquidation proceedings,
will be continued as usual, as will also its “Trust
A/C ” with the Bank of Australasia for the re-
ception of Constituents’ proceeds.
B. B. ALLEN, Manager.
Offices, 43 Pitt-street; Produce Stores, Circular
Quay ; Wool Stores, Central Wharf.

“ Dyckolt ” on a personal matter :
Was rather amused when a corresp. (B. 16/5/’O3) let
himself loose upon “ Scotty the Wrinkler,” and called
the much-travelled “ Scot ” a “flat,” and a “ mug',’’ and a
mere “ bush authority,” who knows nought of the tricks
of the games of chance, and the wily ways of the
chevalier d’industrie. Fancy teaching gambling and its
points and tricks to the man who (as I know) has
gambled on the ’Murkan Mississippi and Missouri steam-
boats, who was wounded in New Orleans (U.S) when
Billy Ardmore shot Shan M'Laughlin in the big Faro-
Bank ; who has seen the “elephant” in Morrissey’s in
New York 1 Also, who has played hard and heavy 8£
years in British Army messes ; who bucked at roulette in
Suez, Cairo, Alexandria and Marseilles. Likewise a man
who was tried for calling Dudley Persse, of the 18th, a
damned blackguard in Gibraltar, when he refused to pay
£640 “ Scotty ” had won ! A man who played and
gambled at every mentionable game all over India and
Abyssinia; who was present in the Melbourne Club,
when and fleeced the new-chums many
years ago! &c., &c. Let your correspondent read this
and weep, and promise himself faithfully never to do it
again.

About the Suttors :
The original George Sutter —as then spelled—came out
in the year 1800 in the ship Porpoise. He was the son of
a farmer and gardener, renting land from Lord Cadogan,
at the place now known as Chelsea. He brought with him
certain plants and a promise of a land grant. He took
part with Governor Bligh, and suffered six months
imprisonment for his loyalty. He went home as a
witness for Bligh at the trial of Colonel Johnson. He
built a house in Sydney at a cost of £2OOO, where it is
said that Allan Cunningham lived with him, and
Leichhardt was a frequent visitor. In 1814 he
was appointed superintendent of the lunatic asylum at
Castle Hill, but did not retain the position long.
Mr. Suttor is known in Australian history as
the first professional gardener to arrive in the colony.
In 1843 he published a volume on “The Cultivation of
the Vine and Olive in Australia.” His third son, William
Henry Suttor, father of the present Suttors, William
Henry and F. 8., married a Miss Frances and went over
the Blue Mountains at the age of 16. For years the
Suttors considered Bathurst their pocket borough.
Old William Henry Suttor, senior, was privileged
to read his own obituary notice—he was reported
as dead, and S.M. Herald came out with a column.
Alloway Bank, his home on the Macquarie, was inundated
with letters and telegrams (with mileage added) of sym-
pathy and condolence. Mr. Suttor spent £lO in answer-
ing these communications, sent in his bill to the Herald
and promptly got a cheque for the amount.

Re that much-talked-about economy by
the cutting down of the Vic. train service. No
sooner was the secret settlement arrived at than
various keen supporters of the Iceberg Govern-
ment began laying siege to Commissioner Fitz-
patrick for a resumption of the services to their
districts on the old basis, and pulling the strings
through their friends in the Ministry. What a
glorious opportunity presents itself for the lee-
bergers to work the “spoils to the victors
dodge ! The districts which return Bent’s sup-
porters will soon be getting their trains as usual,
but the ones which refuse to vote the Kyabram
ticket will probably get a mighty poor service for
many a day, unless they think better of it and
start approving of the Irvine-Bent with great
enthusiasm.
Apropos of George Reid’s recent assur-
ances that, if he becomes Federal Premier, he
won’t dream of imposing direct taxation, this is a
clause from the published programme of the
“Freetrade Association,” under whose auspices
Reid rushed into the Federal fray :
That the first Federal tariff should, as far as the exi-
gencies of the several States will permit, embrace a
number of duties somewhat similar to those in Great
Britain, with the intention of as soon as possible estab-
lishing a free breakfast table, any deficiency of revenue
in such latter case to be made up by direct taxation.
The mighty Wobbler was elected on a policy of
Federal direct taxation, and sits for a seat which
he won on that policy, though he is against that
policy now —at least, he is against it in Protec-
tionist Tasmania and for it in Freetrade Sydney,
and in other places he totters indistinctly on one
leg.
Item from Sydney Telegraph :
Over a very wide number of articles it may be said that
whether duties are 12J per cent, or 25 percent. makes
very little difference to the Treasury, on the principle
that, roughly, half the goods will be imported at the
higher rate which would be imported at the lower one.
It takes some time for local manufacturers to supply the
deficiencies caused by restricted imports, but they do so
eventually, and it is then that a highly protective tariff
becomes unproductive.
Again the beautiful Freetrade doctrine? Let us
keep down local manufactures and local employ-
ment and wages and skill, so that we may buy lots
of things from the foreigner, and then by taxing
ourselves on these things (in addition to paying
the foreigner for them), and paying ourselves the
said taxes, we may have money in the Treasury.

In the Sydney Domain, recently, a perfect
lady—garbed in a green satin bodice, a gashed
and tattered skirt, and a quaint hat —strode along
a path looking for fight. Met another lady, and
straightway accused her of stealing her silk stock-
ings, worth 10s., and, with hoots of defiance,
challenged her to hit out. While she was
skipping about the road and chopping the
air in an impressive way, the accused was
informing the audience that Madam Trollop was
a “butter stranger,’’ and that, though she was
herself a peaceable character, yet if she had two
beers she could flatten her accuser out. She
insinuated that if she had three beers she could
slay Madam Trollop with one blow. There was
a half-hour of angry speech with frequent calls
for the two beers, when a swamped-looking
mariner lurched up and investigated the case.
After hearing particulars he informed the
aspersed party that her honor was involved,
and that she must smite Madam Trollop in order
to clear her character. Thereupon, just when the
assailant lady least expected it, she received a
mighty biff on the lower part of the bodice, and
immediately dispersed and fled into the fast-
nesses of Woolloomooloo. Then the seaman told
the onlookers that it was a scandal, and lurched
on.

ALMOST
EVERY HUMAN ACHE
Is RHEUMATIC in its origin.
Rheumatism is caused by Uric Acid Poisoning.
Gout is caused by Uric Acid Poisoning.
Neuralgia is caused by Uric Acid Poisoning.
Lumbago is caused by Uric Acid Poisoning.
Sciatica is caused by Uric Acid Poisoning.
Blood Disorders are caused by Uric Acid Poisoning.
Gravel is caused by Uric Acid Poisoning.
Stone is caused by Uric Acid Poisoning.
Bladder Troubles are caused by Uric Aoid Poisoning.
WHAT IS URIC ACID ?
Uric Acid is one of the many products of the wasting of the body. Every moment we live the
body is wasting, and it is the duty of certain of our organs to remove the waste matter.
THE KIDNEYS.
To the kidneys is allotted a large portion of this task. The kidneys of the average person
and extract from the blood about three pints of urine every day. In this quantity of urine should be
dissolved about an ounce of urea, ten to twelve grains, in weight, of uric acid, together with other
animal and mineral matter varying from a third of an ounce to nearly an ounce. If the kidneys from
any cause are unable to do their work properly, some of these substances remain in the system, and
become actively poisonous. Uric acid is the most dangerous of these poisons. When it is deposited
in the joints and tissues, it produces Rheumatism, Lumbago, Sciatica or Gout. In other
cases the blood, being contaminated, is rendered unfit to absorb nutriment freely, and we suffer from
Anaemia, Neuralgia, Digestive Troubles, Blood Disorders, and Debility, Sometimes
the uric acid is deposited in the kidneys themselves, and then the frightful pain caused by Stone,
Gravel, and Bladder Troubles is experienced.
Thousands have kidney disorders, and suffer from uric acid poisoning, without knowing the
cause. The most common indication that there is anything wrong with the kidneys is
PAIN IN THE BACK.
A more certain test to make is, to place some urine, passed the first thing in the morning, in a
covered glass, and let it stand until the next morning, If it is then cloudy, shows a brick-dust like
sediment, if particles float about in it, or it is of an unnatural colour, then the kidneys are not
healthy, and no time should be lost in ensuring their restoration, or Bright’s Disease, Diabetes
or some less serious but more painful illness will be the result.
Warner’s Safe Cure.
About thirty years ago, Medical and Scientific men, realising the importance of the efficient
removal of uric acid, and other waste poisons from the system, made special research with the view
to discover a remedial agent. They realised the fact that it was necessary to discover a medicine
which would act specifically upon both the liver and the kidneys, as, when the kidneys are affected,
the liver suffers in sympathy, and vice versa. The outcome of their research was the medicine now
known throughout the world as Warner’s Safe Cure. This medicine possesses the desired property in
the fullest degree, and though over a quarter of a century has passed since it was first discovered, no
remedy has yet been developed of like power. It acts equally upon the kidney and liver, restoring
them to health and activity, when all urinary and biliary poisons are of necessity removed from the
system in a natural manner. Warner’s Safe Cure consequently cures Rheumatism, Gout, Back-
ache, Lumbago, Sciatica, Persistent Headache, Neuralgia, Gravel, Stone and Bladder
Troubles, Ansemia, Debility, Indigestion, Torpid Liver. Even Bright’s Disease yields
to treatment by Warner’s Safe Cure.
Mr. J. T. Castner, of 8 Victoria-street, Paddington, N.S.W., says : “Some time ago I was under
medical treatment for severe pains in the back. The doctor who attended me wanted me to undergo
the operation of crushing a stone in my bladder, but I did not like the idea, although the pain I
was suffering was severe. A friend advised me to try Warner’s Safe. Cure. I did, and, after taking
three or four bottles, I began to feel better. I continued the course, and, thanks to this medicine, I
became free from any trouble whatever.”
A treatise containing particulars relating to Warner’s Safe Cure, and the diseases it cures' will be
sent free by H. H. Warner and Co., Limited, Australasian Branch, Melbourne, Vic.

From some accounts, if the British
Government persists in its encouragement of the
importation of Asiatic coolies to the Rand mines,
there will be a white insurrection there. Jew-
hannesburg Tribune so prophesies, straightly and
N.S.W.’s first notable deficit was the
4 ortaf?e Cowper and Robertson in
lobo. Roth contended there was no deficit, but
the evidence was too strong for them. On the
strength of theexposure Martin and Foster headed
a new retrenchment Government, which promptly
produced a £200,000 shortage to begin with.
Victorian Parliamentary Committee on
railways, in its current report, condemns the
ingenious swindle by which the State Govern-
ments pay the duty on imported steel rails
with borrowed money, and then draw back from
*“ e Federal. Government three-fourths or more of
the duty paid (with borrowed money) and treat it
as revenue.
N.S.W. Treasurer Waddell forecasts for
the next session a Closer Settlement Bill, which
will burst up the big estates.” But its system
of purchase might also “burst up” the Treasury.
Why should not the State, as a preliminary,
resume some of the unearned increment by a pro-
gressive land tax ? The proceeds might be set
aside for repurchase of estates, by way of has-
tening the bursting up.
Rochester (Vic.) tries to run its municipal
business on a sixpenny rate; naturally typhoid
fever is rampant.. Typhoid in Australia is mostly
due to low municipal taxation in country centres.
There has, so far, been devised no means by which
a municipality can throw the work of garbage
collecting on the State Government, and what
the average country crowd can’t cadge on the
Government for, it neglects.
N.S.W. Government is said to be con-
sidering appointments to the Upper House.
There have been several deaths of Councillors
lately, and the Government is in a marked
minority just now. Any taxation proposals that
the necessity of doing without borrowed money
might call for would not have a chance in the
Upper House as now constituted. Possibly Pre-
mier See is thinking of doing without borrowed
money—though he doesn’t look like it.
Let it be pointed out, robustly and insist-
ently, that any sort of a Compulsory Arbitration
Act, such as that of Maoriland or N.S. Wales,
would have absolutely prevented the Victorian
railway strike with all its rage and hysteria. Any
union which ignored the Arbitration Court or
disobeyed its decisions could be struck by almost
illimitable damages. But the blind Victorian
Ministry has always been against such a peaceful
method of settling disputes—and on it lies the
responsibility of the trouble.
When Kingston, with a very moderate
Parliamentary majority behind him, and the
hostility of almost the whole city press and all the
forces of wealth and social influence to face,
enforced the law honestly against the swindling
importers of Australia, nothing was said about
his courage ; according to the press he was only a
pig-headed tyrant. And when Irvine, with a big
majority of the Assembly, all the Upper House,
almost all the papers, all the rich men of the
State, and all the landlord interest behind him,
put his hoof down on a cause that was already
lost, according to the same press his courage was
something enormous.
When the last Commonwealth elections
were on, Australia had not awakened to the fact
that it was possible to have as democratic a Senate
as House of Representatives, and the consequence
was that quite a number of dreary old dodderers
got into the Senate who would have had no possible
hope of getting into the Reps. The average Aus-
tralian was so accustomed to see the State Upper
Houses crowded with fossilised imbeciles and fat-
brained men whose sole qualification was the
possession of much cash, that they allowed some
perfectly avoidable evils to infest the Senate.
There is hope, however, that the ghastly mistake
won’t be repeated.
Judas Chamberlain, in October, 1899,
when stirring up feeling against the Boers with a
view to war and annexation :
The treatment of the natives of the Transvaal has been
disgraceful. It has been brutal. It has been unworthy
of a civilised Power.
Judas Iscariot Chamberlain (March, 1903), when
the Transvaal steal was finished, cynically ex-
plaining what a liar he had been :
The majority of the House were led to believe that the
treatment of the natives by the Boers was very bad . . .
these charges were exaggerated . . . the Boers must be
absolved from the charge of ill-treatment.
Sydney Sunday Retired Grocer is crying
for the suppression of “ agitators ” and points
admiringly to the expulsion of Tom Mann from
Belgium as a bright and shining example of
what ought to be done in Australia. This
suggestion is based on the fact that at present Mr.
Mann is “ going about the country sowing further
seeds of discord at public meetings and elsewhere,
and helping to keep the cauldron of ill-feeling on
the boil.” The Bulletin, for one, wouldn’t
mind so very much if Tom Mann were expelled,
likewise a dozen other agitators. The cause
would still go on, and the precedent would be
most valuable some day when the other crowd
wanted to expel the Freetrade agitator M'Millan,
the private railway agitator, the anti-land-tax and
anti-income-tax agitator, the Yellow Pup agitator,
the Kyabram agitator, and many others. A
precedent is sometimes a handy thing to have
about the house.
Kiwi writes :
It requires no great perceptive power to recognise in
an alleged Maoriland Farmers’ Union the old National
Ass with a new cover on. Seddon’s declaration that he
will next session introduce legislation to give preference
to Unionists has called forth a loud bray of dissent from
the bucolic animal, and it is also beginning to busy itself
about the land question. At a picnic held recently in
Rangitikei, under the auspices of the Union, one orator,
appropriately named Vile, launched out about the free-
hold being the inheritance of every Briton. The crowd
of busy bushwhackers cheered, as if the great mass of
Britons ever owned a piece of land until they got under-
neath the surface ! Another speaker, a real live capting,
implored the farmers to trust the Lord and fight for the
freehold. Which recalls the pious Cornishman who
prayed for a shipwreck before morning. Of course,
Trades Unionists get no quarter from these gentry. In
fact, my experience is that the average cocky has about
the same hatred for the non-landowning class as the
most purse-proud squatter. In a few years Maoriland
politics will degenerate into a fight between Unionism
and cockyism, and between the two Fatman will feel
more comfortable than he has for some time. Faction
has ever been a godsend to Reaction.

The decision of the North Mount Lyell Co.
(Tas.) to finally close down its smelters on May 30 has
made things serious for the dependent town of Crotty.
Crotty is composed almost entirely of North Lyell
workers and the attendant storekeepers, &c., and this
closing of the works means practically the removal of
everybody and the shutting up of everything.
* * » *
Island Eureka, Lake Austin (W.A.), in spite of
protestations that the mine was all right when it was
here stated that it was played out, has suspended divs.
for four months, while the last two crushings read 1260 z.
from 240 tons, and 2040 z. from 200 tons. Yet the direc-
tors were repeatedly assured that, from ore in sight,
*soooz. monthly from 25dwt. stone could be maintained
for the next three years. In June last year shareholders
were also told that the main shaft was to be sunk another
200 ft., and another 10-head of stamps, with a cyanide
plant, were to be purchased out of profits. A year has
passed and the promise is still unfulfilled. It is a signifi-
cant fact that the three local directors—Bates, Brook-
man_, and White —resigned in favor of Adelaide men ; and
it is more significant still that the adjoining
Golconda, which was working the same lode at
a deeper level, has decided, on the advice of
manager A. B. Martin, to abandon the property.
Since 1894 the Golconda has spent £57,250 of Buli’s
money, besides £53,000 from gold won, or £110,250, and
the only return in divs. has been £6750. Martin w'as in
charge of the Golconda before the Eureka was publicly
heard of, so should know the ground well. Eureka may
come again, if anything fresh is found, but, at present,
prospects are far from rosy.
* * • •
Mulwaree East (W. A.), a second reconstruction
of Menzies, Ltd., re-floated, in Aug., 1900, to take over
the deep levels of big John de Baun’s rich show at Mt.
Higgins, has gone to pieces, and the plant is offered for
sale. Three years ago the property was regarded as
first-rate, but the lode, which near the surface was very
Hat, has apparently straightened up at depth.
* » » *
Queensland Menzies (W.A.) is being gradually
appreciated by investors. Co. is now paying monthly
divs. of 6d., and, although this distribution cannot be
absolutely assured, the mine is in such a good position
that a long term of profitable working is in sight.
Manager Ferguson has had a stiff row to hoe, but appears
to be on the road to success.
* * *
Before returns from South Kalgurli (W.A. can
be resumed the plant will have to be remodelled and a
lot of it will have to go to the scrap heap
* * *
Queen Margaret (W.A.) has cleaned up 250 tons
for 3940 z. from its Vosperton property. This ore is taken
from the end of the drive further out than the limit of
the upper workings, and consequently gives a clear 400 ft.
of hacks to the surface. Property looks very promising.
Mill, so far, has only run 12 hours daily owing to scarcity
of water.

maintenance : Oroya-Brown Hill, 68s. Bd. ; Associated,

From the Burra, the one-time S. Aus. copper
town, which for 25 years past has always been just on
the eve of resurrection, but is still dead:-“No sane
resident now expects the Burra mine to be working again
this century. True, tenders are to be called to clear the
drain leading from the shaft to Burra Creek, but it is not
likely' to do much. A more than ordinarily tired boy with a
wheelbarrow could clear said drain within a week. This
move was made by the directors to ease the minds of
local shareholders who hinted that their money was all
going in directors’fees. The Magnetic Extraction plant,
erected several months ago to treat dumps, has not got to
work yet. The made-in-Germany process would not
treat ore, and roasters are now being erected to try and
remedy matters.” What seems to be most the matter
with the Burra is that the largest shareholders, who are
mostly fat as the result of the old richness of the mine,
are also rrow very' ancient, and won’t risk any' money
prospecting for the lost ore-body ; and, as the State was
fool enough to sell the freehold of the mine, there isn’t
any law to compel the ancients to let anybody else have
a try.

R. Cummings, a well-known Ballarat manager,
who has been to Arltunga : “If I had to take my chance

I wouldn’t take a few lashes into the bargain.”

Mount Garnet has come to an inglorious end.
A reconstruction scheme is proposed, but shareholders
will be wise to decline to have anything to do with it. It
will mean throwing good money away after bad. Mine as
far as developed is worked out, and there is no prospect
of any discovery which will be likely to redeem it, starting
as it will, under the suggested scheme, with a heavy
incubus of debt. As a zinc mine something might be
done with it on starvation wages.
Waihi (Maoriland) put up a splendid record for
1902. Ore treated, 179,485 tons, producing £521,574
worth of bullion, a trifle under £3 per ton. Profit was
£273,737, out of which four divs. of 4s. 6d. were paid.
There are 500,000 shares in the co., and their market
value is about £5 7s. 6d. each, equivalent to about a 12
years’ purchase at the present rate of div., which is high
enough. Considering that a massive formation is being
worked, .that the ore is amenable to simple treatment,
and that there is the great advantage of water-power,
working .expenses seem excessive, but as long as share-
holders draw 50 per cent, on the capital so long will they
be satisfied and refuse to worry about economy.
* * » *
The criminal waste of land by Maoriland dredg-
ing co’s. is becoming a burning question in the southern
portion of the State. Thousands upon thousands of
acres are being turned over and rendered utterly useless
for future occupation ; for even a dredge that doesn’t pay
expenses can do wonders at converting good land into a
howling waste of gravel and boulders. The Legislature
is to be asked to do the usual “ something ” in the in-
terests of posterity. What that something is the Lord
only knows.
* * *
A rise of over 100 per cent, in the value of
mother-of-pearl shell in London since the beginning of
the year, due to a demand for ladies’ pearl buttons for
blouses in the United States, is good news for the pearling
fleets in Q. and W.A. waters.
