“ They were as fed horses in the morning. Everyone
neighed after his neighbor’s wife.” —Jeremiah 5, 8.
Jeremiah was thus the founder of the whole French
drama, and so much of the English as comes from
that well undefiled.
Fred Leslie recently played lead in “The Runaway
Girl" at Adelaide Royal. Two years ago Leslie was a
somewhat obscure corner-man in a variety show'.
“Jaybell”: Pagel’s lion-tossing performance didn’t
come to a head on that remarkable horse-hoister’s first
night in Brisbane. The king of beasts stood on whatever
small scrap of dignity the mighty Herr hasn’t pounded
out of him, and defied the programme. An excited
flunkey with a crowbar only tended to heighten his
resolve to spend a peaceful evening with his own reflec-
tions. But on subsequent nights the strengthy one
waved his acknowledgments to the audience with a
struggling mass of resenting beast of prey in his grip.
All the same, I believe the lion will crawl back to his
cage one of these nights with bits of Herr in his back
teeth, and a coroner’s verdict trailing behind him.
Two men were passing the Bioscope parlor, in which
one may see for Id. a living picture in which a beauteous
damsel undresses with irritating slowness, and the dark-
ness comes down before she has taken off any clothes
worth mentioning after all. “ Here, old man,” cried the
first, “come and have a look at this one. It’s real fine.”
The second glanced at the eyehole. “No thanks,” he
said. “It cost me 10s. the last time I looked at that
one. I looked 120 times to see if she’d take off any
more."
"Melb." : Dorothy Marsden, the Puck of “Midsummer
Night’s Dream,” is quite a unique little personage. You
see directly she bounds on the stage that she is not a
girl, but a woman. Truly the most perfect embodiment
of a grown fairy that ever touched our shores. She would
be the Oberon or Titania if such little mantelpiece people
could be got to match. She is a good deal smaller than
Katie Barry, of the Gaiety. How beautiful she is, and
what an infinitude of wit is hound up in that tiny com-
pass 1
The late Max O’Rell, when in Australia, was consider-
ably perturbed by R. S. Smythe’s advertising tactics.
He used to expostulate politely with the Much Travelled
for laying on the adjectives a little too thickly, and every
time he faced a new audience he had a dread of being-
rejected as a fraud. It is an old story of Max that one
night on arriving at a Victorian country town he hurried
towards the local hall, where the whole population
appeared to be flocking. The lecturer’s heart was glad,
until he noticed that a big calico over the doorway bore
the words, “The Lord Only.” This was too much for
O’Rell’g modesty. Could friend Smythe have dared to
lay it on so thick? Then he noticed a smaller announce-
ment on the other side of the calico frame —Admission
Free. “ Ah, no,” said O’Rell, “ that is not like Smythe.
I must have come to the wrong hall.” He had. Smythe’s
pitch for the Prince of Lecturers and Emperor of Humor-
ists was farther up the street.
Though she is still young for a front-rank singer
Frances Saville seems to have already determined on
quitting the opera stage. A couple of months ago she
retired from the Imperial Opera at Vienna, and was
afterwards farewelled at a big concert. Amy Sherwin
has also given up all save casual appearances, and has
settled down to teaching in London. She has already
made a big success in that branch of her work, having
placed John Harrison, hailed as a great tenor, before
London audiences
“ Fiddlededee ” : It is pardonable in the friends of
Ernest Toy to allege that he didn’t get a fair show in
Melbourne. The admirers of an unsuccessful artist
always blame the newspapers, or the management, or
the public. In this case, they seem to have ignored the
fact that Mr. Toy made a Melb. appearance before a big
musical crowd, at an Orpheus Society concert, two days
previous to his first recital. He had the chance to set
the Yarra on fire, and the conflagration didn’t happen.
There is nothing in Ernest Toy’s art of fiddling. No
richness of tone, nothing that appeals to the 99 per cent,
of the public who are uninterested in the technicalities
of his craft. To speak quite plainly, it was not to be ex-
pected that so ordinary an artist could excite any
enthusiasm from a public who have Albert Zelman
always with them. Toy has been to Europe. Young
Zelman has never, or hardly ever, been out of Melbourne.
Just now, he is conducting the orchestra at the Prin-
cess’s and otherwise earning his bread by taking pupils,
mostly older than himself one supposes. But, the other
Saturday afternoon, he popped up at the Town Hall to
play the solo in Bruch’s concerto for violin, with Marshall
Hall’s orchestra at his back. He had the misfortune to
break a string, or two strings, before completing the
second number. Nevertheless, he knocked the audience.
Zelman, jun., is to-day the brightest man violinist Mel
bourne has heard since Musin was around. Almost
genius, and quite unpretentious.
The revival of wrestling among our colored brethren in
Melbourne caters for a new element—the female —which
was not counted on during the great wrestling boom of
15 years back. Through all the big matches held during
that period, only one woman was present, the late
Madame Lucy Chambers, who occupied a front seat in the
circle at the contest between Miller and Clarence
Whistler. Her presence was widely commented on at
the time, but 700 women attended Wirth’s circus to see
Buttan Singh wrestle Gunga Brahn, and scarcely a men-
tion was made of the fact, though one of the ’rastlers was
perilously near getting his very abbreviated costume torn
from him on several occasions, and the other also came
dangerously near to being a naked “ brother.” The frank
audacity of Sandow in coming before a mixed audience
wearing only a few inches of leopard skin, and the fre-
quent revelations of swimming damsels, has done much
to break down the prudishness of the middle Victorian
era—a period which will be long remembered in the
history of art and beauty as something to be heartily
ashamed of.
T.D. : Re the Tragedy Queen who had invested her
savings in a stewed eels shop, mentioned by actor George
Giddens to an interviewer. The other night I met
Ophelia serving out crude, long beers in an obscure pub.
somewhere in the dark wilderness of a Melbourne lane.
It was late, and she had developed her most histrionic
mood ; drew beer as though she were waving a blood-
grimed poniard, handed me back the change in a stage
whisper, and made casual remarks in blank verse. Also
she threw in an odd quotation or two from Shakspeare.
Quoth I, by way of a test, “ Is this a prologue, or the
posy of a ring?” “ ’Tis brief, my lord,” replied the bar-
lady. Then I became Romeo, as it was getting very late,
and announced “ I must be gone and live, or stay and
die,” and Juliet capped it with —
“ Yon light is not the daylight, know ,I;
It is some meteor that the sun exhales.”
So I remained a little while longer, to discover that e
majestic drawer of beer was once on the boards, in the
roaring old Brooke days, when every actor and actress
slept on the works of the immortal William, knew a dozen
of his plays line by line, and was ready to play lago or
Rosalind at an hour’s notice. Yet she never got beyond
the stage of “ attendant ” or “ court lady.” ' How many
present-day supers could quote three lines of Shakspeare,
and where is the mummer in Australia who could rattle
off a whole act of “ Macbeth” or “ Othello ”?
Prof. Miller’s farewell to Melbourne was a matter
of theatrical interest for old-timers. Miller has played
his little parts in Shakspearian drama, and could
walk the boards much better than the giant Toothick
who gets vanquished by George Lauri. When he visited
America an enterprising manager staged “As You Like
It,” in order to star the Australian hero as Charles the
Wrestler. Remembering this, George Miln, when he
produced “ Antony and Cleopatra ” at Melb. Opera
House, in 1889, interpolated a silent character—a
prisoner—into the scene of Antony’s final “gaudy night.”
Miller being brought before Antony Miln, was notified, in
dumb show, that he could win his liberty by downing
the Queen’s boss gladiator—Donald Dinnie. Then the
chains were taken from Miller’s wrists, and a bout of
Graeco-Roman wrestling relieved, as it were, the intellec-
tual strain of the classic drama. Some few years previ-
ously Miss Florence Wade engaged Miller to play Charles
the Wrestler at Melbourne Bijou. Also Mons. Victor
was engaged to do a turn with the professor before he
went through the performance of getting, thrown by
young Orlando. Miss Wade’s season proved a terrible
frost, and the two athletes got instructions to put in
as much work as they liked for their money.
Miller had instructions not to hurl Victor to the ground
inside of five minutes. After that he was to go for all he
was worth, and win his first fall and look horribly dan-
gerous when Orlando faced him. Victor’s game was to
get Miller a bit winded in those first five minutes, and
then give him as slippery a time as possible. Some nights
the little ’un made Miller toil for nearly half-an-hour.
The present scribe has in his mind’s eye a picture of Duke
Frederick, Rosalind, Celia, and Touchstone the clown,
grinning with delight at Miller’s discomfiture. Poor Phil
Day, the Touchstone of the occasion, had his watch half-
hidden in the palm of his hand and was furtively timing
the struggle. Victor’s trickiness combined with Miller’s
want of condition tended to prolong Shakspeare’s
“ delightful comedy ” to a late hour, but the public
didn’t mind.
Adelaide Critic has a weary, silly habit of misrepre-
senting paragraphs from The Bulletin theatrical columns
in order to gird at something this journal did not say.
The trick grows monotonous. Last week the Critic
said: “The Bulletin gets on to ‘My Lady Molly’
as ridiculous, because Miss Florence Young mas-
qnerading as a man would deceive nobody. Did
The Bulletin ever see a girl masquerading as a man in
comic opera with the intention of deceiving anybody?"
The reply to that question is “Yes, all the time.”
Every girl who masquerades as a man is supposed
for purposes of dramatic fiction to be deceiving some-
body or everybody in the story. And The Bulletin
notice of “My Lady Molly” complained that the
pretence of deception in this case was obviously absurd,
not because Miss Young masqueraded as a man, but
because she was represented as deceiving the man
(Harold Thorley) whose clothes she was supposed to be
masquerading in. “He doesn’t note the glaring
fact that the clothes he left in his portmanteau couldn’t
possibly fit a figure 6in. shorter than his own.” Can
the Critic mention any other comic opera or comedy
wherein the heroine is supposed to deceive her lover, half
a foot taller than herself, by presenting a tightly fitted
figure in the tall man’s togs ? Surely a man is supposed
to know, within half a foot or so, the height of his own
clothes ?
“Where’s ‘Hop’?”
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
[For the first time in many years an issue
of The Bulletin —that of May 23rd, 1903—has
appeared with no work by Livingstone Hopkins
between the red covers.]
The blessed world is upside down,
The times are out of joint;
We cluster, and with earnest frown,
Discuss the knotty point.
And all for “ Whys ” and “ Wherefores " seek,
And various notions swop—
The Bulletin came out this week
Without a sign of “ Hop ” !
We know his humor—keen and gay—
That mocks the moment’s whim ;
We’ve seen our Bulletin to-day
And found no trace of him.
And so around the big camp-fire—
As in the city shop—
We lift our voices to inquire :
“Hi! What’s become of ‘ Hop
The lads recall the various jokes
By which he’s “ rubbed it in
His very monniker provokes
The reminiscent grin.
And now despair is all our share,
And woe has come to stop ;
O ! Fate in editorial chair,
What has become of “ Hop
Ah, newer men rise, one by one
And we admire, of course,
The opulence of Vincent’s fun,
And Lindsay’s graphic force.
But still we oldsters can’t forget
The years he’s been on top ;
And whilst we praise the others, yet
We love and swear by “ Hop ” 1
N.S.W
CECIL POOLE.
“Hop is, unfortunately, ill; his early complete
recovery is hoped for.—Ed. B.]

Hector M‘Donald’s funeral:
The War Office did not know officially that the late
general was married, and, consequently, it is understood,
messages after Sir Hector’s death were sent to his brother

existence of its star-performer’s honest wife.
Premier See says the Government intends
to extend the area of the Western Division—that
already vast portion of the State which has been
given over to three Commissioners with instruc-
tions to do the best with it they can, ordinary
administration having proved a dead failure. The
extension of the area is an admission that even
more of the country is hopeless, so far as ordinary
settlement goes, than had been supposed.
B. R. Wise, on behalf of the N.S.W.
Govt., replied to the dismal complaints and
plausible mis-statements of the Freetrade papers,
when given a taste of their own Freetrade.
Evening News printed his letter as it was sent.
The D.T. chopped it to pieces and sandwiched
it with columns of comment, whereupon Mr. Wise
protested to that paper :
Since you publish in this morning’s issue of your paper
my statement with regard to the free carriage of news-
papers in your own way, I should be glad if you could
now see your way to give it in mine. Ido not object to
any comments, provided my statement is set out con-
secutively and in a form comprehensible to your readers.
I cannot think that the way in which you have published
the statement complies with either of these requirements.
But the D.T. didn’t take any notice. What
better argument could that newspaper put into
the mouths of its opponents than this partial sup-
pression of a Government reply to its criticisms ?
The law is behaving strangely again in
Victoria. Six years ago a Beechworth farmer
took his child in his arms and jumped down a
well. They fished him out alive, but not the
child, and he was tried for murder. Insanity was
pleaded, and he was acquitted, the usual order
being made that he should be confined during the
Governor’s pleasure. The man’s wife has been
living on the farm since then, supporting the
family (six children). Lately the farmer, who
is still in gaol, decided that he would sell
the farm, and instructed a lawyer to move
for a warrant to eject his wife; and the
court found that it must grant the application.
So the position is this: If the man is still
insane the court has acted on a lunatic's applica-
tion to turn his wife and family out of their
home; and if he is sane, having (as is alleged)
recovered, then by being still confined he is being
punished for doing something when he was insane
and unaccountable for his actions. Either way
the law has a good deal to answer for.

“Kati-Kati”j Do you know anything
about happiness ! It seems to me that there is
only a fixed amount of happiness in this world,
and we can neither increase nor decrease it—
merely distribute it. When the locusts are thick
on the corn the farmer’s happiness is lessened to
the extent of the sum total of happiness among
the locusts. There is the same amount of happi-
ness in a suit of clothes covering either a mere
man or a man tormented with fleas, but in the
latter case the fleas have some of it. In disease,
the microbes are having a devilish good time. To
prove this theory by experiment, kill the cat that
merrily singeth on your roof at midnight, and
you will feel the happiness that had been its
soaking into you at once.
The other day, a casual young Maori-
landress, of Napier, submitted, with great
emphasis, a vote of want of confidence in the
married ladies who go into partial retreat for two
or three months and into complete retreat for a
month or two more. She was in the middle of a
Monday morning’s wash when she remarked that
her toothache was pretty bad, and she thought the
things wouldn’t hurt for another half-hour’s soak
or so. Then she “ retreated, ” but appeared later
on and resumed operations at the tub. And
nothing would have been known for certain of
what had happened in that half-hour or so if, a
few days after, two small infants hadn’t been
fished out of a local waterhole. Two of them !

DARKanoLIGNTPLUG
The Development of Agnes
O’Rorke.
POLITICAL POINTS
Sitting at the cashier’s desk of one of the busiest
bars in town, raking the stuff in with both hands,
and spending her soul (what’s left of it) 16 hours
a day. Gold-topped, rouge-tinted, velveted,
chained and bejewelled ; just a sneer on her face
as a curse or a groan comes down the back stair-
way. That is Agnes to-day. Is it the same
woman we knew, hatchet-faced, hawk-eyed, slat-
ternly, running a boarding-house on the railway
works ? Of whom an old navvy would say to a
traveller, “Ask her for a feed 1 My poor man,
ye had better ask the rock in the face beyant 1 ”
The very The same, too, who came into
Waipapa 10 years earlier as Mrs. Middleton. Her
husband signed himself Arthur Middleton, Capt.
He had manners, seemed to have money ; but no
memory whatever of recent experiences in the
service. There was an odor of Sunday-school and
bread-and-butter about her then, and the towns-
folk took to her kindly ; but Arthur Middleton,
Capt., was not well-disposed towards social inter-
course. He bought a farm a few miles back in
the foot of the hills, laid in a stock of liquor, and
began deliberately to drink to a finish. It took
about four months. At the end of that time the
buggy came rattling into town, and Agnes
Middleton got down at the hotel, all dishevelment
and tears ; while George, the man, went round to
the police, the doctor, and the undertaker. Then
there was an outflow, or rather an outgush, of
sympathy for the poor widow. Every house in
the town was opened to her, and in a little while
she accepted the invitation of the doctor’s wife •
not having, so far, paid the doctor’s bill. The
doctor couldn’t dun his own guest, but for all
that he shunted her on to the parson within a
month, and a week later sent a collector round.
Parson felt something queer in a week or two, and
offered Nathan, the jeweller, a chance. Nathan got
to the bottom of it in 48 hours, and the widow went
to the hotel, where the landlord thought it better
that her meals should be served in private. Then
the sergeant of police looked in, and suggested
the advisability of taking up quarters in a larger
town—Christchurch, say, or Wellington. But
she looked him in the face with steely eyes (all
the bread-and-butter and Sunday-school absorbed
in patchouli now), and insisted on her ability and
intent to manage her own affairs. She proposed
to settle in that little town of Waipapa. She
might open a school or a fancy-needlework estab-
lishment. The sergeant talked about creditors.
She replied that Mr. Finn, her lawyer, would see
to that ; and, though they came up in battalions,
Finn did. “Three thousand pounds worth of
diamonds upon her,” was whispered through the
audience, but nothing on which a sheriff’s officer
could levy.
Old Nathan smiled when he heard it; smiled
and was silent. It was not Nathan’s habit to
eulighten fools.
The Widow Middleton had an establishment
then, and Finn, the lawyer, and Trott, the racing
man, and Petrie, at the store—who had seen life
in Melbourne—used to come round in the evening
bringing various contributions of grog for supper.
No harm to them, but one night they must bring
little Paddy Welsh along. Paddy was two-and-
twenty ; as happy and combustible an Irish boy
as ever wore a shamrock. The pink-and-white,
set in the black-and-white, did for Paddy in an
hour. Next day he was down on his knees, and
she was saying, “I do love you very much, but
without money it is quite impossible.”
“ I could raise a hundred,” said Paddy, “ per-
haps two, and once my people saw you they’d
divide the last pound with us.”
“ If you could be sure of a thousand.”
“ How would I be getting a thousand ? ”
“ I don’t know, and I don’t care. Get it and
come to me and—She stooped down and kissed
his feverish lips.
She bought him with that kiss—body and soul;
and it cost some of us wild and desperate work to
bu}’ him back.
There were six-and-twenty draught colts run-
ning in a 10,000-acre paddock, 12 miles out of
Waipapa. If the boundary-rider saw them once
a week he reckoned it good enough. They were
worth in those days from £4O to £6O each. Paddy
had talked with the boundary-rider the day
before. At one o’clock next morning he went by
a back track out of town, riding one good horse
and leading another. The day passed as usual,
but at eight o’clock next morning a sharp tap
came on the widow’s door, and when Finn opened
it the sergeant pulled him outside. He was a
good while away, and when the lot went out to
see what the trouble might be, we heard the
sergeant say, “ If I could give her seven years as
well, I’m damned if I’d save him.”
Finn said, “Come along to the pub,” and we
went without saying good-bye to the widow.
Once inside, it was blurted right out. “That
young fool, Paddy, has shook Masop’s colts and
is driving them towards Porter’s Pass.”
“ How do you know that? ” asked Trott.
“ Bindon,” answered Finn. (Bindon was the
sergeant.)
Then Con, Paddy’s brother, broke out. “Finn!
Finn ! Ye’re a murtherin’ villain to beguile the
poor boy into the arms of that sorceress.” But
Trott, the racing man, cut him short. “There’s
nobody going to be hanged, nor yet lagged, if
everyone here keeps his lips closed, and two up-
river men will ride out with me in half-an-hour.”
Trott had two hurdle-racers in training, and
there were a dozen good hacks in the stables. We
rode three of the worst for 20 miles, and led the
jumpers and the best of the lot. Then unsaddled,
turned the spare beasts into a gully, as arranged,
and mounted those that would have to do the
work.
Paddy had 48 hours’ start. He might knock
near 80 miles out of the colts in that time, but
they would get stiffer and sorer with every mile,
and we reckoned on covering 100 miles in 12 hours,
if necessary.
The moon was full and clear, and the track
hard and smooth. A stumble against some jut-
ting piece of shale was the only serious risk. Hour
after hour we rode, Trott generally ten lengths
ahead. Nearer and nearer, and higher and higher,
rose the tremendous mountain-range, cleft by the
high pass. No man would believe that, when
daylight flushed the peaks, it was still 80 miles
distant. An hour later brought us to the big
river. Trott rolled off and unsaddled ; then from
a bit of valise produced—what? Breakfast? Not
much. A dozen good-sized carrots ! He gave
his horse twenty gulps of water, and bade us do
likewise ; sliced the carrots then, and cursed Tom
Wilkes for a life-robber when he slipped a bit
into’ his own mouth. Trott knew horses as a
mother of ten knows babies.
We got Paddy’s tracks both sides of the river,
and we sighted him an hour before noon. He
hadn’t done 70 miles from the paddock, and was
as dead-beat himself as the fattest of the colts.
“ Where d’ye get ’em, Paddy ? ” asked Trott.
“ Cunning beggar, you, to head us off that way.
The reward ’ll be a hundred, sure.”
Then Paddy’s head went down on the horse’s
mane, and he cried, like the silly boy he was.
We were 30 miles towards home, when, out of
the dust-cloud, emerged Mason, with overseer and
boundary-riders, and Bindon with two troopers—
carbines slung and steel scabbards flashing.
_ Mason rubbed his chin, while Trott explained
till the stubble crackled with electricity; but it
was hard to quarrel with Trott, and the rest of us
were very faithful echoes.
So little Paddy was saved from gaol and worse,
and the widow’s evenings were left desolate.
Bindon wanted her out of the town ; and so did
everybody else, and it was reckoned that there
was about evidence enough to go upon, when a
new development occurred. O’Rorke came to
town with ten ounces of gold cable across his
stomach and sovereigns jingling in both breeches-
pockets. He had finished a big road-contract and
was worth a few thousand. So at least other folks
said, and he did not contradict. O’Rorke ran up
against the widow, and she went through him.
He had a glass of wine next night and saw the
diamonds. Three days later a wagonette with
O’Rorke and the widow, rid of her weeds, on the
box-seat, drove up to the church, and the parson
did his business and pocketed the fee.
Next day O’Rorke faced his bride and said,
“Agnes, I’ve desaved ye.”
The steel hardened in the eyes of Agnes. “It
is just as well to be straightforward,” she said.
“ Barring a hundred sovereigns in my belt and
the watch and chain, here —worth maybe a hun-
dred more—l’m a poor man, Agnes.”
“ It is just as well to be straightforward,” said
Agnes without much emotion.
“It is money we must have, Agnes; and there
is only one way of getting it.”
She unlocked her box and took out a green
velvet case. Within it lay a necklace of “ dia-
monds”; wonders of stones, all. “Nathan will
let us have a thousand on that.”
“ He should.”
“ Take it to him, then; but, wait a moment.
Just let me have that gold and your watch and
chain. If you get the thousand, how do I know
that I shall ever see you again ? ”
He unloaded without a word, and went away to
Nathan’s with the diamonds next his heart.
Ten minutes later Agnes’ boxes were on an ex-
press waggon and she was driving like mad for
the beach, where the last surf-boat was putting
off to a northern-bound steamer.
O’Rorke had to wait an hour for Nathan, and
then, said the latter, “Pat, you are cooked like
one dinner. These are very good paste, Pat.
Good stage props, and I will buy them right out
for 355.”
There was wild work in the town of Waipapa
that night. The “force” got O’Rorke inside
with difficulty and bloodshed. It was a fiver or a
month in the morning, and, as the landlord’s
bailiffs were in the house, the alternative had to
be taken. When the month was ended ca. sa. and
ca. re. appeared, and O’Rorke went to the “ other
side ” for his wife’s debts.
But Mrs. O’Rorke had begun to find the world
small, or otherwise, and news of all sorts most in-
conveniently winged. Nobody seemed to know
her, nobody would know her in Christchurch, ex-
cept the police and those of her own sex to whose
level she had not vet sunk. She was the daughter
of a small country shopkeeper, and the huckster’s
instincts were dominant in her in all crises. The
high-flying game was done; why not make some
use of O’Rorke ? The beast could at least work,
and he would be humble now and thankful for re-
lease. A sum of £4O squared the creditors, who
were already sick of maintenance fees, and one
sunny spring day the blusterer, subdued and
emaciated, stepped down the street, his soberly-
clad missus tripping beside him.
She fed and clothed him, nursed him up, and
brought back his manhood and strength; then
broached her plans. He must get a billet as
ganger on a railway. She would start a shop on
the works—no matter what sort. Grog would be
sold. Navvies earned plenty of money, and why
should not some deserving people get some of it ?
O’Rorke jibbed at first; he had always been a
man amongst men. But at length he yielded,
and had better have stayed in gaol. For Agnes
worked out her plans remorselessly as success-
fully, cajoling all who promised plunder as the
boa-constrictor slimes over its prey before swallow-
ing. The hatred of the men came to him, and he
sought to retaliate by driving them harder and
harder in the interests of the boss. But the boss
had only a contemptuous nod for him as he rode
by, and brother gangers, even on Sunday, slewed
round their backs if he entered the gangers’ parlor
at the pub.
He jumped one night when Agnes said, “ I
don’t think I’ll follow the works any farther.
There’s more fools and more money, too, in town.”
“And what shall I be doing ?”
“Keeping them up in their traces, of course, as
usual; and drawing your own pay, and sending
me —well, say, half.”
He had no heart to argue or rebel, and was
only silently dumbfounded when next day the
boss said, “We won’t want you any more,
O’Rorke.’’
She had to take him along, but he went to sleep
in the threepenny bar and got transferred to the
cockloft, with the offside man to carry up drinks ;
the doctor looking in just for decency’s sake, and
the undertaker waiting. But the agents speak of
Alice O’Rorke as a “warm woman,” and share-
brokers “set ’em up” quick and lively in her
parlors, and the bank-manager nods affably if he
chances to cross the banking-hall as she brings in
her bag. “ Shouldn’t be surprised to see her die a
woman of six figures,” says the brewer’s traveller,
“ if —she keeps off it.”
FRANK MYERS.
Dunedin is more crowded than ever with
horribly good people since the Torrey-Alexander
mission came to the damp city, and the place is
full of hymns. Recently a party of cheerful
Christian Endeavorers ascended Mount Flag-
staff, and fell by the way on a hut containing a
distressed family—husband out of work, and wife
ill in bed with a week-old infant on hand, and
mighty few assets of any kind. The Endeavorers
didn’t pass the hat round or do a few “chores” ;
they sang “Count your blessings ; name them one
by one.” Another evening, after a hot and
sensual meeting at the Garrison Hall, a swarm of
Christian Endeavorers rushed the horse tram to
go home, and loaded it to its last available inch of
standing room. The two tortured mokes struggled
desperately to get their huge burden up the awful
Princes-street hill, and the Endeavorers didn’t
get out and push; they sang “You can help a
load to lighten as you go.” The singing was,
fortunately, interrupted by a heathen who used
such language to the crowd that at last it did get
off and walk up the hill. But it lightened the
load under very much protest.

The Sorceress.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
My lover travels through the world
And seven years more shall roam
Before, soft shining through his dreams,
My beauty draws him home.
Salt of the wave, brown of the sun,
Deep scarred of dragon claws,
Leaped from the cruel wild boar’s tusks,
Safe from the kraken’s jaws,
Loved by the fairest of the earth,
Yet one he seeks piore fair ;
And all his loves are worthy him—
Red, gold, and raven hair.
In magic crystal still they pass,
They charm him with their spells ;
A little while they keep my love
From the place where his true love dwells.
* • * •
The poppies wave in the sunlight white,
And the wine-red blossoms glow:
The Persian woman would keep him there,
Down where the roses blow.
Let her keep him and teach him the secret
The poppy holds at the core—
Free from the spell of her weaving,
He is out on the quest once more.
Down in the golden sea caves
A mermaid has lured him to stay ; ,
Her eyes, as clear as the wave heart,
Were melting his soul away.
A dryad gleams through the tree trunks,
He follows close through the wood —
She would keep him prisoned for ever,
Yet I know that my charm holds good.
• * • •
From golden hair and hair of black
My love to me shall yet coine back.
From nut-brown hair and hair of red
Surely to me my love is led.
From hazel eyes and eyes of blue
He comes, my arrow winging true.
From eyes of black and eyes of green
He seeks the eyes his dreams have seen.
From sword and spear and stealthy knife
My charms shall win his waning life.
From tiger’s claw-and scorpion’s sting
Still hold him safe, my'magie ring !
From sprite, nymph, wizard, witch, and gnome
Quick, charms, and draw my true love home !
WILD BEE.
An Up-to-date Scandal.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Allard came in out of the sun, which had filled
the garden with the odor of flowers.
In the drawing-room he found Lottie by herself,
her usually pale face flushed, and her cheeks
wet with tears.
“ O,” she said, “I am so glad you have come.
He has been ill-treating me again.”
And she tore the little glove she had in her
hand, from between the fingers to the buttons.
Allard breathed heavily.
“ Ah, the brute ! ” she continued, “just because
I was late for breakfast.”
“ Well—well ?” demanded Allard.
‘ ‘ He struck me ! ”
“ Struck you ? ”
“Yes, struck me with the newspaper—and in
front of the servants.”
Allard doubled his fists.
“ I wish,” he said, “I were in a better position
—morally—so that I could make him regret it all.’’
Lottie caught him by the coat.
“Come. You’re a great big lump of a man,”
she said ; “ why don’t you punch him ? ”
“O—oh, it is< impossible,” exclaimed Allard,
shutting his eyes and waving his head as though
to avoid something in the air.
“ Ah, that’s what you always say. I begin to
think you cannot love me sufficiently.”
And Lottie showed her short white teeth for a
minute.
“ Well,” said Allard, “ I shall do it.”
“Yes. I’m glad to hear you talk that way. It
makes me happy to feel that I have so much
strength and determination on my side.”
“ And,” continued Allard, “when shall it be?
To-morrow when ”
“No, no,” burst in the woman. “Let us
do away with to-morrow altogether. To-night!
To-night! O, yes,” she went on, “we will brazen
it out. We will tell him, here, in this room,
together, just as we are standing now. He will
learn how we love each other, and how nothing
on earth can part us. And then you will thrash
him and we will go out into the night, away under
the stars, and through the sobbing pines, into the
bright world of eternal love.”
Allard trembled before this terrible display of
passion, and proportionately weakened as she
gained strength.
The noise of a watch-case snapping made them
both look round.
They saw the man of whom they had been
speaking. He was fat and healthy-looking, and
very neatly dressed.
“ I must have come home a little early,” he re-
marked ; and without so much as a glance at
Allard, whom he hardly knew, he sat down in a
comfortable arm-chair and selected a book.
The woman put her hand on his shoulder, and,
when he had stuck a marker between the pages,
he troubled himself to look up.
“Arthur,” she said, “I have come to a con-
clusion.”
“ Have you ? ” he answered.
“Yes, I have. We were not meant for one
another —however we may have thought so at one
time. And, also, lam not the woman to stand a
blow from any man.”
“ Pshaw ! A playful pat with the paper.’’
Allard looked uneasy. He did not feel quite up
to breaking a man’s head in for just tapping any-
one on the face. The misdeed hardly merited
such a punishment, especially if it devolved on
him to carry it out.
“ Well, and is there anything else ? ” asked the
fat man, readjusting his spectacles and opening
the book.
“Of course there is,” cried Lottie; “you know
yourself what must follow.”
, The man poked his head forward in an insolent,
listening attitude.
“ I am going,” she said ; “I am going.”
“Then go, for goodness sake, but don’t make
such a fuss about it,” he replied, with vehemence.
“I am going away,” she continued, “for ever
and ever. You will never see me again. Allard,
here, will protect me and take me to the world’s
end.”
“’Pon my word,” muttered the fat man,
getting out of his ohair, “this is becoming serious.
Really, I am deeply grieved. It is all so hideously
unusual. You actually propose to leave the com-
fortable home of your lover, where you have
everything you want, and, in exchange, you seek
—the miserable quarters of your husband ! Go—
go, by all means, never stopping for a moment to
think how you wrong me, how you drag my—my
—my —my reputation for successful immorality
through the mire. But I will not curse you, nor
will I curse your miserable partner by law ”
But Allard and his wife had rushed from the
room, and, as they appeared at the window on
their way to the gate, the fat man threw up the
sash. “Lottie! Lottie!” he cried,.
Thinking she detected a note of remorse in his
voice, the woman turned.
The giant pine trees behind her tossed their
arms in the breeze, and the sound of a breaking
wave came from the beach.
“ What is it ? ” she asked with studied coldness.
“Poor little girl,” he replied, “your hat isn’t
on straight.”
Lottie started.
“ Why didn’t you tell me ?” she said to Allard.
“I —I—er—didn’t notice it,” he stammered,
and stood petrified as his wife walked back to the
house.
She never returned.

HUGH R. M'CRAE.
Johnston’s Girl.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
“You should marry, ” Perry said, settling him-
self comfortably on his stool; “marry and be
happy like me.”
Perry was fat.
Johnston laughed.
“ Girl ? ” he asked.
“Plenty of ’em,” Perry answered. “ You’re a
well set-up youngster. They’d rush you.”
“ What about me ? ”
“You’d be happy—like me.”
Perry weighed more pounds than he possessed.
“ I’ll think about it,” Johnston said. He went
on adding up figures. It struck him that Perry
resembled a nought. Alone, he meant nothing.
His value depended on the figure before him, and
he looked happy beside any figure. Matri-
monially, Perry was a cipher.
Perry went out and Johnston pulled a soiled
envelope from his pocket, and laid its contents in
an open drawer so that he could read them. The
handwriting was a girl’s:
Jim, you’ve got a mighty good opinion of me—better
than I deserve. I wish you would modify it, or some
day you will have no opinion at all, and I should not like
that. Sometimes I do very mad things, not because I
know no better. You may not have heard of any of
these, but some day you may, and then —well, perhaps
you’ll be rather disappointed in me. I wish you wouldn’t
worry about me ; you need your brains for your work.
You’re going to be a good man, and I’m not going to be
a drag on you. I’m not good. I can’t help it. But no
one shall say I was not straight with you. And oh !
Jimmy, I wish I could tell everyone what a dear old
fellow you are. The other day, someone asked me who
that long ass was—meaning you. I swore at him. I
couldn’t help it, Jim. Perhaps I’ll reform some day—l’m
only 19. And then I’ll say to you—you are so slow at
speaking, so I must speak for you—“ Will you marry me
now, Jim ? ” And this, of course, I must answer ; and
I’ll say, “Yes.” That is—some day. You have to work,
and so have I. I want to learn how to chase a chop into
a,frying-pan, and what makes my cat wipe her paw over
her ear when it’s going to rain. Also, how to behave my-
self. Fellows often say things to me they should not say.
Don’t get “ scotty ” with them—the faultis mine—l ought
not to give them the chance. But I can’t help laughing
when I see them look like whipped puppies after I’ve
spoken very straight to them. Dear old Jim, I love you
—with both hands, shall we say ? (That is what you are
always saying—“shall we say?”). You are so different
from other men. But you are good and lam bad. With a
great deal of love, I am your Molly.
As Johnston replaced the letter in the envelope
Perry returned to his stool. “Saw a ripping
girl just now,” he remarked. “Tall, graceful——”
“Slab,” Johnston interrupted.
“Make any man happy,” Perry continued,
breathing noisily and wiping his forehead. At
times he was superabundantly fat.
Johnston was silent.
“How do you like ’em ?” Perry persisted .
“ ’Bout a mile away.”
“ Yes, but what shape ? ”
“Nuggetty.”.
Perry was relieved. His wife was lean. Up to
now he had been jealous of Johnston since he had
smiled at Mrs. Perry—only once. Still it was a
smile.
But Johnston wasn’t thinking about Perry’s
wife—there wasn’t enough of her to occupy his
min'd. She occupied Perry’s completely. Perry
was narrow-minded—and newly-married. John-
ston was thinking of a girl who was considered
“fast” by the goody-goody people of .the town-
ship she lived in. She had been his comrade for
many sunny days during a couple of years. They
had sat and sprawled on -the cliffs above the
breakers that hurled their silver and grey plumes
skyward in fruitless endeavor. And she would
suddenly become grave and tell him he must
strive hard, like the breakers, to be a good, great
man. She said his would not be a bootless battle
like the breakers. And he swore as he kissed her
that he would never succeed witliout her. But
she threw his arm aside and said, “ No ; not till 1
can go straight, which I can’t do now.’’
But he was satisfied.
She carried one of his bags to the station when
he left the last time ; and just as the engine
whistled he saw a man point her out to his
comrade and sneer ; and Johnston stepped off the
carriage-platform and knocked the speaker down.
And he remembered that when he got home there
was a letter for him. It ended—
“ Don’t get the blues, and keep going, Jim, I
might reform any day now. —Your Moll.”
“ T’ll wait, ’ Johnston muttered.
“ I beg pardon ? ” Perry said.
“It s going to be wet,” Johnston answered.

Pagel, the brawny horse and lion lifter, has had another
disagreement with his noble beast. He had gone through
the usual performance of baring the docile one’s fangs
and claws, and was about to lift up the beast for the
final throw off, when it eluded his grasp and sprang at
him. But for the presence of the huge mastiff which
accompanies him, and which savagely attacked the lion,
things might have gone hard with the giant ; as it was,
he was badly mauled and lost much valuable foreign
gore.
A Westralian showman, charged with being the keeper
of an unregistered dog, set up the defence that, as the
dog performed nightly, it was not a dog but a tool of
trade.
Wirth Bros, give this information : The writer of
“ Poverty Point ’* par. on Australian circuses (16/5/’O3) is
a little astray on the early experiences of Wirths’.
Wirth, senior, was with Jones in the business 39 years
ago. In the seventies the family was successively with
time-honored shows like Stanthorpe’s, St. Leon’s, and
John Ridge’s Japanese Typhoon circus. They left
Ashton in 1880, and started an acrobatic show at Paddy’s
Market, Sydney. Two years later Ashton, who had
a first-class lot of performers, met the Wirths in opposi-
tion at Inverell, and Ashton went down. Perhaps this is
what last week’s writer refers to in saying that Wirth’s
ran a small acrobatic side-show in opposition to the late
Jimmy Ashton’s well-known circus. The Wirths never
in their lives earned a very precarious living as a
travelling brass band, as the writer states. They took a
trip to Noumea in the eighties, and in 1893 thev shipped
off the whole show for a tour round the world, playing
in South America, Europe, South Africa, and India, as
an Australian combination. After gathering reputation,
dollars, and experience, the brothers came home three
years ago, leaving two of their number behind in their
graves, viz., John at Aliwal, South Africa ; and Harry at
Hongkong.
Yvette Guilbert, at time of departure of last Paris
mail, was suffering from severe kidney trouble —a re-
crudescence of an old complaint which before necessitated
the removal of one of the kidneys.
“ Melb.” : Professor Baldwin Spencer’s limelight and
cinematograph lecture on the aboriginals continues to
please. He was nervous at first about his corroboree
scenes, genuine moving picturts, and used to announce
them as “for men only,” but ladies somehow arrived all
the same. I heard him in Oollingwood, a night or two
ago. Maybe he only expected men, but young women
predominated. Quite harmless it proved, although all
the crowd of darkies appeared to have nothing on, and
some had nothing to a dead certainty. Then there were
crowds of naked women in the moving pictures. A few
—the weeping widows—had plastered themselves, or
chalked themselves, all over, so that they looked like
Venuses in motion.
“Top 0 ” : The recent visit of English tenor Edward
Lloyd reminds me that he is the single tenor artist of
real eminence that England has produced since Sims
Reeves. It is now 30 years since Lloyd made his first
public appearance and then he was doubly eclipsed, first
by the supreme popularity of Sims Reeves, and next by
the splendid voice of Joseph Maas. Upon the premature
death of the latter, and the artistic decline of the former,
Lloyd shot to the front and has never since had a real
rival. Ben. Davies is thought by some to have a voice of
power and sweeter quality, but he has neither the range,
nor the artistic ability of Edward Lloyd. Among French
tenors, the best to-day are Jean de Reske and Albert
Alvarez. Ernest Van Dyck, the Fleming, runs them
closely. The only great German tenors are Kraus and
the popular Dresden artist, George Anthes. Neither of
those are of the first quality, however ; while Germany is
wonderfully strong in heavy basses she seems unable to
produce a really excellent tenor. Italy is the breeding
place for tenors. Lloyd, I believe, is an uncle of the New-
castle (N.S.W.) soprano, Miss Violet Ludlow (sister of Dr.
Ludlow, of Waverley) who is now touring the English
provinces with a concert co. of her own and with very
considerable success too.
A man who has travelled with them both asserts that
lately-dead lecturer Clark was the cheeriest, and Frank
Lincoln the worst-tempered, of all the one-man shows that
have struck the boards of the Commonwealth. Snazelle
was also an aggravating and embittered cuss.
There were two quaint old English names in Edward
Lloyd’s little co.—Miss Foxcroft and Mr. Peterkin.
Which suggests that this country rears a few children of
unknown parentage who might as well be furnished with
characteristic Australian names, such as Ferntree, Tail-
tree, Wombat, Drycreek, Wallaby, Bushtrack, &c., &c.
Much better than Jones. The imagination of an Aus-
tralian foundling’s godfather rarely rises above Jones or
Macsomething.
William Mower Akhurst was the best writer of Mel-
bourne Xmas pantomimes, and got for them £lOO apiece.
He was Herald sub-editor, in the palmy days of Michie,
Evans, and Aspinall. None of his pantos hit the mark so
well as “The House that Jack Built, or Harlequin Jack
Melbourne and Little Victoria.”
There is a concert-hall in Victoria so big that it takes
up part of two towns. The performers sing in one town,
and a great part of the audience sits in the other. This
is the Alfred Hall, Ballarat, which is built over the
Yarrowee, the “ river” that separates Ballarat from Bal-
larat East. Many years ago, when the Duke of Edinburgh
visited the Golden City, it went off its alleged head with
grovel, and in honor of the visitor the city determined to
build a hall. The city, however, would not have it built
in the East, and Ballarat E. would be d if the West
should have the “ honor,” so to meet the difficulty the
structure (a huge, unlovely barn) was built with'its head
in the East and its tail in the West.
Buzzi, remembered as the basso of the Simonsen
Italian Opera Co., has become a monk. After enjoying
himself in Melbourne 12 years he returned to his beloved
Italy, and has since entered the Carthusian establish-
ment at Rome.
